Life is n>ad€ (ip of Trifles.
Trifles which make just the difference between comfort and discomfort. An iced drink, a cold bath, a well-cooked
dinner, a good novel, or a cigarette, which we cannot get just when we want them. All trifles in themselves, but all
capable of turning the temper sour and producing irritability—and a “ liver.” What slaves we are, after all, to
trifles : crying like children for the moon, and settling down to peaceful contentment because we have got just exactlv
what we wanted—no more, no less. It was only a cigarette—but it was a sine qud non that it should be a
CAMEO. Some people would scarcely enjoy life at all, if it were not for
CAMEO CIGARETTES.
THE RED PAGE.
THE FLIGHT OF THE GODDESS
A man should live In a garret aloof,
And have few friends, and go poorly clad,
With an old hat stopping the chink In the roof,
To keep the Goddess constant and glad.
Of old when I walked on a rugged way,
And gave much work for but little bread,
The Goddess, with me night and day,
Sat at my table, haunted my bed.
She narrow, mean attic, I see it now 1—
Its window o’erlooking the city’s tiles,
The sunset’s fires, and the cloud of snow,
And the river wandering miles and miles.
lust one pioture hung in the room,
The saddest story that Art can tell—
Dante and Virgil in lurid gloom
Watohing the Lovers float through Hell.
Wretched enough was I sometimes,
Pinched and harassed by vain desires ;
But thicker than clover sprung the rhymes
As I dwelt like a sparrow among the spires.
Midnight filled my slumbers with song;
Music haunted my dreams by day.
Mow I listen, and wait, and long,
But the Delphian airs have died away.
I wonder and wonder bow it befell:
Suddenly I had friends in crowds ;
I bade the housetops a long farewell;
“ Good-bye,” I cried, “ to the stars and clouds;
“ But thou rare soul, thou hast dwelt with me,
Spirit of Poesy 1 thou divine
Breath of the morning, thou shalt be,
Goddess 1 for ever and ever mine.”
And the woman I loved was now my brfd%
And the house I wanted was my own )
I turned to the Goddess satisfied—
But the Goddess had somehow flown I
Flown, and I fear she will never return;
I am muoh too sleek and happy for her,
Whose lovers must hunger, and waste, and burn,
Ere the beautiful heathen heart will stir 1
I oall—but she does not stoop to my cry ;
I wait—but she lingers, and ah 1 so long !
It was not so in the years gone by
When she touched my lips with ohrism of song.
I swear I will get me a garret again,
And adore, like a Parsee, the sunset’s fires,
And lure the Goddess, by vigil and pain,
Up with the sparrows among the spires,
For a man should live in a garret aloof,
And have few friends, and go poorly clad, s ’
With an old hat stopping the ohink in the roof.
To keep tbs Goddess constant and glad.
THOMAS BAILET ALDRICH.
A GOOD idea is introduced by William Areher
in translating Ibsen’s new play, ‘John
Gabriel Borkman.’ When an English writer
wishes to stress a word or a passage, he has prao-
tiealiy only one resource—italics. But italics
aoroam at you—thsy irritate the mind and dis-
figure the page. Often, too, their effect is out of
all proportion to the effect of delioate emphasis
desired in order that readers may at a glance
appreciate the full meaning of a sentence. So the
Germans (and Norwegians, too, it seems) fqr long
have marked a slight stress by putting a small
space between the letters of the emphatic word.
As thus—
Mow advertised that Wm. Gay is about to be married 1
Will these ad-misericordiain appeals never cease?
• • • •
Some one wrote the other day suggesting sar-
oasms about the attempts at grammar and puno-
tuation in the prospectus of The Australian Tech-
nical Journal of Science, Art, and Technology
(mercy on us !), but the game seemed hardly big
enough to waste cartridge on. Now the Journal
itself—the Technical Journal of Tech**’-
ever that may *• * what-
—is to hand ; and it is so
and so pedagoguish, and so abominably
well satisfied with itself that one takes pleasure in
saying money was never thrown away on a heavier
and less interesting publication. (Two or three
artiolea like Owen Blacket’s are insufficient to
modify this judgment.) The corps of Sydney
Technical College instructors who issue it are
doubtless very good souls in their way, and have
much valuable information to impart, but most of
them seem to have as little knowledge how to
use words to convey thoughts as Minister Jacob
Garrard himself. And one is haunted with a
horrible suspicion that grease for Minister Jacob
Garrard will be supplied in next number, as grease
for Minister Joseph Carruthersis supplied in this.
* * • • *
N.S.W. Agricultural Gazette is a much more
creditable official production. The A.G. —partly,
no doubt, because it is given away—penetrates
regularly into country regions whioh rarely see a
metropolitan newspaper. Farmers and selectors,
as a rule, are poor patrons of literature. There is
extant a Sydney bookseller who tells how in
his young days of ideals and enthusiasm he
partly imported, partly oompiled an immense
manual of agriculture specially written rour-rl
Australian conditions of weather and drops,
and humped it laboriously into the back-blocks.
And the representative Australian farmer would
dubiously turn over the pages which showed
him with many illustrations how he could rise
lo fortune in three good seasons, while holding
bis own in the three bad seasons and the three
half-and-half seasons intervening, and would groan
as he olosed the volume that times were too hard
—he really couldn’t afford it. Then, when the
disappointed agent had packed up, and was turn-
ing away to curse the representative Australian
farmer in his incomings and his outgoings, and
his downsittings and his uprisings, and his en-
silage and his fodder and his sheep, the r.A.f.’s
eye would suddenly brighten with an idea, and he
would lean over to whisper hoarsely, ‘ Say, mister,
hain’tyergot hanything blue? What’s thishyer
“Maria the Monk” I hear them talkin’about?
I wouldn’ mind givin’ ten bob for somethin’ real
spicy.’ So the book-man laboriously humped his
ideals and his enthusiasm and his whole art and
praotice of Australian agriculture back to Syd-
ney, and sold them for waste paper; and im-
ported tons of ‘Maria Monk’ and ‘Boccaccio’
and * Plain Blue Talk ’ and the rest; and on his
next visit to the backblocks the representative
Australian farmer would ride forty miles after
him on the chance of securing ‘ one of those books
with pictures of women like yer sold to Bill ’Arris
at the Nine-Mile Scrub.’
» * * *
That bookseller’s experience is another illustra-
tion of the dominance of primitive instincts in the
oountry. The patronage given to Burton’s
‘ Arabian Nights ’ at Sydney Free Public Library
shows the same instincts at play in the city.
Burton’s version of the ‘Nights’ probably con-
tains as much unadorned houri to the square page
as any book with classical pretensions enough to
be kept in a decorous Scotch institution, and day
after day the impecunious Domain deadbeat drops
in to relieve cerebral tension in visions similar to
those of the hysterical religionist who dubs her-
self (like Annie Besant at her passionate age) the
‘bride’ of a sensuously-imaged divinity. [Not
long ago a Methodist class-leader was inveigled
by a party of jokers to the Free Public in
order to read a book which justified the ways of
God to men in a most remarkable manner; and
was solemnly planted in front of a particularly
warm passage of the second volume of Burton.
He read on for a page and a-half, shocked, scan-
dalised, but expectant of some miraculous
moral; and when he reached the end of
the episode, and found it contained abso-
lutely no moral or ‘morals’ whatever, his in-
dignation was tremendous. Slamming the volume
with the slam of an outraged class-leader, he
lifted his hands over his head in the imprecatory
fashion of pictured angels at the bombardment of
Sodom ; yelled ‘ O, that men should imperil their
immortal souls by reading such a book! ’ and
rushed in one furious stride from the accursed
and ill-ventilated chamber. —But this is by the
way.]
• • • •
The primitive instincts play a larger part in
the country than in the cities, for agricultural
bush-environment not only fails, as a rule, to
stimulate the complex tastes which we asso-
ciate with civilised life, but gives no oppor-
tunity for indulging suoh tastes when formed.
And an unexercised ‘taste,’like an unexeroised
limb, quiokly atrophies and becomes useless.
Many of the poorer bush agriculturists are re-
duced by their environment almost to the level
of savages; and their promiscuous relation-
ships shock a city moralist. He calls these
‘ depravity,’ but there is more reason for calling
them ‘necessity.’ The city moralist, if he has not
wine in his cellar, or ale in his cupboard, oertainly
has a public-house at his corner. The ‘ immoral ’
bushman is possibly twenty miles or more from a
public-house—where the liquor is never examined
by a Govt, inspector ; and with maize at a shilling
a bushel he probably doesn’t often visit that pub-
lio-house. The city moralist has schools for his
children, a church for his wife, a theatre for him-
self ; parks to walk in and libraries to read in;
music, and picture-galleries, and pleasant scenery,
and agreeable social intercourse ; a suburban resi-
dence with gas and water laid on, and dainty food,
and a white cloth on his table, and flowers, and
slippers, and sheets, and many of the other things
that help to m&ko life tolerable. The ‘ immoral ’
bushman has none of these, but he has
twenty acres of ‘ clearing ’ studded with ntnumer-
able stumps which he looks forward to digging out
in his spare time —and the average city moralist
would be a raging and blasphemous heathen long
before he had dug out two stumps. Also, the
bushman has a creek half-a-inile away with a
muddy pool and memories of dead bullocks in it,
and he has a bucket in which he painfully carries
water from the creek, and he has tweed trousers
with fifteen seats in them laid one on top of the
other, and boots which he has made of raw calf-
skin, and a hut with a bark roof kept down by
huge stones which bulge it perceptibly and keep
the casual stranger awake at night wondering
when they are going to fall through on top of him.
Further, the bushman has children neatly dressed
in oatmeal-bags, and a horse so thin that he cuts
the harness, and occasionally he takes his gate off
its hinges—if he is rich enough to have agate—and
drives three-inch nails through it, and attaches it
to the thin horse, and starts out to ‘do a bit o’
harrowin’,’ while the oatmeal-bags toddle enthusi-
astically behind. And if you are dining with him
he may offer you some ‘ goanna,’ which he re-
commends as being nice and white, like fish —if it
is a bit tough ; or a little stewed wallaby ; or even
a slice of mutton, if there is a squatter near enough
tru.borrow a sheep from in the evenings. And he
has economical damper as heavy as the puddings
boiled by North Sea fishermen between two dishes
lashed together, so that they may swell in the
stomach and keep you comfortably full for
forty hours at a stretch. And he has heat,
and flies, and scrub—and when he tires of
them be has more heat, and more flies, and more
scrub—and so on to what seems like eternity.
And be has—the creature that he calls his wife.
She is his theatre, his pioture-gallery, his restaur-
ant, his new novel, his pleasant musical evening,
and a lot of other city relaxations—all rolled into
one.
* • • •
The monotonous misery of this picture is
realised to the full—with greater or less variety of
detail—by hundreds of back-blocks families in
New South Wales, in Queensland, and in Vic-
toria—probably in the other Australian provinces
also. The city moralist shudders at the deeds of
a Dederer or a Lyfield. Place city moralists for
ten years in the environment of a Dederer or a
Lyfield, and how many of them would aot differ-
ently ?
' « * » •
A while ago an Australian social reformer,
in great favor with the We-Can-Tame-U’s,
was led to ponder these things [He was the
same social reformer of whom it is told
that after an unprofitable spell at Sunday
oratory he sank lower and lower, until he was
glad to accept the hospitality and share the
hut of a Chinese gardener at Brunswick.
A week later another Champion contributor
met the gardener, and enquired how his colleague
X. was getting on. ‘Oh,’ said Ling Fish, ‘me
only keep him two day—him smell too much.’
And it turned out that X. had been evicted with
contumely—he was too dirty for the Chinaman. —
But this is by the way.] and straight resolved to
exploit the revolution obviously slumbering among
the mallee cockies. But after a fortnight’s mis-
sion and starvation he had not persuaded one
cookie to awake or arise, so returned despondently
to Melbourne and journalism.
• • • *
This social reformer failed in the most impor-
tant article of rhetoric : he did not identify him-
self with his audience—put himself in their place;
he had not learnt the advooate’s rule of giving the
jury not good arguments, sound arguments, bub
arguments that will tell. His comparison of
country misery and city luxury fell flat, for many
of the cookies had forgotten or knew nothing of
such luxury—oould not effectively comprehend it.
To them their own state was the normal state.
That Andy Jones killed a bullock every month,
and Susy Martin had tea twice a day— there, to
the poorer cockie and his wife, was a magnifi-
cence they could understand, an ideal they oould
strive for. But museums, libraries, picture-gal-
leries —of what use were those ?
* * • •
It is exalting, inspiring, to see to what heights
the human mind can rise, to mark how it stretches
and expands till its generalisations reach infinity,
and still hungers onward through the territory of
knowledge unexplored. But it is weird and ter-
rifying to see how the human mind shrivels and
contracts when the sunlight of a stimulating
environment does not call its rootlets out and up.
Now, like the tent in the Arabian tale, it is great
enough to house an army ; anon it is eo small that
the palm of your hand will hide it. Conceivably
a man of middle age, disillusioned of the world,
yet richly endowed with memories of years of
social intercourse and action, might find in the
vast solitudes of the Australian bush, in its splen-
did silences, in its seasonal changes and elemental
strifes, an intellectual stimulant unique in
potency, an intellectual peace in which he could
concentrate and develop the best of himself to an
extent impossible in the hurry and worry
of a city, continually draining nerve-force, as an
octopus-sucker drains blood, from a thousand
tiny pin-pricks. But the ordinary ‘selector,’
uneducated, hardly stirred by refining influences
of arts or letters, with his energy often exhausted
day after day by severe muscular labor, goes into
the bush as into a mental tomb. The loneliness,
the monotony, the round of mean and dispiriting
tasks, the eternal pressure of sordid trifles—these
things poison hope and aspiration at the source.
A bushman kills the snakes on his ‘ selection ’;
but there is a snake which avenges them all—the
‘ selection ’ itself. He may come to it bright in
face and weak in body. His body grows; his
muscles strengthen; his hands are hard and
knotty ; but his face becomes vacant, expression-
less—he is mentally inert. The ‘ selection ’is
twined round his throat, stopping the flow of
blood to his brain. The pressure tightens—
tightens : presently the man is dead. Then, pro-
bably, he puts a piece of crape round his hat.
There are whole bush communities in the N.S.W.
agricultural districts where the male population
wear crape habitually, constantly. It is a trade-
mark, like the butcher’s blue apron. A stranger
asks, surprised, ‘ Has there been an epidemic?’
Not so; these men mourn as by a kind of dumb
instinct. The Bush has strangled their souls.
• * * *
The women do not wear crape in this fashion ?
Why ? Their tragedy is as deep : have they less
imagination to realise it? The daughter of the
squatter, or small stock-raiser, seems for a time
more alive than the son to the palpitations of the
universe. But, as far as the writer’s observation
extends, in the poorer agricultural communities
the women live almost contentedly in their—
well, with the picture of a struggling selector’s
home before you, you might say ‘sty.’ Many
bush women leave the impression it is no
mere legend which declares that, in some neigh-
borhoods, when a stray lover from another dis-
trict woos and wins a girl, she has to be blind-
folded to get her on the train. But the agricul-
tural youth has tor a brief period gleams that
might almost be called poetic. He has even his
* wanderjahre ’ of a month or two in the great
city of his province. Insurance-canvassers or
sewing-machine agents driving along a lonely bush
track to a township not infrequently meet a
lanky, hard-featured, slab-sided son of Australia
returning to his father’s ‘ selection ’ ten or twenty
miles further on. He wears a soft hat, a new slop
suit, and an uncommunicative expression; and says
‘ Good-day !' stolidly, without a hint of the exul.
tation within him. Yet every kookaburra is
chattering of his glory; every wallaby along the
track is an amazed spectator of his prowess ; the
sun glints down through the branches, and the
breeze blows, and the gum-leaves eddy and whirl
—all to swell a triumph greater than Pompey
ever dreamt of. ‘Er hat seine wanderjahre
vollendet ’ —he has completed his travels; and
is returning home with his concertina wrapped in
his spotted handkerchief. To-morrow the news
will fly like lightning round the district—‘ Billy
Smith’s back again ! ’ —and next Sunday after
dinner, with the spotted handkerchief tied gal-
lantly round his neck, he will sit on the big log
near the gate and make music for a mass-levy of
neighbors, while he tells of the adventures that
befell him in the great city. It is quite safe
to wager that at this moment there are pacing
along scores of lonely bush tracks scores of Aus-
tralian youths filled with pride and vain-glory,
and anticipations of how they will ‘ take down *
the boaster who came home last year. And every
one of those youths is tenderly carrying a conoer-
tina wrapped in a spotted handkerchief. Here U
his tribute to Music and Art—his response to the
sweet influences of sound and colour—and here
probably is represented the climax reached by his
aesthetic impulses ere they are stifled in marriage
and the monotony of the bush. What pathos 1
what tragedy !
• * • •
Between isolated Man and Nature in the bush
there is perpetual warfare. You must either
dominate or be dominated. Not one in ten
thousand lonely selectors can avoid being forced
into the familiar agricultural stupor. Australian
shearers, who travel much ; Australian miners,
who often travel more; are frequently compared
with Australian agriculturists in point of intelli-
gence, and to the disadvantage of the agricul-
turists. Put shearers and miners on selections,
give them the same horizon to look at, similar
trees to cut down, and different cows to get out of
the same bog from one year’s end to the other,
and their superior intelligence will evaporate like
morning mist. Of all the cries that are cried to
Australian Governments there is none with, more
force than this : ‘ Help the farmer 1 favor the
selector 1 for Australia’s sake make the cockio’s
life more tolerable! ’ If there are subsidies
going, subsidise his roads and let him brush hk
rust off by friction against his neighbour; if there
are endowments to spare, endow travelling leo-
turers, and teachers, and singers, and reciters,
and strolling players—anything to lift him out
of the slough of mental stagnation. Or a world
of good might be done during Parliamentary
vacations by giving the members monkeys and
organs, and sending them forth to earn their
salaries by brightening dull agricultural evenings
—with proper allowance made to every member
who decided to be his own monkey and save ex-
penses. Eor the men and women who are fight-
ing the Bush need all the help and all the sym-
pathy that the less plucky or more fortunate
inhabitants of the town can give them—pioneers,
as they are, who are fertilising the desert with
their lives.
* * * »
There are sufficient clumsy suggestions of
agricultural apathy scattered over the foregoing
paragraphs to make it pretty clear why that sooial
reformer failed to excite a revolution among the
mallee cockies. Perhaps, if he had taken more
time, he might have achieved better results. Only
very ardent sooial reformers, who have but just
joined the Labor party, expect to do much revolu-
tionising in a fortnight. A whole generation
came between the era of Rousseau and Vol-
taire and the day when Louis Sixteenth’s blood
bubbled under the guillotine. N.S.W. Agricultural
Department—to think that all this sermon has
sprung from the text of the Agricultural Ga-
zette ! —seems content to go slowly. It has
learnt something since the days when it estab-
lished a mulberry farm to oust China from ths
silk market —though neighboring residents to this
day find the worms the best bait to use when they
go eel-fishing. And the Agricultural Gazette,
haying a practical and energetic young Australian
editor, is doing good work in assisting to combat
the farmer’s pests, and vary his crops, and give
him a little science to eke out his ancient rule o’
thumb. Some of its articles appear too technical
and abstruse for the average farming intelligence,
and Bill and Jim have knocked stuffing out of
Dr. Cobb’s views regarding the crow; but, on the
whole, the country seems to be getting good value
for the Government’s moderate expenditure.

UTILITIES, one who eschews everything but
what is strictly necessary. You’ve a penchant
for FLANNELS, but have a DERRY on SILKS,
and when there’s HOMESPUN about you can’t
see the occasion for bothering about PLUSH and
VELVET. Simple and good is your motto ;
you’re almost a Quaker, though not quite, and
you’d rather see your money in the bank than on
your back, and dividends are better, say you, than
Ribbons and Laces. Well, have your own way.
We provide for all sorts and sizes.
Or perhaps you are of another class. A world-
ling, if you will, floating down the stream of life,
with “ youth at the prow and pleasure at the
helm,” you want to dress well and to look well.

win their smiles. You want Clothes and Hats
and Boots, Gloves you must have, and a thousand

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SYDNEY COVE, JANUARY, 1788.
She sat on the rocks—her fireless eyes,
Teased and tired with the thoughts of yore,
And paining her sense were alien skies,
An alien sea and an alien shore.
In gold-green dusks she glimpsed new flowers,
And the glittering wings of gleaming birds,
But haunting her still were English bowers,
And the clinging sweetness of old love-words.
A soft breeze murmured of unknown shores,
And laughed as it touched her with fingers light,
But she mourned the more for the wind that roars,
Down sullen coasts on a northern night.
Like topaz gems on a sable dome,
The stranger stars stole shyly forth,
She saw no stars like the stars of home,
That burn white-fired in the frosty north.
A restless sea was at her feet,
A restless sea of darkest blue,
The lights burned dimly on the Fleet,
And these were all the ships it knew.
She watched the dark tides rise and fall,
The lion-tides that night and noon
Range round the world and moan and call
In sad sea-voices to the moon.
Through hour and hour they ebbed and flowed,
Till last with sudden splendor day
Lit all the scene with gold and showed
An arrow black on a garb of gray.
RODERIC QUINN.
MY CITY OF SAD THOUGHTS
I love the Night, the lonely Night,
When darkness like a pall,
A dewy pall, enshrouds the earth,
And silence covers all;
When in the low and quiet east
A faint band marks the sky
In dim and mystic undertone,
And sad-winged night-fowls fly.
When fields are lone and wet with tears
By dewy Earth-breaths shed,
And shadowy trees disturb the breeze,
And stars lie low o’erhead ;
When hushed and rare the sounds of air,
And Slumber spreads her courts—
For then I roam a queen within
My City of Sad Thoughts.
I am its queen, I reign alone ;
My servants come and go ;
Dim shadowy hosts of shapeless ghosts,
My sovereign will they know ;
They bring me dreams long past and gone ;
They bring me visions lost,
To move before my eyes again
Like scenes on white screens tossed.
A magic wand at my command
Waves wide o’er land and sea,
And all my sweet, far-drifted hopes
Brings memoried back to me.
Ah, dear the sights by Night revealed
That throng its visioned spots,
As I drift down the shadowed streets
In my City of Sad Thoughts.
I am its queen, I mount my throne ;
No other enters there ;
No foot profane its gates may gain,
No eye its spaces share ;
But in its solitude secure,
Lapped in its silence wide,
The mask I must by day endure
I here may cast aside.
For sheltered from the world’s cold eyes,
And guarded from its sneers,
The heart may breathe in freedom here,
Although it breathe in tears.
Oh, sweet respite from Fate’s despite,
From all Life’s fever spots,
I find within the twilight halls
Of my City of Sad Thoughts.
N.S.W.
G.M.V.K.
"WHEN I AM OLD."
When I am old
And my short span of years—•
Of mingled smiles and tears —
Is nearly told,
What will the bright world be ?
What will it seem to me
When I am old ?
When I am old
Will Life’s sun roll away
In evening mists of grey
And shadowy fold '!
Or in a golden light
Sink softly into night
When I am old ?
When I am old
Shall I find old friends true ?
Shall I find others new
My hand to hold ?
Will friendship prove to be
A bitter mockery
When I am old ?
When I am old
Will you, who love me best,
Have passed away to rest
Beneath the mould ?
Or living, will you still
Love me through well or ill
When I am old ?
When I am old—
Who knows how great the change—
What hearts the years estrange ?
Should yours grow cold,
Then every bitter breath
Shall be a prayer for death
When I am old !
When I am old
Surely thou shalt be near ;
No rusting years shall sear
Thy heart’s true gold !
May the great gods of grace
Grant me my light—thy face,
When I am old !
ZADIG.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission

other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, MARCH 27, 1897.
Federation Must Wait.
Premier Reid finally announces that he is
going to England, after all, to assist in the
great “record-reign” grovel, and otherwise
go on all-fours in a loyal crawl before his
beloved sovereign. In this matter, he is on
much the same level as most of his fellow-
Premiers, the crawl being nearly, if not quite,
unanimous. The only respect in which
Premier Reid is lower down than the others
is that he is the only one who struck a lofty
posture at the beginning of the trouble, and
announced —like the boy who stood on the
burning deck—that he would remain at his
post whatever happened, and that, whoever
else went to England to abase himself, G.
Reid would maintain an erect democratic
attitude, as befits an animal of large discourse
looking before and after. Then the succes-
sive gradations of Reid’s downfall became an
interesting study. First, he would only go
if Parliament and the nation showed clearly
that they thought it his duty to do so.
Secondly, he would go if strongly pressed.
Thirdly, the Government whip went round
canvassing M’sP. to see if they could be
induced to press him, and, just here, the
scornful refusal of Opposition-leader Lyne
to assist in pressing Reid nearly upset the
great scheme. It was also announced that
the matter would be left to Parliament to
decide ; and finally, G. Reid, without wait-
ing for Parliament, announces that he is
going. He isn’t going like the other
Premiers, because he wants to have a holiday
and an orgy, and to get any title that may be
going, and to wear a uniform and associate
with the Royal family, and have all his
expenses paid at a gorgeous hotel, and to be
an honorary member of a particularly aristo-
cratic club. Premier Reid is going from a
cast-iron sense of duty to see that the Anti-
alien Bill isn’t vetoed. The fact that the
sense of duty struck him like a pile-driver
just at this especial moment is mere coinci-
dence.
* * * *
All this, of course, makes very little
difference to anyone save Reid himself. To
all appearances, all the Premiers will roll up
to the great celebration. Whether they grab
effusively at the chance as Nelson and
Br addon did, or whether they go after the
emotionless fashion of Turner, who seems
absolutely indifferent whether he goes or
not, but is going all the same; or whether
they are reluctantly dragged by a sense of
duty like Reid, it is all one. Each man
goes after his own fashion, and Reid’s
fashion is naturally circuitous and apologetic
and crooked. The point which really inter-
ests the community is the conduct of public
business in the absence of all these poten-
tates. The present is the very worst time in
the year, and also the worst time in all the
country’s history, for such a unanimous
absence. Most of the Treasurers present
their annual statement and tell the annual
lie about the deficit early in July, and the
finances of five provinces will have to be
hung up and left to drift till the various boss
politicians get back from their gilded riot in
London. This, however, is a comparatively
small affair ; the finances are accustomed to
drift a good deal, and the absence of Nelson
and Reid can’t make them much more in-
comprehensible than they usually are. The
most lamentable feature of the case is that the
Federation question, which looked as if it was
really getting into motion after forty years
of suspended animation, has also to be hung
up, and what the consequences will be no
one can say. The Convention is expected to
finish drafting its constitution some time in
April. Then, according to the original plan,
the document should be immediately con-
sidered by the various Parliaments at a time
when there was no financial trouble to dis-
tract their attention, and the Convention
should meet again about August to discuss
the proposed amendments and make any re-
pairs to its original scheme which might ap-
pear necessary. The great record - reign
grovel breaks up this arrangement badly,
and the inevitable result is a long postpone-
ment, during which public interest in the
matter will slowly freeze, and Federation will
probably cease to be a live subject and be-
come again the abstract weariness it used
to be.
• * * *
In the first place, there will, according to
present arrangements, be no Parliaments
sitting in May and June to discuss the Con-
vention’s scheme. The absent Premiers
could, of course, delegate their powers and
let the work of the country go on as usual.
Brunker, Isaacs, Holder, and Fysh could
probably fill the places about as well as the
men who were away ; and the Convention
scheme could be put through its Parlia-
mentary stages, and the Convention could
meet again and finish its work without the
Premiers, if need be ; seeing that they would
all be absent, the balance of power, as be-
tween the provinces, would be absolutely un-
disturbed. Also, these deputies could de-
liver the financial statements at the usual
time, and be just as eloquently inaccurate
about the alleged surplus, if necessary, as
anybody else. The whole trouble is that if
the Parliaments meet as usual, the fact
that the Ministerial party is one vote short
and is minus its most important man will
be a source of weakness; and the locum tenens
may steer badly ; and the cablegrams which
keep explaining what a particular fool the
head of the Government is making of him-
self in England, and the fearsome manner in
which he is screaming after a title, and the
impossible things which he is being beguiled
into promising by the astute Chamberlain,
may break up his party, and the Govern-
ment may go to pieces like a house built of
cards. Consequently, a cablegram might
reach the Australian potentate just as he was
in the midst of the anniversary procession,
informing him that he was a Premier no
longer. Then, the reason for his presence
no longer existing, that politician, if he was
a strictly conscientious person, would get
down off his horse, if he was on one, and
quit the pageant then and there, and pay his
own hotel bill for the rest of his stay, and
drop right out of the show. The business of
the country is to be suspended so that the
Premier who is away grovelling in England
can’t meet with any such disaster as this,
and may have his grovel out in peace and
security. A Premier’s billet is essentially
precarious ; there is nothing in the country’s
contract with him to the effect that he shall
be especially secured against disturbance
while away neglecting his duty and attending
to private grovel ; and to suspend the
nation’s business indefinitely for the con-
venience of six or seven men who want
special facilities to flatten themselves out in
the presence of royalty is, at the very least,
unusual.
♦ * * *
As far as can be foreseen, the Parliaments,
instead of being ready to consider the
Federal constitution early in May, w r on’t meet
until the Premiers get back about August.
Then, instead of being able to devote their
time to the Federal question solely, they will
also have to deal with financial business
and other arrears. Most probably, if the
Premiers have been led into the usual
foolishness which every Australian politician
seems to get into when he visits England—
if, under the influence of champagne and
dazzle and titles and royal associations and
Joe Chamberlain’s persuasiveness, they
have promised to enlarge the Naval Tribute,
or to lend military aid in time of war, or
have committed themselves to some other of
the innumerable devices for strengthening
the “silken bonds’’—some of them will
have to face a motion of censure, and as
likely as not one Government at least may
go under. Then Federation will be again
suspended till an election takes place and a
new Ministry is formed. Even if there is
no such downfall, it is not likely that the
Convention will contrive to hold its second
meeting till November or thereabouts. By
that time some of the delegates may be dead ;
and some fresh inter provincial squabbles
may have arisen to disturb the always-pre-
carious harmony ; and the returned Premiers
will probably be in a beautifully loyal frame
of mind, as all returned Australians are, and
will want a very different kind of Federation
from the one they previously advocated ;
and the public enthusiasm which was roused
with such difficulty and which flickers out
so very readily, may be dead ashes once
more. If there ever was a subject which
wanted to be struck while it was hot, Fede-
ration is that subject. It has taken forty
years to get it even reasonably hot, and the
proposal that it should be immediately hung
up to cool and dry and moulder again for
anybody’s personal convenience is treason to
the Australian Commonwealth.
* T * *
There is no special reason why the Premiers
should not go to England—except that they
will probably be of very little use to any
Democratic community when they come
back, and that the spectacle of seven middle-
aged gentlemen trying to walk on their knees
is absurd at any time, and that George
Reid’s stomach and George Turner’s spec-
tacles won’t look notably beautiful in any
royal procession ; and that John Forrest’s
stupendous legs are not eminently calcu-
lated to decorate a Windsor uniform. In
fact, Bradpon is the only one of the seven
who is calculated to look like an ideal duke
and to reflect credit on this country so far
as personal picturesqueness is concerned.
But there is all manner of reason why the
business of the country should not be stopped
short, merely to insure any Premier against
loss of his billet while he is away grovelling.
The country doesn’t insure any Premier
against loss of his billet when he isn’t absent
in a state of moral abasement, and there is
no cause why neglect of work and a habit of
going on all fours at the other end of the
globe should carry with it any special privi-
leges. It is impossible to suppose that
Parliament can’t get on with its work for a
few weeks without the Premiers ; it will get
on without them when they die or are ejected
from office, and it can do so now also. The
Federation of Australia is too important a
matter to be hazarded for any mere ephemeral
crawl in London, and postponement is a
serious hazard. The Federal movement of
1891 died out from delay and sheer weari-
ness, and the Federal movement of 1897 may
easily do so likewise. Moreover, the work
of constitution-making was entrusted, in
part at least, to the Premiers as five sober
men with whom the country was reasonably
well acquainted. The five drunk loyalists
who will probably come back to take up the
work next August are quite a different con-
sideration. The country didn’t elect any
drunk loyalists.
Lone Leperland.
Queensland stands alone. A black-labor-
loving Government has admirably succeeded in
shielding her from the contamination of alliance
with the Democratic provinces of Australia.
It is amusing but sad to follow out the tricky,
shifty course of evasion and lying resorted to
by Queensland politicians to keep the province,
as a pariah, out of communion with the rest of
Australia, while not admitting the real reason
why isolation was so much desired. But the
reason can be proved to demonstration, and,
being known, the course to be pursued by those
provinces that have kept faith with one another
and been true to themselves is abundantly
clear. The curse of black-labor which makes
Queensland unfit to associate with white-labor
countries impels her Government to find excuses
for isolation which will sound better than the
cry, “ Unclean, unclean ! ”
* * * *
Look back a few years only, and what do we
find ? At the Convention of 1891, which
evolved the Commonwealth Bill, Queensland
was effusively loyal to Federation. The prin-
ciple that the presence of colored races in Aus-
tralia should be under the control of the
Federal Government was embodied in the cele-
brated clauses 3, and in the accompanying debate
the representatives of Queensland defended and
advocated that clause. The point to be remem-
bered is that Queensland was then a white-
labor country. It is true that colored labor
was still being employed, but the white labor-
party had triumphed at the ballot, and the
victory seemed so overwhelming that the advo-
cates of black labor were forced to abate their
demands and declare that, though not con-
vinced, they bowed to the will of the people as
expressed at the ballot-box. Alas ! the white
labor-party had not reckoned among the possi-
bilities that a Parliament elected expressly to
keep black-labor out of the country, could be
manipulated in such a way as to turn round
and bring it in. Yet the unexpected and the
marvellous happened; and by an act of gross
and treasonable treachery the filthy thing was
in violation of all constitutional precedents
again forced upon the country.
Mark the immediately changed attitude of
Queensland towards the rest of Australia. The
Premier of South Australia, Playford, imme-
diately after the proposed re-introduction of
Kanakas, respectfully suggested to Queensland
and the other provinces that a “ Conference of
representatives of the various Australasian
Governments should be held immediately with
the view of discussing the colored-labor ques-
tion and adopting a federal course of action in
the matter. To this federal and statesmanlike
&nT Sa w eV n y P rovince assented-but Queens-
i wu*" G ? vernmen t wriggled and shuffled
worked the wires to try and seduce Westralia—
the last hope of the Queensland Tory—to back
out; and at last, driven into a corner by the
remorseless logic of the S. A. Premier, consented
to take part in a “ purely consultative confer-
ence to discuss the question of coolie immigra-
tion into the Northern Territory, or intc
Australia generally, provided that no resolu-
tions are formally submitted.” Then one pro-
vince after another gave way and the proposed
conference was consigned to the limbo of abor-
tive federal projects. Since then the course of
the Q. Govt.—plausible generally and disingenu-
ous always—has been distinctly anti-federal.
Its pretended acquiescence in the resolutions
agreed to by the Premiers at Hobart, and its
subsequent violation, were strictly in keeping
with every other action of the province since it
took the leprous Kanaka back to its bosom.
How any sensible person can be surprised at
the Q. Govt.’s perfidy, or how any ordinary
truthful person can affect to believe in the
multifarious and fantastic excuses put forward
by the Premier and his colleagues, passes com-
prehension.
* * * *
The whole position is, and always has been,
clear as crystal. In The Bulletin of 26th
March, 1892, very shortly after the unscrupul-
ous planter-cim-banker party had got from the
M'llwkaith-Griffith combination a promise
of all it wanted, it was written :
The plantation aristocracy of the Southern States [of
America] came into collision with the white democracy of
the North, and the result was the bloodiest civil war of
modern days, and for the same causes, should the capi-
talistic party in the North succeed in making- Queensland
a plantation State, with servile labor for its corner-stone,
alas for the cause of Australian union !
* * * *
Alas, we repeat it, for the cause of Australian
union ! The magnificent province was thrown
again into the hands of the black-labor party;
and a reign of bribery and corruption was ini-
tiated such as has never before disgraced Queens-
land or any other province of Australia—a reign
of bribery and corruption so open, so flagrant
that even the capitalistic press is now crying
“shame” upon the Government. So utterly
immoral and debased has the prevailing tone
become that the Government does not take the
trouble to cover up the nakedness of its
infamy, and the marvel is that it took the
trouble to fling a tissue veil of mendacity over
the determination to stand out of the Federa-
tion. Queensland, governed by a colored-
labor clique, will never desire nor consent to
join a federal union. It is, therefore, the clear
duty of the Democratic provinces to proceed to
frame a constitution suitable to their circum-
stances ; if the constitution so framed is based
on broad and Democratic principles, it will be
accepted by the people of Queensland when
they resume the Government of their own pro-
vince.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Land Grant Railway Jobs.
Some more of the joys of the private lancl-grant
railway built by foreign capital are coining
home to the people of YVestralia. The present
trouble is over the W.A. Land Co. and the
Great Southern line. According to the original
agreement, the co. was to build the railway as
its own private spec., and receive from the
State, by way of moral support and encourage-
ment, a subsidy of 12,000 acres for each mile
completed. The land was to be selected by the
co. anywhere within 40 miles on either side of
the line, sections already sold and those re-
served for public purposes being alone excepted.
Thereupon the co. proceeded to t elect all the
unsold lands in all the towns along its route.
This was a vast amount more payment than
the State had reckoned upon—it had merely
contemplated giving 12,000 acres of agricultural
land per mile, and even that was worth about
twice as much as the whole railway. Conse-
quently, the Forrest Government flatly refused
to give away any town lands whatever. The
case has since then wandered back and forward
from the Governor-in-Council to the Privy
Council and the Supreme Court and other
places, and the general tenor of things has
been in favor of the co., but the Govern-
ment, so far, refuses to budge. It intended to
make an atrociously bad bargain, and appa-
rently, by sheer stupidity, it made one that was
several times worse, and now the only remedy
is to sit hard down and turn a deaf ear to
everything, and postpone the trouble as long as
possible. The province has paid for its railway,
first of all, by giving for it nearly 3,000,000
acres of land, which looks pretty good value
for 243 miles of line through easy country.
Secondly, it paid for it again by taking it over
at the price of =£1,100,000, or about £4520 per
mile, which is a very exorbitant figure, seeing
that the Government built its own lines at
.£3804 per mile. Thirdly, it seems to be in a
fair way to pay for it a third time by having to
hand over the best part of eight townships to
the co. bodily. At least, the letter of the law
is apparently on the company’s side, and unless
the Government simply takes its stand on the
ground that the land-grant crowd has been paid
nearly three times the value of the property
already, and decides to meet any adverse de-
cision of the courts by calling out the perma-
nent forces, if necessary, and bombarding the
syndicate, the probabilities all point to the miser-
able railway having to be paid for over again.
At present the co. and the Government are
engaged in an undignified process of hustling
and shoving over the matter, and their corre-
spondence consists mostly of irrelevant dash
and cuss and football scrum, and the point at
issue is avoided principally by bad language.
The private land-grant syndicate job almost
always ends more or less like this, which is one
good reason why Australia isn’t taking any
more private railway swindle at present.
“ ’Ats Hoff!”
It’s a queer and little-known circumstance chat
“ God Save,” at present almost as offensively
in evidence as “Ben Bolt,” is actually the pro-
duction of those to whom the House of Han-
over was the rankest of abominations. James
Stuart—“ the King over the water ” —was the
King the Jacobites wanted God to “save;”
“ send him victorious” clearly didn’t refer to
the mutton-headed monarch already on hand


And, queerer still—out of the nine stanzas of
ignominious fustian first mouthed in honor of
George 11., the six appended Hanoverian
howls have got somehow dropped by the way ;
so that, with the substitution of “ Queen ” for
“ King,” the wooden-legged psean, as it limps
along to-day, is, word for word, the original
Jacobite whoop. Miserable stuff enough, but
hardly such abject balderdash as the Han-
overian additions—per example—
Confound tall Jemmy’s plot ;
Pope, French, and Spanish knot—
Confound them all !
Villains notorious,
Their fears inglorious
Never shall conquer us—
Confound them all!
Now, if the Briton must have a “ hanthem ”
what’s the matter with “Rule Britannia”?
That chunk of national arrogance at all events
is national, and not fulsomely personal to a
family of stolid and undistinguished Germans ;
and Thomson’s words, though they embody a
vainglorious absurdity, are at least decent and
dignified verse—while the music, with its large,
defiant swing, doesn’t give you the impression
that you are assisting at the obsequies of a
deceased donkey. That’s the way “ God
Save” takes this paper, at all events ; while, as
for the sentiment, far better be a brazen “ bar-
racker” for the accident of one’s nativity than
the invoker of greasy blessings upon the head
of a prosaic lady in whom one is profoundly
uninterested.
The Federal Financial Muddle.
According to last year’s returns the revenue
which N.S. W. derives from public works (rail-
ways, tramways, telegraphs, telephones, &c.)
constructed with borrowed money is £802,000
per annum short of the interest winch has to be
paid on the public debt; therefore that amount
is the shortage which it is necessary to raise by
taxation. Victoria’s shortage is £754,000, and
South Australia’s £288,000. In other words
the shortage in N.S. VV. is 12s. 7d. per inhabit-
ant annually; in Victoria it is 12s. per in-
habitant ; in South Australia, which comes out
w r orst in the calculation, it is 16s. Id. Figures
for Tasmania and Westralia are not available.
The above is another of the pleasant aspects of
things which the Convention will run up
against if it tries to adopt Minister Car-
ruthers’ plan by which the Federation is to
take over the provincial debts and the
assets which they represent on a valua-
tion. Most of the N.S.W. delegates strongly
advocate the valuation principle, the idea
being that the N.S.W. railways pay so much
better than those of the other provinces,
and especially than those of Victoria, that it
would come out a heavy loser if the debts and
the corresponding assets were taken over in a
lump, without inquiry. But, though that part
of the N.S.W. debt which is represented by
railways and tramways pays remarkably well,
the very large portion which has no assets
against it, except fortifications, immigration,
forest-thinning, roads and bridges, public
offices, and the like, doesn’t pay at all.
Similarly, the part of the S.A. debt which is
represented by railways pays very well, but the
huge loan expenditure on roads, bridges, light-
houses, “geological examination of the pro-
vince,” etc., pays so very little that S.A. comes
out the worst of the three. In Victoria, the
railways pay badly, and the irrigation loans pay
still worse, hut there is so very little borrowed
money invested in roads, bridges, defences, and
other absolutely non-paying works that Vic-
toria, despite its miserably unprofitable rail-
ways, would come out the best of the three
under the proposed valuation system. There
are so many ways of adding up the figures,
however, that the valuation system is
likely to produce more blood and hair than
information. If any province will pay the pre-
sent writer a reasonable fee, he will guarantee
to produce a perfectly plausible-looking state-
ment of accounts proving that that province—
whichever it may be—will be the biggest loser
by Federation, and must receive large con-
cessions from the others to make things square.
And if they will all pay him a reasonable fee
he will prove that every one of them is the
biggest loser. This is what the respective
Statisticians are going to Adelaide to prove,
and when they get fairly started on explaining
each other’s unreliability the results should be
battle, murder, and sudden death.
Parsons and Politics.
A big noisy Wesleyan conference, sitting some-
where in Victoria, has resolved—
That in order to lessen the evils of intemperance at
election times, and reduce the cost of the general and
federal elections, as well as save the time of those who
live far away from the centres of population, the con-
ference strongly urged upon the Government of the
colony the advisability of introducing the plan of voting
by post.
This preposterous proposal to abolish the
secrecy of the ballot comes appropriately from
a corrobboree of parsons. That they should
give gratuitous advice to the world at large is
only to be expected. If the parson stopped
preaching he might have time to reform him-
self, and that would be disastrous to churchi-
anity. But when the cloth ventures to put a
sacrilegous hand on the political liberties of the
people, it is time for at least ink to be shed.
The secrecy of the ballot is a perpetual check
on the machinations of parsons, priests, fat
men, and bosses generally. It is now the cus-
tom in certain banks to insist on every clerk
producing proof that he is on the parliament-
ary roll—but Fat-man has to content himself
with the assumption that his quill-drivers
wfill vote the Tory ticket. It can never
be proved to him that they do or do
not. With voting by post, a regulation
would be made enjoining that the manager
must post all ballot papers (simply to prevent
neglect)—and the clerk that did not vote like a
“ right-thinking-person ” would suddenly hear
that the alarming continuance of the prevailing
depression necessitated further retrenchment,
and the wrong-thinking clerk would “go out
on his ear.” Likewise, the pleasant parson
would instruct the young men of his Bible-
class to bring their voting apparatus along
to his front parlor, so that they might
all plump for the prayer-and-property ticket.
The squatter would take a particular paternal
interest in the political antipathies of his varie-
gated staff. The contractor and the factory-
owner, and the militia colonel, and the priest,
and the landlord, and the mortgagee would all
adopt a most amiable, but undeniable, curiosity
concerning the voting of their subordinates.
Mr. Fatman would send canvassers round
the country buying up ballot-papers at
half-a-crown a piece, and the Argus would be
able to record an overwhelming Tory victory
at the next elections. Under the present
system (may the Lord be praised !) no man can
know for certain how his own son has voted.
The U.S. Fatman paper, the Nation, complains
that “ men took their employers’ money and
wore M'Kinley buttons, but voted the Bryan
ticket.” That is to say, the voting shows that
many must have done this, but under the ballot
it is impossible to prove any specific instance of
it. Under voting by post, men “who took
their employers’ money'” would have to take
their employers’ political opinions. Which
would just suit the trivial parsons down South.

The Libel Law.
Dear Bulletin,— Of all the laws of N.S.W.
the law of libel, as it affects the press, has been
the most execrated ; but as there are two sides
to every question, perhaps you can find room
for a line or two in favor of it, together with a
few suggestions for improvement. The sole
object of the libel-law is to protect the public
and to enable a man to keep his good name. A
man may have a “past”—that, as a rule, con-
cerns no one but himself, and it is expedient
that no one should rake up that past save on one
ground—that it is for the public benefit. The
exception protects the public at large ; the
general law shields the individual. Why
should a newspaper proprietor, whose sole aim
and object is to make money by his paper, be
placed in a better position as regards the libel-
law than a man who does not run a paper? In
N.S.W. there are no press laws, strictly so-
called—there is only a Defamation Act, which
applies all round—and on this account the
press is the power it is to-day. Once give the
press special rights, and inevitably the demand
will be made that special duties shall be im-
posed on it. The printer is as free as any other
member of the community—he is under no dis-
ability ; why, then, should he expect special
immunities? It must be conceded, however,
that a newspaper proprietor is often the prey
of the blackmailer, yet here again the ordinary
citizen is in the same position. • Both may be
sued by a man of straw, and in defending
themselves be put to expense that they cannot
hope to recover from him. There is only one
way to escape this evil—that is by giving the
Judges power—in any action, not in libel-
actions only—to order a plaintiff to find security
for costs unless he can satisfy a Judge that he
has a good cause of action. This will effectually
wipe out the blackmailer. Further, provide
for the consolidation of suits when they are
brought by one party against several, or by
several parties against one, provided that they
arise out of the same cause of action. Amend
the general law in these two directions, and I
think that the libel-law will be found fair and
liberal enough for any newspaper conducted on
decent lines. —Rus. Roberts.
Rev. Wardlaw Thompson, foreign secretary
of the London Missionary Society, addressing a
meeting in Melbourne the other evening, said :
“God has given the Anglo-Saxon race an
amazing hold of the best part of the earth, and
has surely done so for some high and holy pur-
pose.” Why drag in God? Shouldn’t the
obligation be put the other way about ? But,
taking the statement as it stands, it looks
“ uncommon steep.” The hordes of Tamerlane
—the Tartar humorist who used to built towers
of human heads—overran a considerable portion
of the earth in their time. Did God allow them
to do this for some high and holy purpose ? The
followers of Mahomet— whose representative
in Stamboul delights in butchering Christians
by proxy—were lords of most of the earth
worth having at one time. Was this in accord-
ance with some high and holy purpose of the
Deity ? To do them justice, they themselves
always said it was. So did Attila when the
horse-hoofs of his Huns trod the green
fields of Europe into dust and mud.
So did Ferdinand of Spain and his
successors when they sent the Inquisition
with its racks and thumbscrews and autos dafi
to convert the Aztecs. And so precisely does
John Bull when in more civilised, but quite
as relentless, fashion he grabs all of the earth
that he can lay his large, fat paws upon. He
regards himself as the agent of the Almighty,
and is mean enough to say that all who pre-
ceded him in the billet were frauds. One of
these years the tallow-eating Muscovites, or
some other crowd out of chaos, will come along
and say that Bull was a fraud, a blasphemer and
a rapacious ruffian, and will scoop in all of his
conquests for some high and holy purpose known
only to themselves and the Creator. It is facts
of this sort which make one think that the
Almighty must have gone on a long journey to
the other end of space shortly after dumping
Man on the Earth and that He must have ior-
gotten all about that little monster ever since.
The remarks of The Bulletin re the immense
effects which flow from trifling causes are well
illustrated (writes “M.A.8.”) by President
Zeal’s election for the Federal Convention.
Purse-proud, mediocre and self-sufficient, no
one had less claim on the democratic vote
which put him in. As The Bulletin remarks,
his inclusion in Syme’s list can only be ac-
counted for by the fact that he stood by the
Age in the big trial. And in connection with
the fact that Zeal stuck to the Age, it may
be noted that he had a little quarrel of his own
with the Vic. Railways. In one of his journeys
the President desired to take in the carriage
with him a favorite dog. The porter objected
that the free-pass system did not extend to
animals, and insisted that the dog should go in
the van. The station-master, when appealed
to, sided with the porter. Then the potentate,
incensed at the suggestion that his dog was not
good enough to travel with the ordinary paying
public, sought redress from the Commissioners,
but Speight stood firmly by his subordinates
and—well, he did not make a friend-for-life of
Zeal. When the big trial came on, Zeal’s
condemnation of the railway management was
comprehensive and thorough—though, of course,
he was probably not consciously influenced by
the dog-incident. Now Richard Speight is
seeking his fortune in the gilded West, and
Zeal, perhaps in no small degree through the
instrumentality of his dog, is going to help us
to fulfil our manifest destiny, and to establish
an eternal bond of union between the peoples
of this island continent. Who can tell what
changes in the course of history, what conse-
quences to millions yet unborn, may result
from the fact that, in times gone by, a porter
and a President had a row on a railway-station
about a puppy ?

In a somnolent sort of way Tasmania adopted
the Hare system of voting. Several times the
island has come within an ace of doing a great
thing in its sleep, but, unfortunately, it gener-
ally manages to wake up in time to prevent
itself becoming really famous. The other day
it became necessary to call the newly-elected
Parliament together, just to show that there
was such an institution in the country. As
there was so little business to be transacted it
was agreed not to trouble Governor Gormans-
ton to step around and make an exhibition of
himself and of Tasmania’s standing army,
Premier Braddon proposed to dispense with
the Speech from the Throne and other in-
augural flummeries that ought to have been
ridiculed out of existence years ago, and to start
things without any fuss and feathers whatever.
But at the last moment he repented of his
bright idea, and a scratch commission was ap-
pointed to hang around in giddy rags and go
through the usual formalities. The Usher of the
Black Rod summoned the Commons to the
House of Lords, the Commissioners read their
letters patent and formally announced that
they had nothing to say, and the Jawsoleum
was set fairly going without a solitary gun
being fired. Naturally, the local law’n’horder
press ponderously deplores the “ attempt to
degrade and bring into contempt Parliamen-
tary institutions,” but Democrats hope yet to
see Australian Parliaments meet and do their
business in a rational manner, without the
absurdities of wigs and gowns and Black Rods
and fal-lals, and men in false hair and petti-
coats, and the letting off of big crackers & la
the Chow Joss-worshipper.

Mr. F. A. Rochford writes to The Bulletin
from Port Moresby, New Guinea, on 19th
February :
Govt, is yetting out two parties to open two tracks
into the interior, and to build huts about every four miles
along the clearing for the use of those travelling. One is
to go towards Mt. Scratchley, and the other will open a
way to the main ranges 30 miles to the east. No commu-
nication has been received from the Mambare field for
many weeks ; and the last news was not reassuring, as it
was stated that no one was making anything like wages
and the men were about flooded out. Rumored that a
Malay and some whites (the latter must have come from
the Mambare fields) are on gold on this side of the ranges
near Mt. Musgrave. The information, however, is so vague
that little reliance can be placed on it. The only thing
that tends to give it color and hope is that the Malay (a
well-known man here) remains out during the wet season,
and those who know him say he must he on something or
he would have returned long ago. He had been out be-
fore, and found faint colors in black (iron) sand. Then he
took out a miner’s right and left here with the intention
of returning to the same place. That is about two months
ago.
A party of 13 men (new arrivals) has left here for the
interior by way of the Vanapa River. This is the route
that Gov. MacGregor opened, and one which is to be im-
proved by one of the clearing-parties. It is also the
route to where the Malay is supposed to be. The country
is slate, and there is a very large extent of slate formation
right through to the main ranges, which offers a fine
opportunity for prospecting, and no doubt contains gold.
My warning as to the difficulty of getting carriers is
justified by the trouble in procuring “ boys” for the cut-
ting party for the Vanapa route. The party was sup-
posed to start about 15th instant, but the comple-
ment of natives has not yet been secured. When natives
will not engage with Govt, it is of little avail private
parties trying any inducement. This question of carriers
will be the greatest obstacle that men going inland have
to encounter, and not one-twentieth of the men who are
said to intend visiting these shores will ever see the in-
terior. Until Govt, makes a good-horse-road—which will
probably be when the other track is rotting with the
bodies of the poor unfortunates who came here with
hopes of a “ rise ” —there is simply nothing to be done.
Gov. MacGregor thinks it more incumbent on him to
look after a few natives in the extreme west of the colony
than to help his own countrymen. To complicate the
situation, from Ist March a new law takes effect,
that “no person shall have in his possession a firearm
unless such person holds a license. The word or term
‘ firearm ’ shall mean and include any kind and variety of
gun or pistol used or intended for the discharge of any
projectiles of any kind, and also any part of a gun or
pistol.” The license is 20s. per annum. Such a tax in
Australia would not mean much, for there a person can
do without firearms, but in a country where his very life
depends on his being armed the effect is serious. Govt,
admits it is not in a position to protect those going
inland, and a man is asked to contribute 20s. per annum
for the privilege of protecting himself! And the head of
the Government is to be absent during the period when
every official resource will be strained to the very utmost.
A big rush is expected, and not the slightest preparation
has been made to cope with it, with the exception of the
two cutting parties. The work now is more than the
staff can get through by working a reasonable time. In
fact, one or two of the civil servants are doing the duties
of two. As an explorer the present Governor cannot be
be;ken, and all men must admire his indomitable courage
and perseverance, but something more than mere courage
will be required in dealing with the mass of men who
visit these shores. What the country requires is an
administrator on the spot, not an autocrat in the far dis-
tance.
In conclusion, every man who comes here should pro-
vide himself with a compass or two before leaving Austra-
lia. It is absolutely necessary. A man in the scrub is per-
fectly helpless without one, and it ought never to leave
his person. I owe my life to one, which during a most
trying journey seldom left my hand. In the mountains,
even if you can see the sun you have a sky more or less
densely cloudy, and without the compass it is impossible
to know where you are going.
One-Eighth.!
One who believes in the Woolgrowers’ Co-opera-
tive Association, Ltd., tenders this statement to
The Bulletin :
“ The conflict, Sydney Woolselling Brokers’
Association v. Woolgrowers’ Co-operative Asso-
ciation, Ltd., acts and re-acts on the chief industry
of N.S.W. The main cause of the conflict is the
woolselling brokers’ charge to the buyer of per
lb. ; which £d. is not only allowed for by the buyer
when bidding-, but is passed on with doubled
measure by him to the grower. For because of
this |d. charge, and because of the trade law that
no buyer shall advance less than |d. per lb. bid on
wool, the buyer who would bid, say, Bd. per lb.,
stops at 7fd ; for, did he bid Bd., he would have
to pay the broker Bd. + Jd., whereas, if he stop
at 7fd., he pays 7fd. + Jd. = 7gd. Thus, the tax
in its operation causes the buyer a profit of £d.
and the grower a loss of id. The buyer’s stopping
at 7|d. further injures the grower per medium of
this market. For, on comparison with the London
market, wool value here appears to be Jd. less.
* * * *
“In addition to the nominal §d. and real id.
the grower has paid, from Ist July, ’9G, and will
pay until 31st March, ’97, a further distinct £d. per
the broker. Here are Goldsbrough. Mort
and Co. s charges for past season \\ p.c.' + id.
per lb. to buyer = on 6d. per lb. wool, £2 Is. Bd.
p.c. + Jd. per lb. to grower = another £2 Is. Bd.
p.c. ; total =£s 13s. 4d. p.c. This percentage
on the 1 000 bales at <£lo per bale—often sold,
in one day—amounts to £3966 13s. 4d. ; not
a bad day s commission these dull times.
Yet Mort and Co. from the beginning of the wool
business up to their amalgamation in ’BB with
Goldsbrough and Co. made a fortune, built
magnificent stores on expensive land, and paid
handsome salaries out of a broker’s charge varying
from 3 p.c. to p.c. without any Jd. per lb. levy
on either buyer or grower. Contrast 3i p.c. on
one day’s sale of 7000 bales at £lO per bale with
past season’s charge of £5 13s. 4d. p.c. = £2450 as
against £3966 13s. 4d. Up to ’B9 the broker’s
charge was from 3 p.c. to p.c. inclusive ; from
’B9 to ’95 it = 3 p.c. on grower+£d. per lb. on
buyer, which on 6d. per lb. basis =£s Is. Bd. p.c.
From July, ’96, to 31st March, ’97, it = 1J p.e.+
sd. per lb, to grower + £d. per lb. to buyer =
£5 13s. 4d. p.c. From 31st March, ’97, it will=
3 p.c. on grower + £d. per lb. on buyer=
£5 Is. Bd. p.c. And as the £d. to buyer really
means %d. off grower, the latter’s virtual tax is
£7 3s. 4d. p.c. Against this the Wool-growers’
Co-operative Association, Ltd., will charge 3 p.c.
commission inclusive.
* * * *
, “ The £d. charge to the buyer was introduced in
89 from Melbourne, for the purpose of enabling
big firms like Goldsbrough, Mort and Co. and the
N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co. to meet the
losses resulting from banking business and pas-
toral advances. All growers who could estimate
the whole value of fractions quickly formulated
the impost’s effect—' the grower shears the sheep
and the broker shears the grower.’ The growers'
actual loss during past season, indeed, pans out in
surprising figures. 1£ p.c.+|d. to grower+the
nominal £d. to buyer which is a real |d. to grower
P-c. +£d.+|d.=just about £d. per lb. ; or, on
a £12,000 clip, averaging 6d. per lb. =£lloo=9j
p.c. off grower. Yet Mort and Co. made a fortune
by deducting from 3 p.c. to 31 p.c. from grower.
Most of the growers, however, were unable to
oppose the impost. They, in the first place, had
generally to remain on their stations during shear-
ing, and by the time the shearing was done three-
fourths of the clip would be sold. Again, scarcely
a grower felt himself capable, single-handed, to
successfully fight the big ring with its knowledge,
its tactics and its power. Many growers, indeed,
are held hand and foot by the big financial com-
panies, and each of these compels its growers
to send their wool to such broker as
is concerned in the said company. The
buyers, on the institution of the Jd. charge to
them, struck for the first five weeks. Con-
sidering, however, the non-organisation and other
general constraining circumstances of the growers,
they saw that they could not only make the grower
pay the buyer’s £d., but also that the buyer’s
nominal loss was his actual gain.
* * » *
“ The power of the brokers’ ring being so great
and comprehensive, only the big growers whose
knowledge, facilities and bank-balances made them
independent of everybody were in the position to
offer battle. The first to move was Jas. Leslie, of
Leslie Bros., Lerida, Muttaburra, Q. Leaving
his brothers on the station, he stored his wool
in Sydney premises, preparatory to selling it
privately or sending it to London to sell it there.
Wool-buying experts at first offered him his price
less the tremendous fraction, but, as he was his
own broker, they promptly closed on his terms. His
saving on 3000 bales was £lO4B. Other wealthy and
free growers refused to sell in Sydney, and shipped
to London, as the Sydney brokers’ charges were
equivalent to costs of freight, insurance and in-
terest ; so that Bd. in London is as good as Bd. in
Sydney, also, there is often a difference of Id.
per lb. in favor of London.
* * * *
“Last Xmas about a dozen growers, including
Jas. Leslie, Duncan M'Master, Jas. M. Allison,
C. S. MThillamy, and Francis Bacon, met and
decided to try and form a co-operative wool-sell-
ing co., unless the ring of associated brokers
reduced or modified their charges. Their pro-
spectus was published in the first number of their
official organ, The Co-operat:or, on Feb. 6 ; they
offer 20,000 shares at £3 each to wool-growers, and
will sell wool at a consolidated rate of 3 p.c. for
shareholders, or 6s. per bale, as against £5 13s. 4d.
p.c., or Us. 4d. per bale, as will now be taken off
the grower by the latest charges of the big ring.
♦ * * *
“ The ring, in its latest circular, sets forth that
the charge of ljd. p.c. + Jd. per lb. to grower
will, from Ist April, ’97, be altered to an inclusive
charge of 3 p.c. That Ist April manifesto, how-
ever, must not make it an All Fools’ Day for the
grower. The concession pans out, indeed, to
tremendous figures ;it means on 6d. wool 11s. Bd.
p.c., and, therefore, on the 379,000 bales sold in
Sydney from Ist July, ’96, to 27th Feb., ’97,
estimating the bales at £lO per bale, it reaches
the formidable total of £21,500. The conflict will
still wage while the brokers’ charge remains 3 p.c.
to grower + nominal |d. to buyer or real |d. to
grower. And as this 3 p.c. + £d. off grower
represents on 6d. wool a percentage of £7 3s. 4d.
off grower, the Wool-growers’ Co-operative Asso-
ciation, Ltd., charging a 3 p.c. inclusive rate,
should make the brokers’ ring more uneasy every
day of its existence.”
“ General ” Booth, ever fruitful in raising
the wind, now advises all his customers to estab-
lish the Lord’s corner. It means this: You are
to set apart one hen, concentrate her, her eggs,
and chicks, for the Loard’s treasury. So with the
milk and butter of one cow, the profit of one line
of grocery, drapery, and the like.
The finding of those two dead prospectors
by the Calvert Relief Party when they were look-
ing for Jones and Wells is another commentary
on how swagmen die in Australia. They passed
out of existence unsearched for and un missed
because they had not taken the high-sounding
name of “ explorer,” and their corpses were dis-
covered by a pure accident. In one page of S.A.
Govt. Gazette (4th March), giving the names of
persons found dead in public places in the pro-
vince during the previous six months, occur these
entries : —Benjamin Wood, aged 64, bushman ;
Charles Richardson, aged 47, found floating in the
Murray near Calperum station; Duncan M‘Aully,
aged 35, run over by train, body found on line ;
John Patschke, digger, aged 70, found dead near
Tiver’s well; Thomas Donnely, aged 30, found
dead on bank of Rocky River; James Murray,
aged 56, shepherd; John Hisgrove, swagman,
aged 60, died from want of water near Cadelgo
station ; Guy Mannering, aged 65, swagman, died
from want of water near Undoolya station ; Albert
Blanford, aged 22, found on Christmas Day on
Anna Creek station, died from thirst.

A sceptical doctor recently called at the
Edison Electric Parlor, 162 Pitt-street, Sydney,
to test the effectiveness of the X rays. He placed
his hand upon the fluorescent screen, and straight-
way saw several shot pellets which had been
lodged in his hands in boyhood. The latest
novelties for the Vitascope, sensational and
humorous, provide more entertainment than all
the numerous imitators put together. Other
attractions are the Edison Kinetoscope and
Kinetophone.*
