“Nemesis,” a sketchy, gauzy thing, with
a good deal of fun and bustle and pretty music in
it, yet with no very tangible characteristics except
the bustle, is this week’s bill at Sydney Lyceum.
It was produced in Sydney once before at a rather
uncertain date by someone this paper can’t dis-
tinctly identify, but to all intents and purposes it
may be set down as new. The music consists
largely of judicious steals from many sources, and
the name is a misnomer, for all the nemesis which
strikes the bad, giddy hero is that he gets hugged
by two very nice girls, whereas if he had adhered
strictly to the paths of rectitude he would only
have been hugged by one girl. Bracy is the man
who falls into this pitfall. He is engaged to one
nice girl, but about 1 a.m. another steps out of
a sea-side hotel in a species of undress cut low,
to shed poetry in the moonshine on the sea-shore ;
and Bracy mashes her, and her fierce military
uncle rushes out, armed with ironmongery, and
causes him to become engaged to her on the spot.
Thereafter the play consists of the usual comic
troubles of the man with turn girls on hand, set to
music. Ha has to dine with both fathers,
and consequently has to eat a great deal more than
is good for him, and he has to explain many things
, with much difficulty, and to get out of the way
judiciously when the two fathers start bashing
each other. Knight Aston is possibly rather the
better of the pair, but John Forde as the other
father is close up, and they are two particularly
, admirable old men. Also, they have two very
admirable daughters, Julia Spencer being the
one whom Bracy wants to marry, while Mabelle
Darley is the lady whom he reckiessLy mashed by
moonlight on the shore. Miss Darley made a
marked success on her first appearance at the
Lyceum, and she seems to improve both in her
singing and her acting as she goes on. When she
finally gets rid of her nervousness, and can let her
belldike voice go without wearing the anxious
look which now pervades her two pathetic eyes
she should do even better. A number of other
people, including Bracy and Mrs. Bracy do very
well; the chorus is drilled to perfection, and it in-
cludes more commendable legs than this paper
ever saw before in a chorus of the same size ;
Pack’s orchestra is practically perfect; and the
scenery is all that could be desired. The only
thing which can be complained of is a certain
want of meat about the play, which is a jumpy
and gassy little trifle with more leg and gauzy
frock in it than anything else.
• * * *
A casual interval in the story, when the
confused Bracy is away getting married to both
girls at once, is filled in by Albert Whelan, a
young solo humorist of the Knowles order, but
with'far more versatility than Knowles has dis-
played up to date. Whelan vaguely suggests the
lamented Frank Lincoln in some respects. He
can mimic almost anything and anybody, and in
the course of a few minutes he does banjo, man-
dolin, and violin solos with no other instrument
than a large and expressive mouth, gives perfect
imitations of several well-known actors, renders
“ I won’t play in your yard ” in the childish treble
of the little girl actress, and throws off several
other trifles. They are all admirably done, and a
little more experience and a little encouragement
in the shape of .£6O a week salary should develop
Whelan into something far above the average.
The Bulletin particularly wants to hear him mix
that spectral cocktail which used to be a refreshing
feature in Frank Lincoln’s programme. Years
have elapsed since that gratifying drink was last
heard of, but this paper has never come across a
cocktail to equal it since, and never hopes to,
unless Whelan can supply it.
** * *
Thornton’s new venture at Sydney Cri.
has caught on much better than this paper
anticipated after a first night’s inspection of the
garish “Bookmaker” and his uncouth munifi-
cence. Perhaps it is the simple little love-story
which appeals to the public soul. Every one
knows how unaccountably the domestic Sarah
Jane will get her hair on fire over the kitchen
candle, while she sheds a grimy tear on the grimy
page of the Jernil that is grasped in her copious
red hand, and reads about the woes of the lovely
girl who was blighted in love. There is a lot
of the Jernil element in humanity generally,
and as the “ Bookmaker ” has two agreeable girls,
both blighted, and two pleasant young men
both ditto, it appeals strongly to this side
of the public character. Bessie Rignold and
Noney Seabrooke, when they weep jointly over
their wrecked affections, are a soulful picture.
And the pathos of it is relieved by the turfy man
living in an agony of embarrassment all over the
stage, and shedding gold on the insolvent family
at every turn. He is a quaint mixture of Guppy
and Monte Christo, and though the humor which
consists in dropping a very aggravated Guppy
into the middle of an aristocratic family is by no
means new, the lavish generosity of this particular
Guppy, and the nobility of character which he
attempts to conceal by devouring peas with his
knife, make him look more or less novel. The
utter want of any redeeming feature in the
character of the marquis may also have something
to do with the popularity of Thornton’s party.
The marquis in the Jernil is always a very bad
rich man, and when the blackened Sarah Jane is
resoued out of a blazing kitohen, with the remains
of the paper still clutohed in her hand, it is gene-
rally discovered that the menial’s hair combusted
over the candle just at the place where his perfidy
began to fully develop itself.
* * * *
Westmacott announces that the “ Sham-
rock and the Rose ” both wither to-morrow
(Friday), two weeks of the noble-hearted Irish
peasant and the process-server and the informer
having been sufficient for a tired public. The
last night of the dual herb is to be signalised by
the distribution of a Red Bird bike to some lucky
ticket-holder, who will probably hie forth and
break his neck falling off the machine shortly
afterwards.
# # * *
There is little to say this week about
“ Matsa,” except that that brilliant spectacle is in
its usual good health. Knowles’s songs, “ P’raps ”
and “ The Benches in the Park,” are not quite so
young as they were, but that comedian is still
advertised to grind them out nightly; and he
tells most of the same old stories in the same old
hurry, with th s same bashed hat and white pants
and long black coat and rather venerable boots.
Gulliver mail tains an air of novelty which the
other weary jokist has long since lost. But even
if Knowles was several dozen times more ancient
than he is, “Matsa” could stand him ; the pic-
torial magnificence of that spectacle, and the soul-
fillingness of the Rising of the Nile, and the view
of modern Cairo, and Lauri and Howard Vernon,
and the legs and other things would cover almost
all the sins in ten shattered commandments.
# * * *
All the good old names appeal as usual on
the programmes of Sydney Tivoli and Palace.
Godfrey appears at both houses. The Allisons,
Jessie de Grey, the Leslies, and Frank
Lawton, that successful whistler and unsuc-
cessful pantomimist, are at the old establish-
ment ; while Granville, Cogill, Coleman, and
Alice Leamar haunt the Palace. The shiftings-
about of these people from one theatre to the other
are difficult to follow, but they can always be
found at one of the two institutions. Most of
them have settled down to be almost as regular as
washing-day by this time.
* * * *
A benefit is being organised for J. W.
Sweeney, an old favorite and probably the best
Irish comedian in Australia. As an artistic and
amusing stage ruffian in red hair and a shattered
hat, there are very few men in Australia who can
even approach within gunshot of Sweeney. That
gentleman, despite his many years of good service,
has never yet had a benefit, and he wants to see
what it is like, so it is to be hoped he will have
a big one.
* * * -*
The latest cinematographe, a very fine
one, has opened at the Crown Studios, 382 George-
street, Sydney. The special glory of this show is
a series of mountain views taken from an express
train —a beautiful piece of scenery which
skedaddles past the spectator in red-hot haste and
produces a weird effect. This makes the sixth
cin. that has struck Sydney, and already another
one is rumored.
* * * *
A theatrical man, who saw “Two Little
Vagabonds ” in London, criticises the Melbourne
performance with reasonable severity, pointing
out that much of the pathos is sacrificed to melo-
dramatic effect in the adult-character acting, and
that one or two of the secondary parts have been
strangely misread. But shortcomings in the
pathetic scenes may not be business faults where
an Australian audience is concerned. The trouble
is that only a small percentage of the population
takes kindly to plays which bring tears into the
eyes. “ Two Little Vagabonds ” was not intended
for a country where callousness is cultivated by
the rising generation as a virtue, and a lump in
the throat produces a sense of false shame.
* * * *
“ Two Little Vagabonds,” at Melb. Prin-
cess’s, is only a moderate success thus far, and
The Bulletin, looking for reasons why the very
pathetic points of this melodrama have not hit
the managerial mark as expected, finds a principal
reason in the fourth act, which presents a series
of hideously impossible events in the Gaffer’s
garret. It is here that the enterprising vag. and
his long-lost father turn up, one at a time, for the
purpose of obtaining a bundle of letters. The
father proves himself an unprecedented ass by
drawing cheques for largo amounts whilst Gaffer
and Co. are preparing to take him into captivity ;
also the heroic small boy, who ultimately
steals the precious letters and rescues his
parent from bondage, rubs a lot of sentimental
bloom off his character when he passes the gin-
bottle to a drunken harridan and sprinkles the
last remaining drops of liquor upon her dishonored
head. A wild acrobatic finish to this scene of
squalid dissipation is foolish beyond endurance.
A few urchins in the amphitheatre applaud voci-
ferously, and the rest of the congregation stares
aghast. “Two Little Vagabonds” would be
vastly improved, for Australian purposes, by the
instant suppression of Act 4. The festive nipper
might steal the letters when the curtain was down
and return home in the last act to be formally
presented to his old man. However, the easy
possibility of dispensing with Act 4 entirely is
not worth discussion, seeing that no such reforma-
tion will be attempted.
* * • *
Other glaring faults in “ Two Little Vaga-
bands ” are, firstly, the death (by a fall from his
horse) of an incidental soldier who gets robbed of
the aforesaid letters, and, secondly, the hazy
assassination of the consumptive boy. The corpse,
in both cases, is quite uncalled for, serving merely
as an excuse for a melodramatic blood-splash on a
white shirt-front, and a solemn removal—in the
soldier’s case —of some rigid remains upon
a stretcher. The boy would be better left alive
than dead when the curtain falls, but since the
authors chose to kill him in the interests of Gloom
they might as well have added a grand funeral
tableau.
• * * *
Miss Florence Terriss and Marie Neilson
improve carefully on their first-night performance,
and all the places where the audience ought to
weep most freely are now saturated with tears.
Miss Terriss, indeed, begins to seem thoroughly
well suited to her character after she gets into
velvet knickerbockers. Miss Ferrar is the same
warm, gushful—perhaps too gushful—mother as
before, whereas Julius Knight weakens a good
deal on acquaintance, and the vulgar Gaffer side
of the drama, misplayed at the outset, is getting
jvorse in its extravagant unreality.
** * *
“ A Village Priest,” with a somewhat new
cast, fetched an appreciative crowd to Melb.
Bijou on Saturday. The revival is for six nights,
and, although a long, searching enquiry into the
religious character of Grundy’s play (from the
French) justifies a verdict of guilty against con-
fessional oaths, and notwithstanding that the
drift of the famous miracle text is quite incompre-
hensible to ordinary mortals, The Bulletin accepts
l’abbe Titheradge with thanks. He means well, no
doubt, and his piety does no harm to anyone except
convict Brough. For that matter, even Brough
comes out all right in the end, when he returns to
prison for a short twelve months —a trifling cir-
cumstance compared with his previous incarcera-
tion—and thereby wins a Martyr’s Crown. Many
people would be glad to win a place in Heaven on
the same terms. H. R. Roberts, in the character
of young D’Arcay, is not so filling as Miss Watt
Tanner’s performance of the blind widow, but it
passes muster, whilst all the old favorites appear
to greater advantage than ever. Time is bring-
ing them nearer to the ages of the people they re-
present. Next Saturday “The Passport,” fol-
lowed by “Mrs. Tanqueray ” on Wednesday.

At Poverty Point .
Two Hambourgs are coming to Australia this time :
Mark, as pianist, and his younger brother Jakoff as
violinist. The pianist, though petted and feted in Paris
and London m! Vienna, still yearns for Australia—all
along of a Mtlb .urne girl, it is said. Or can it be that he
wants to be picknicked again by the 27 Sydney ladies who
took him out for an afternoon and refused to allow any
other male to be present on the occasion ?
Melb. demise : Sylvanus Angus, compo. A fine bass
vocalist, unrivalled from 30 to 40 years ago. His singing
of “ Ruddier than the Cherry,” from “ Acis and Galatea,”
was thought almost as good as Melb. ever heard. The
Philharmonic was never complete without him.
Mr. and Mrs. Caleb Porter, who arrived per last mail
under engagement to Williamson and Musgrove, are
pretty well known on the London stage. Caleb was for
three years stage-manager at the Adelphi, and for two
years with the “ Niobe ” co. on tour. He is a cousin of
Softgoods Sargood, but that’s not his fault. Mrs. Porter,
otherwise Marie Neilson, has been associated with several
of the Adelphi successes, and will appear in Australia as
Stephanus in “ The Sign of the Cross ” and one of the
“Two Little Vagabonds.” The husband is a good black-
and white artist.
Last week’s cables brought news of two men who
appeared in the first English production of “Cloches de
Corneville.” Shiel Barry, the original Gaspard, is dead ;
Harry Paulton, the original Bailie, is coming to Aus-
tralia, where he will meet his old colleague, Knight Aston,
the tenor fisherman of the show aforesaid. Shiel Barry,
having started his stage life on this side, some years
before he recrossed the sea, ranks up to now as the
strongest of Australian actors. At ’Ome he soon made a
mark in the Irish “informer” line of character, and his
name became identified with Boucicaultian drama in its
halcyon days, when author-actor Dion B. earned and
squandered thousands a year, and the virtuous colleen
hadn’t yet lost her freshness, and his riverence the parish
praste was a comparatively new personage. No subse-
quent process-server and blayguard informer has quite
come up to Shiel Barry, though they all do their best to
reproduce him. His Harvey Duff, in “The Shaughraun,”
was a remarkable performance which prepared the London
public for his thrilling success, two or three years later,
as miser Gaspard. “ Les Cloches ” dates back to ’7B, but
Shiel Barry will not be really dead until the last surviving
witness of Father Gaspard’s terror has ceased to remem-
ber that awfully serio-comic spectacle.
Frank Cates s tw r o daughters, the younger of whom was
shipped ’Ome the other week, have been adopted by a
great-aunt. His two boys remain in Melbourne under
the care of Tom Kennedy, and are supported for the
present by the Cates fund, which was raised from a
benefit performance in Sydney.
Signora Guidotti was the most curiously looked for
amid the principals at the Coy benefit, in Melb. During
the years since she became Madame Rubira she has
almost doubled in size.
Dear Bulletin,— With Cecchi passes out the last of
a notable trio of Melbourne voice-tutors in the main
responsible for the vocal predominance of the stink-
ful city. Taking results, Sydney has never had three
teachers like Madame Simonsen, Lucy Chambers and
Cecchi , nor is Melb. likely to get worthy successors to
those “mighty dead” until the papas of fashionable
Misses find it safe to float a fresh lot of limited com-
panies.—T.D.
Writes a fair-sex correspondent“ Walter Baker in
passionate scenes rouses a wild desire in one’s breast to
heave a brick at him, or do something to liven him up a

Dear B., —Your scribes3es all seem to hold a brief for a
nice young Melb. man who has designed divers some-
what-garish dresses for pantomime, and been credited
with every created thing in this line that has come along
during the last two years. “Cleo” has joined in the
chorus, and remarks that whilst “ his color is all right, in
form his designs rather need simplifying.” This is picked
out of the jam. The reference is to “Matsa” dresses.
Really, the color is nearly always a crude and trying com-
bination of reds and blues, with an occasional yellow
that gives an artist bilious symptoms. The prettydresses
in “Matsa” are mainly imported, notably those worn in
the flower-ballet, which were sent out by Musgrove. This
artist’s designs, too, with their hard lines, are usually
suggestive of nothing so much as the picture-cards in a
fantastic “ Murkan ” euchre-pack. There are good spots,
and the dresses of the girls representing the local papers,
when “Matsa” was in Melbourne, were worthy of a two-
line benediction.— Dead-Hkad.

is rendering substantial assistance ; and the performance
of Berlioz's “ Faust,” set down for next month, will wit-
ness Josephine Deakin and Jules Simonsen in the soprano
and tenor rOles respectively.
Kennedy, now on his way to Australia again, was lately
reported to be running his awful mesmeric show with
women subjects in the States. A mesmerised female
stiffened across two chairs, with the fleshy professor
squatting on her chest, would probably be a great draw.
Also, there are possibilities, principally in the way of
stocking and ankle, when the lady subjects begin drop-
ping off their chair 1 and accumulating in an insensible
heap on the floor.
Walner Gregory (M ss Watson’s husband), who is looking
after the front of the house for Bland Holt, owes his
stage Christian name to a printing error. An ingenious
compositor set-up “ Walner ” for Walter on a playbill
when Gregory was quite new to the profesh, and the
mistake suggested an easy means of distinguishing him-
self from other actors of the same surname and initials.
Critic Archer hints Mrs. Pat Campbell could not get a
part in the last new London production. How’s this?
Bubble reputation.
Some One :—“ Why don’t the ‘ Lucifers ’ get themselves
photed with some little regard to anatomical possibilities?
In a recent Australasian photo, the male Lucifer shows
on his left shoulder a huge mass of cable-like muscles,
while his right shoulder looks quite different. The only
defect of those cables is that from their position they
could not have Buckley’s chance of attaching to bones
anywhere. Wonderful is the power of the brush in photo-
graphy, but the brush might at least lend artistic veri-
similitude to a bald and unconvinoing narrative.”

dence since Boucy left, and is as good on rather different

the stage, treating people like bags of sawdust. D.G.
veritably relaxed, and Brough is yet freer. Wooden
staging comes from France. In Germany and Italy there
is more ease.”

is 22 years since I came to Australia, but 10 out of the 22

prominent tenor part in comic-opera during that score of
years.

Irving’s rather serious knee-sprain came about in an
odd way. After the Richard 111. premifere, he chickened
and champagned the critics, as per usual. After going to
bed he got hungry as a hunter, crept down stairs, and
wolfed some cake. Going back he hurt himself, so that
the house had to be raised and the medico telephoned.
At Brunswick, Vic., where horrible
ructions were expected on St. Patrick’s day, there
were policemen enough along the line of march
to have eaten the Hibernians. At length the
people raised a cry : “ Get away, and let’s see the
procession ! ”
THE SEVENTEENTH.
You fool, with green looped in your coat,
You other fool with yellow,
Each hungering to clutch his throat
And choke the other fellow;
Leave men in lands afar from ours
Their quarrels dunder-headed,
And learn horse-sense in bushland bowers,
When o’er the hills the wattle flowers,
And green and gold are wedded.

The WILD CAL
COLUMN
According to the Town Clerk’s report, the total
Income of the Municipality of Sydney for 1896 was
£166,500—£40,000 less than it was two years ago—and
this left a deficit of £40,000. It was suggested, the other
day, to add a penny to the current year's rate with a
view to slightly reducing the deficit—it would have re-
quired an extra rate of 4d. in the £ to quite fill the gap—
but even this very moderate suggestion was set aside.
Sydney municipality is a blunderingly-managed concern
which has had woeful ill-luck with its officers and its
accounts, and in many other ways, and it badly wants
some new blood in its municipal billets. It is expected
that Mr. Robert Anderson, of the Bank of N.Z., will be
the new City Treasurer —the former Treasurer being
“in trouble” over an affection of the chest—and if so, it
ought to be the beginning of a much-needed reform in
one department, anyhow.
• • * * •
Sydney City Council recently floated a per
cent, loan of £150,000 privately at £99, and was rather
proud of the operation. Apparently it would have done
much better by calling for tenders. The third-rate town
of Geelong (Via) has just put a 3J4 per cent, loan of
£13,000 on the open market and it realised £lOO 2s. sd.
At this rate the Sydney operation was a needless loss of
£l6BO, even assuming that Sydney’s credit is no better
than that of—ye gods!—Geelong. Sydney municipality
has always had a fine record for financial blundering.
• * * •
This is about the time when N.S.W. Treasurer
Reid should begin to think hard about faking the surplus
for 30th June. Under the old system of keeping the
accounts, each year had to be charged with all the expen-
diture belonging to it, and the Treasury was so slow in
finding out what expenditure belonged to any particular
year that the accounts, of 1892 were not expected to be
closed and the deficit actually arrived at till 1895 or ’96.
Under the “ cash basis” inaugurated by Treasurer Reid
the year is credited with all the money received and
debited only with the money actually paid. There is no
obligation upon the Treasurer to pay all the year’s
accounts that he possibly can and merely leave unpaid
those which can’t be got in. He may, if it suits him, re-
verse the order of things, and for the last three months
or so pay as few as possible and leave as many as possible
unpaid, and by leaving £300,000 of the year’s accounts
unpaid he makes a * 300,000 surplus quite easily. There
is no independent authority to inspect him and see that
he doesn't do this, the matter being outside the Auditor-
General’s province, so naturally he does it. At the end
of February, with only eight mon f hs of the financial year
gone, the N.S.W. deficit was already £442,000, so to pro-
duce even a £58,000 surplus it is necessary to save half-
a-million inside four months. Therefore April is about
the time when a spell of hard, consistent faking should
commence.
• • * *
The N.S.W. wheat-market is in a curious con-
dition. In Riverina alone it is reckoned that there are
250,000 bags of wheat in store, and at present un-
saleable. The quantity in other parts of the province
is unknown, but the sum total is believed to be something
enormous. The Sydney market is glutted with imported
supplies, and further shipments are still pouring in. The
farmers are now willing to sell to the Sydney dealers
below the price of imported grain, but the dealers are so
loaded up with shipments to arrive—ordered under the
impression that the N.S.W. harvest would be a poor one
—that they cannot buy further at any price. It is a
quaint combination of Freetrade circumstances. The
Riverina farmer can’t find a buyer in Sydney, yet on the
17th March some 4000 sacks of Maoriland grain were
landed there. On the 16th 39,000 sacks wheat arrived
from San Francisco. On the 13th over 12,000 bags of flour
from the land of the fragrant leper were fired ashore per
Chinese steamer; on the 11th some 2000 bags flour
dropped in casually, mostly from Maoriland ; on the Bth
over 2800 sacks of flour turned up per Japanese steamer ;
21,000 sacks flour arrived on the 4th from Vancouver ; two
or three small consignments arrived from other provinces
on the 3rd ; the San Francisco steamer brought in 5000
sacks flour on March 2 ; and so on ad infinitum. A really
busy day recently was February 25, when three vessels
arrived from places outside N.S.W. and dumped down big
lots of grain and flour on the Sydney wharves simul-
taneously. No wonder the Riverina farmer finds the
market glutted.
* • * *
The Mutual Assurance Society of Victoria pub-
lishes its last balance-sheet as a separate institution,
being now in proa ss of amalgamation with the National
Mutual. The figures show a steadily declining business ;
despite its unquestioned soundness, the Mutual of Vic-
toria has been doing a smaller and smaller business ever
since 1887. The large amounts written off for deprecia-
tion last year have apparently further “knocked” the
institution ; an office which admits and writes off its
losses, and goes in for absolute soundness even at
the cost of small bonuses, has no chance with
the average insurer against the one which pays
big bonuses and denies the existence of losses, and
gaily dashes the consequences. The Mutual’s income
from renewal premiums is £100,304, against £102,533 in
’96, £107,272 in ’94, and £113,112 in ’93. New premiums
are £7641, whereas in ’93 they were £8489. The total
income from premiums is therefore nearly £14,000 less in
’96 than in ’93. Interest and rents are £44,851, which is
onlv £9O more than it was four years ago. Death losses
(£34,689) are very moderate, but nearly £24,000 goes in
surrenders. In ’95 the surrenders were over £24,000 ; in
’94 they were over £29,000, and in ’93 they were nearly
£28,000. Another sum of £lll3 is added to investment
fluctuation fund to cover possible depreciation of assets,
making this reserve £22,523, and with all these heavy
drains upon it the amount added to the funds is £36,000,
which is perhaps rather more than might have been ex-
pected, making a total of £901,000.
The interest earned upon the invested funds is £5
Is. 6d. per cent., which is decidedly good, and the fact that
the amount of interest in arrear is very small indeed and
steadily growing less speaks well for the safety of the in-
vestments. The only depressing item is “ Freehold pro-
perties acquired by foreclosure, £85,274 ’ which is an
increase of £20,000 over the figures for ’95, and is £51,000
larger than the figures for ’94. As a rule.it is a bad
thing to see a large mass of foreclosed property on hand ;
foreclosures too often mean that the advances made on
mortgage have been so near to the full value, or so much
over the full value, of the properties that the borrowers
found it cheaper to let the society have them than to pay
up. Also, it is in some cases a much worse thing not to
see a large mass of foreclosed properties on hand ; in the
absence of any independent State audit, or any law
to regulate life assurance business, it is apt to mean that
the situation is too painful to be revealed, and that pro-
perties which are really abandoned to the society are
therefore left to figure among the mortgages, instead of
appearing under the proper heading. The Mutual of
Victoria’s expense rate remains practically the same as
last year (29 per cent, on premiums) and is still very high,
but amalgamation will doubtless reduce this immensely.
The foreclosed properties sold during the year have yielded
£lBOO more than the value put upon them, which cer-
tainly looks as if values had been cut down to bedrock
and any tendency to inflation strictly avoided.
The Mutual of Victoria, to all appearance, is a
very sound and solvent office that was crippled by exces-
sive competition, a high expense rate (partly caused by the
said competition), lack of energy, perhaps, in the manage-
ment, and unappreciated honesty. An office which, in
bad times, fully explains its position, writes off all losses
regardless of consequences, and cuts down its bonuses todo
so, is at a big disadvantage with the unthinking mass
compared with the one which conceals its losses, writes
up the paiper value of its assets and calls the imaginary
wealth thus created profit; which takes credit for interest
not received and never likely to be received and calls it
income ; which creates an imaginary surplus by estimat-
ing probable future interest too high or probable future
mortality too low ; and pays gorgeous bonuses out of the
imaginary funds thus created. And until the State fixes
the method of valuation, and values the assets and audits
the balance-sheet, the society which is too painfully
honest will be at a serious disadvantage compared to the
one which is mainly flashy and gorgeous and sensational.

Vio. State Land Bank i 3 putting another
£20,000 worth of 3 per cent, bonds on the market. This
will make £45,000 worth issued, and as the bank has
already granted loans to the extent of £45,000, and the
money for the new bonds won’t be available for some
weeks, and as the bank is receiving applications for loans
at the rate of 45 per week, and has a hundred or two
accumulated on hand, it deserves all possible credit as a
very calm and deliberate institution. Any bank which
was in a reasonable hurry, with such a business doing,
would dump £lOO,OOO worth of bonds on the market and
push things ahead.

A week or two ago, it was reported that the
second reconstruction of the A.J.S. Bank was practically
arranged, and that the solicitors were drawing up the
needful documents. No details of the rearrangement,
however, have yet been made public, and the state of the
share-market shows no revival of confidence in the
harassed institution. The shares (£7 10s. paid up) were
on offer last Saturday at 155., without attracting buyers.
A 5 per cent, dividend would mean 50 per cent, to the
buyer at present rates, but perhaps the 5 per cent, looks
too far off to be worth gambling about.
* * « *
The Queensland National Bank’s wonderful re-
construction has practically got through, and after this
almost anything in the way of reconstruction should be
possible. The institution is at present paying interest at
41 per cent, on £5,996,000 —equal to £269,820 per annum.
Under the new arrangement it is only to pay 3J per cent,
on £4,497,000—equal to £157,395 per annum—so that it
saves £112,000 a year interest off the depositors. It
claimed in its last balance-sheet that it only needs to save
£3200 a year to make ends meet, so the above, by its own
showing, is largely a gratuitous looting of the miserable
depositors who are cornered by the operation of the
Government’s prior claim, and have to agree to almost
anything that is offered them. The Q.N.8., by its new
arrangement, pays 3£ per cent, interest on three-
quarters of its deposits, and no interest at all on the
other quarter, so the real rate of interest is
only £2 12s. 6d. per cent. Also, the institution strikes a
truly ingenious way of owing money, and yet not
owing it. It is admittedly *2,163,446 to the bad—
if not more. The shareholders, who are morally
responsible for the whole of this, contribute £464,264
by writing off half their capital; £200,000 comes out
of other accounts; and the remaining £1,499,182 is
provided by writing off one-quarter of the deposits. The
bank admits that it owes this £1,499,182, and promises to
pay it by instalments out of the amount'saved in deposit
interest. But while it thus owes the whole £5,996,000 of
deposits, only £4,497,000 will seemingly appear as a debit
in the balance-sheet, and thus things will look beauti-
fully solvent—at least that is the apparent meaning of
manager Ralston’s explanation. It seems certain that
private deposits are to be treated on this basis at all events.
There is something so simple about the idea of making
things square by omitting a lot of liabilities that it is a
wonder the idea didn’t occur to anybody before.
Under the published scheme, which has appa-
rently been fully adopted, the Government deposit is to
be repaid by 1922. After that, the repayment of the
private deposits may commence, or it may not —there is
apparently no compulsion to repay the private deposits at
all. Out of the profits created at the expense of the de-
positors by the reduction of interest the bank keeps one-
quarter, and the other three-quarters are to be used in
repaying that £1,499,182 of deposits which the bank owes
but doesn’t apparently include aniong its liabilities. After
this is repaid, the whole £112,000 a year saved at the
expense of the depositors apparently goes to the
bank. In order to attract new business, new
depositors are to have a nominal priority, but
as the Government is really to rank as first creditor, and
the new private depositors only come in a bad second, the
priority isn’t so good as it looks. The Q.N.8., with a
saving of £112,000 a year, ought to be able to get straight
in the course of years, unless there are more revelations,
but the cast-iron assurance of the crowd which could
propose such a scheme as the above is an amazing circum-
stance.

* * * »
The London Bank of Australia is reported to
have its second reconstruction scheme practically fixed
up, barring a number of formalities that have yet to be
struggled with. The institution has already repaid 30
per cent, of its deposits ; another 5 per cent, is to be pay-
able in 1899 ; and similar instalments in 1902, 1904, and
1906 ; 30 per cent, is payable in 1915, and 20 per cent, in
1920. This is 13 years later than the date fixed by the ’93
agreement, which is a pretty large extension. Interest
arrangements are weirdly complicated. Up to 1900, de-
posits are to carry 3 per cent, interest ; then preference
shareholders are to get 5J4 per cent, dividend ; and out of
the remaining profits, if any, depositors are to get an-
other 1 per cent. This, however, isn’t to be paid them in
cash, but is to be set aside as a reserve to make up any
future shortage in the above-mentioned 3 per cent., and
if any of the reserve isn’t used for that purpose the
balance is to be divided among the depositors in 1915,
and 1920, or whenever the last payments are made.
This reserve has just three years in which to accumulate,
so it can’t get above £lOO,OOO. After 1900 depositors are to
get 3 per cent, interest; then preference shareholders get
5J per cent. ; and after that depositors get another 1 per
cent, in cash if the profits allow. The bank is not
allowed to pay its ordinary shareholders more than 2 per
cent, dividend until all depositors are paid off. This is a
beautifully circuitous arrangement, but the general tenor
of it is that the interest can’t exceed 4 per cent., whereas
it is now 4J, and as the deposits are £3,350,000 the Lon-
don Bank must save £16,750 a year. Also, as it can
reduce interest to 3 per cent, if required, it may save
about £50,000 a year. Last balance-sheet showed that,
after paying 5J per cent, to preference shareholders, it
was going to the bad at the rate of about £lO,OOO a year,
so the new complicated device leaves plenty of margin,
unless the fading away of the profits continues very
persistently. New deposits are to have a prior lien on the
bank’s assets.
• • * *
The Union Bank shows a profit of .£48,837 for
the half-year ending 31st August, 1896, after applying
£lO,OOO towards depreciation. This is £lO,OOO better
than either of the two previous half-years. A 5 percent,
dividend (£37,500) is paid, and the other £11,337 goes to
increase the available funds. The Union now boasts that
it has £4,641,000 in coin and bullion, and over £3,000,000
in readily realisable securities, against liabilities to the
public of a little over £20,000,000, so its position looks
about as strong as it well can be.

The Standard Fire and Marine Insurance Co.
(Maoriland) pays its usual 7J4 per cent, dividend with
some difficulty. The profits of the insurance business for
the year were £1679, and £4142 received as interest on
investments made £5821. The dividend absorbs £5625,
so the co. adds £196 to its funds. There is not much joy
in the insurance business at present. In 1893 the Stan-
dard realised t'4918 interest on investments; in ’96, with
larger funds, it earns £BOO less. Five years ago the profit
on the insurance business was £13,000 ; now it is difficult
to keep i't up to £l7OO, and in ’95 it got down as low as
£770. The irruption of the underwriting Goths and
Vandals from Europe has taken most of the glory out of
the business.

The Federal Coffee Palace (Melb.) takes advan-
tage of the new Companies Act to write off as lost £109,989
out of its £122,752 paid-up capital. The shares, which
were formerly paid up to 195., are now reckoned as 2s.
paid up only. This is about the worst Cold-Tea calamity
of the century.

It is a matter of much wonder to suburban pro-
perty-holders about Melbourne to find the property of
building societies invariably valued for rating quite 20
per cent, below that privately held. Some of the cases
are very glaring. When there are two houses exactly
alike in every particular in the same locality, one owned
by a building society and the other by an “ornery”
citizen, the former will be found to be valued anything
from 20 to 40 per cent, less than the latter. So far this
circumstance has escaped general attention, but Tin?
Bulletin brings it up as an interesting study for Melb.
property-owners who have no “influence” worth men-
tioning.

came round to my tenant the other day offering him half-
a-week’s rent to clear out into another house. Agents
get the first week’s rent on letting. The ramifications of
this swindle need only be hinted.”

The recent flotation of the Carbine Gold-Mining
Co. (Coolgardie) has a unique appearance. The vendors,
who “ refused to receive any monetary consideration for
the property,” were content to take £6OOO of working
canital for a sixth share. After spending “ close upon
£6OOO in developing the mine ” and being assured by the
manager “ that he has an enormous body of gold-bearing
stone available for treatment,” the fortunate holders
parted with a sixth share to the public instead of pro-
viding some more of their own good greed fora crushing
plant. The generosity of such speculators is touching.
They risk a considerable sum in proving the richness of
their claim, and when it is proved beyond “ all shadow of
doubt whatever ” they decide to let the public buy a slice
of the sure thing.

* • • •
There is gold—and gold. Some W.A. mines
sent large parcels (200, 300, 500 ounces) of gold to Melb.,
which was duly chronicled as sensational yields from
miraculous mines. Nothing in particular was said
about the value of the gold. Fine gold (i.e., ab-
solutely pure, never supplied by Nature the adul-
terator) is worth £4 ss. per ounce. This W.A.
“gold” fetched from 6d. (sixpence) to 2s. (two shillings)
per ounce. The proceeds of 200 oz. at 2s. per ounce
would not pay freight, insurance, bank charges, etc.,
from the field to Melb., but it reads fine in print. “ The
Plethora of Bullion reports a yield of 200 oz. from a crush-
ing of 10 tons.” A little thing like that enables the
directors to unload their shares on the mere public. The
game is not being played so boldly just now—but it is not
abandoned altogether. Large parcels of gold alloyed with
lead (the like of which Nature never produced unaided)
are now being received from the “Gilded West.” And
oh 1 the ingots are a genial shimmering yellow that
sparkles in the sun—just like gold. Add 33 per cent, of
lead to a 3oz. yield, and you bring it up prettily to a 4oz.
yield. The mining managers out west have struck the
philosopher’s stone—that turns lead to gold. They are
quite shocked when the bullion assayers, with unsympa-
thetic, unemotional acids, decide that the yield is worth
£3, perhaps only £2, an ounce. Many mining folk on
Westralian fields add to their splendid ignorance of
mining a pathetic ignorance of how to make auriferous
lead look like fine gold.
• * * •
Great Boulder, W.A. : fortnight, 792 tons,
25760 z. The latest from this mine is the discovery of a
rioh leader “of almost pure gold.” It is supposed to be
identical with the leader in the “jewel chamber,” where l
ever that is. Anyhow, 1200 oz. were picked out, and some
of the nuggets exhibited in a Kalgoorlie window. “The
public are much excited over the great future of the
Boulder and other mines of the locality.” Naturally !
They could hardly be expected to get excited over the
past of many Westralian mines.
A Queensland paper par. : “A wire from Kal-
goorlie says ‘ the Brisbane syndicate claim at Mt. Margaret
has been forfeited through neglect of the labor conditions.
An intending purchaser was within a few miles.’” The
last sentence was probably sent as a consolation, not
as humor.
* • * •
“Morning Star, Mt. Magnet, W.A., cleaned up
4540 z., being an average of 14dwt. to the ton, showing a
profit of £1400.” The profit end of it is probably ex-
aggerated. Manager Bryant has pulled this mine through
many troubles, and the Ballarat crowd owning it has
given a lesson to the Bull managers on the Murchison in
economy of working and management. The yield would
not be worth more than £1760, and to mine and mill 700
tons on the Murchison for £350 looks over-tall.
Iho Brisbane boom in Westralian mines has
gone right off. Queensland Norseman paids 5?d., .contri-
buting 2jd. ; Kalgoorlifes and Glenorehys paids 3Jd. and
4d. ; M‘Clay’s Welcome, 10Jd., lid.
* * * «
The world has received the wired assurance of
Randolph Bedford that “ there is nothing wrong with the
mines at Bulong (W.A.), that they are looking well—only
market operations.” Perhaps Mr. Bedford will be'good
enough to wire something of the same sort re the General
Gordon mine and pups, the shares and shareholders of
which have gone wrong and are very sick. Strange that
the market does not lose its reason concerning the
Boulder, Lake View, and Ivanhoe.
* * » •
Cable interruption last week, and as usual, Wes-
tralian stocks were very much depressed. Confidence
was only restored with the wire. The confidence appears
to be not in the mines, but in Bull, and it is the fear that
Bull may be selling that almost knocks the bottom out of
the W.A. market whenever the line is down.
» » • »
Coolgardie : “Some rich specimens of fine and
coarse gold were brought into town yesterday from the
Bendigo-Coolgardie mine. There is a battery on the pro-
perty, but ft is hung up owing to want of water.” Not a
word re the facts that this bat tery has been hung up over
two years for the want of gold, that thousands of shares
have been forfeited, and that over £20,000 have been sunk
in the show.
* * * •
Meeting of Dresden G.M. Co., Coolgardie, held
in Brisbane to consider a cable offer for the mine; con-
ditional, of course. Anyhow, it was unanimously decided
to accept. When mining men agree to sell, their
unanimity is wonderful. The mine is under exemption,
has more liabilities than shareholders are disposed to pay,
42,000 shares have been forfeited, and Bull is asked to
remit £3OO for working exes. Certainly the best thing to
do with a mine like that is to sell it. What may be
thought of the buyer is another matter.

* * * *
Mr. Leslie Jolly to The Bulletin, from Laun-
ceston, Tas. He your par., 13th March, on the
departed glory of the Lefroy mines, a line may in-
terest you. So far as proved on the field, all payable
stone runs out at 400 ft. from the surface. The stone
goes on down, the gold does not. Only two mines so far
have had the pluck to go to a depth. The New Pinafore
is down over 1100 ft., and sinking. The Volunteer over
1000 ft., and still sinking; both these mines will make or
mar the question of deep sinking on Lefroy. Each is
on a distinct and well-defined line of lode! Their records
to date : New Pinafore—capital called up, £460, dividends
paid, £72,000 ; Volunteer—capital called up, £2500, divi-
dends paid, £60,625, good enough to pay a few
calls to fi d out if the gold shoots make again.
The*countrv rock has not changed, the reefs look just the
same —everything is there, bar gold. No official records
before July, ’B6, hut from then to Dec., ’96, the Lefroy
field has produced 84,776 tons of quartz and 88,9190 z.
gold, valued at £355,676, and none of this gold has been
got below 400 ft. But provided we don t strike Tophet
first, we will keep on sinking and hoping.”
* * * *
West Golden Gate (Tas.) just floated with a list
of 28,000 at Is. each. But, so far as is known, there is
only one road to fortune thereabouts—through the Golden
Gate. As the latter is looking better than ever, the
name of the new float ought to be worth something for
scripping purposes.

persistently predict another boom in copper, by the way.

* * • *
Apropos of the gorge to celebrate'the opening of
the Lyell Co.'s railway, it is interesting to scan the list of
those invited (remarks an irate Bulletin correspondent).
Shareholders generally were ignored, but useful person-
ages whom it is advisable to propitiate were there in
grand array, and included Ms.P., other local big-wigs,
and last, but by no means least, Gov. Gourmandston. It
is also rumored that the go. pays all travelling expenses !
And why ?
• * • *
Last four weeks’ output from Mt. Lyell: ore
treated, 5667 tons, matte produced, 387 tons, containing
238 tons of copper, 1106 oz. gold, and 15,7440 z. silver. In
mining enterprise before production, this mine has no
equal in Australia. The railway cost £129,000 ; smelting
plant and other machinery, £135,000 ; mining and sun-
dries, £7o,ooo—total, £377,207.
* • » •
Gov. Macgregor’s three-years’ exclusion of
Chinese from the New Guinea goldfields is all right. And
N.Q diggers are reminding each other that, when a hun-
dred or more Chinese sought to invade Towalla goldfield
(near Cairns) on the very day when its three years ex-
pired, the whites simply rolled up with their gups and
warned off the pigtails. Although Cairns always stank of
Chinamen, never a Chinese has fossicked on Towalla
since. The Palmer experience was too recent and too bitter
for the Towalla whites.
• • • •
Mt. Morgan has paid dividend duty to the ex-
tent of £93,750 since its opening, and £6154 royalty.
Struck Oil, a place employing 200 men a short time ago,
is again deserted. The old story in the Crocodile district
—refractory ores. Mt. Champion is a small, but pro-
mising, concern that turned out 7J tons for 50oz. lately.

In another column appears the prospectus of the
Rutherglen and Carlyle Deep Leads Company, Ruther-
glen (Victoria). The property consists of 3600 acres of
alluvial ground, which has been proved by bores passing
through favorable strata and striking wash from 3ft. to
lift, in depth, in some of which gold was obtained.
The Rutherglen and Carlyle Proprietary G.M. Co.—the
vendors—retain 14,000 shares, fully paid up to £l, and
7000 contributing shares, paid up to 2s. 6d., in full pay-
ment for the property, the balance of 19,000 shares being
now offered to the public at Is. per share on application,
and Is. 6d. on allotment. The brokers are Messrs. Walter
and Harris, Messrs. Boyes and De Little, and Mr. W. O.
Bury, Melbourne ; Mr. Fred. Barker, Bendigo; Messrs.
Stoddart and Binnie, Ballarat; and Messrs. Wilkinson,
Harrison and Porter, Adelaide ; and Mr.,W. Grant Meu-
dell (Messrs. Meudell and Cameron) is legal manager, pro
tern. *

pear Bulletin,— lt may sadden the heart
of “Melb.” to know there is a Chinese mission
hall in Pitt-st., Sydney, close to Paddy’s market.
The other night I saw sever*! sleek and cherubic
Chows emerge, and behind them two girl-teachers.
One Celestial, more wily than the others, escorted
the ladies down street. He wore tan-shoes, a six-
penny nosegay, and his trousers had the accepted
Piccadilly fold at the ends. Evidently a love-
sick Chow.—T.D.

A CONVERT.
(Founded on Facts.)
Miss Zeal: “You come my mission class
Thursday night, Mr. Lung, learn be very good
man ? ”
Wun Lung : “No feah, Lung no be good man,
no savee.”
Miss Zeal : “ But you attend the mission one
time, long time. Why you leave Miss Stiggins’
class ?”
W? N Lung : “ Miss Stiggin too muchee skinny.
No like’m all a same bone.”
Miss Zeal (severely) : “What has Miss Stig-
gins’ figure to do with the truths of religion ? I
very much fear you no good Christian man, John.”
Wun Lung : “No feah Chlistian. Wha’ for ? ”

portrait for “ The Bulletin .”
Miss Zeal : “ Well, all I can say is that we
never buy vegetables from the heathen.”
Wun Lung : “ Huh ? ”
Miss Zeal : “We cannot deal with you, Mr.
Lung, unless you are converted to the true faith.”
Wun Lung: “ Me belive him baptize you buy
’m cabagee me, letucee me, callot ? ”
Miss Zeal : “Yes, John; you come my class.”
Wun Lung : “ You get’m other lady buy Wun
Lung cabagee, Wun Lung letucee, all a-same
Missee Blown, Missee Jackson, Missee Young ? ”
Miss Zeal : “Yes, John, all.”
Wun Lung: “Welly good, o ki; me believe
baptise, same Ilish.”
Miss Zeal : “You good man, Lung; you go
Heaven by and bye.”
Wun Lung : “You buy cabagee me. Missee
Young, Missee Jackson, Missee Blown buy
cabagee me, Lung baptise ? ”
Miss Zeal : “Yes, yes; all buy your vege-
table. ”
Wun Lung ( insinuatingly ) : “You buy tea
Lung? Missee Blown, Missee Missee
Young all buy tea Lung ? ”
Miss Zeal (dubiously) : “ Perhaps.”
Wun Lung ( making a bargain) : “ You, buy tea,
Lung believe blanky hell. ”

* * * »
The Cairns Chinese have been banqueting
again, to the unspeakable joy of the whites of that
sultry town. Various unspeakable soups, mysteri-
ous condiments of grasshopper flavor, sharks’
fins, stewed slugs and fried frog were present in
abundance —to say nothing of a weird, unspeak-
able stink as of sulphureous sheol. White guests
have much ado not to offend their pig-tailed hosts,
inasmuch as etiquette demands that the guest
should partake of every dish offered, and when
the number of dishes mounts up to 20 or 30 the
order is rather big—even for a Cairns man.
* * * *
The “ sassiety ” lady, who lost her balance
one night whilst attempting the Court curtsey in
“ Prisoner of Zenda,” and who subsequently
“ threw up her engagement,” has not retired
altogether from the dramatic profession The
fair artist is “ resting ”at present—on her laurels.
It may have been her laurels that she was resting
upon when the Ruritanian courtiers rushed to
assist her train-entangled form from the Princess’s
stage.
♦ * * •
Somebody writes from Sunbury (Vic.) to
protest against the assertion that Baronet Clarke
and family are objects of local worship. At the
same time the writer admits that the neighbor-
hood is infested by marriageable Ethels and full-
grown Ruperts, christened after the infant
Clarkes of former days. This is a useful thing to
know. When you see a nice girl, with a yearning
maidenly expression of countenance, leaning over
the garden-gate of a Sunbury villa residence, you
just rush up to the vision and whisper “Ethel! ”
and ten chances to one you’ll .be right.

Matrimony in three acts—l. Coalition.
11. Collision. 111. Collusion.
Rockhamptonites are
blowing their trumpets
at present over the great
future generally predicted
for gifted Florence
Schmidt, of whom an
excellent portrait, speci-
ally taken by Falk for
The Bulletin, is printed
herewith. And in this
case their confidence is
probably not misplaced.
Before she left the Tropics
to study in Sydney, and
gladden the heart of her
patient and able tutor,
Steffani, the cheerful,
practical young Queens-
lander constantly acted
and sang, as if to the
manner born, with a
fashionable local operatic
company of amateurs.
With her in the leading
role, there was never any
trouble in keeping good
old Gov. Norman awake,
at local entertainments
arranged in his honor,
on his occasional visits
to Rockhampton. And
that says volumes for
Miss Schmidt’s grace and
go. At an earlier period,
when the debutante of
to-day wore short frocks,
she was a most pro-
mising juvenile pianiste,
and, at children’s balls
and such-like, was always
conspicuous as the nimble
and graceful leader of
minuets, gavottes, or
cotillons. Thenherfamily
history is all in her favor,
for though neither her
deliberate German father
nor her buoyant Irish
mother ever laid claim
to unusual musical ability
their children, without
exception, are gifted in
that respect. I lorence, the youngest and only un-
married daughter, is a well-grown, shapely girl, not
pretty, perhaps, but with charming manners, and
hair a few shades warmer than Ada Crossley’s. The
languid, sticky climate of Rockhampton is ap-
parently not unfavorable to the development of
musical talent, though so far only the subject of
this paragraph and Mary Bourcieault have ven-
tured to think of a public career.

This month the Gothenburg system of
selling liquor has been inaugurated at Renmark
irrigation colony (S.A.) previously run on prohi-
bitionist lines. The Act authorising the new
departure was passed last session,, and under it a
committee manages the hotel on behalf of the
town, the profits being devoted to objects ap-
proved by the Treasurer,

Maorilanders are
just now so inflated with
pride as to be abso-
lutely unfit for any-
body to associate with.
M.L. has just pro-
duced the meanest man
going; hence the infla-
tion. This person-
adopted an orphan niece,
and for five years kept
her hard at work on his
farm carting earth,
grooming the horse, dig-
gingpost-holes, andother-
wise saving the cost of
a laborer. She got no
wages, and was flogged
regularly to keep her up
to her work. At the
age of 19 the niece infor-
mally resigned her billet,
and bolted with her world-
ly possessions, which com-
prised one pair of shoes,
a skirt, a blouse, and one
or two other trifles ; she.
bolted with them on, as
they were the only ones
she had, and the outfit
didn’t include stockings,
as the old man dis-
couraged such uncalled-
for luxury. Then the
uncle had her arrested,
and demanded that she
should be severely pun-
ished for embezzling the
skirt and shoes. When
the Bench declined, he
requested that the skirt
and the other things
should be returned to
him on the spot, which
would have left the girl
with practically nothing
on. Failing that also,
he retired in high dud-
geon. Maoriland is
thinking of sending its
mean man to England
as a rare exhibit, to take
part in the rejoicings on
Victoria’s 60th anniver-
sary It thinks that something should be done to
celebrate sucn monumental meanness

A Melbourne girl-wife left
her home the other day and
stepped gently into the Yarra,
whence two or three interfering
persons promptly fished her out
and removed her to the hospital.
Then the usual large policeman
clumped seriously to her resi-
dence in S. Melb. and informed
her husband, and it was agreed
by all parties that “no reason
could be assigned for the rash
act.” Great Scott ! Isn’t the
prospect of a life in clamorous,
crowded, muddy, bricky South
Melbourne enough to account
.for any act ? Think of being 23
years of age, and sentenced to
iook at the brick wall opposite,
and to open the grimy front
door to the Chinese vegetable-
man, and to have the stairs
opening straight out of the
dining-room for life ! Also,
think of a life spent in getting
21bs. of sugar from the corner
grocery, and trying to keep the
dust off the threepenny china
ornament on the mantelpiece,
and gathering up the brown-
paper that blows into the yard,
and occasionally speaking over
the fence to Liza Ann next door !
If there was any poetic senti-
ment in the police force, it
would know exactly why a South
Melbourne woman wants to com-
mit suicide, and would take her
to the hospital reverently, and
get her the usual seven days in
a sympathetic spirit when she is
sufficiently recovered to get
anything. The fact of having
the bedroom stairs going right out of the dining-
room is one of those tragedies whose cheap,
sordid, dull-brown unpicturesqueness eat into
the inmost consciousness of the woman with a
soul.
* » * *
The “great” Sydney dailies watch each
other like jealous cats. The Telegraph recently
gave prominence to Newcastle news by putting it
under a special heading like those given to inter-
provincial paragraphs. The Herald followed
suit, and also set three men and a boy to work up
news-items in the coal city. Now one of the
evening papers likewise devotes a separate column
to Newcastle matters. What the Newcastle daily
has to say is probably unprintable.
Both theD.T. and S.M. Herald now lay them-
selves out extensively on country news, with the
result that “mullock” which would only be
inserted at ad vertising rates in the average country
sheet takes precedence of metropolitan news.
Samples :
COOLAMON, Monday.
The convent school just established here opened to-day
with a good attendance of pupils.
GOSFORD, Monday.
A library has been established in connection with the
local public school.
A telephone is to be placed in the local post-office. The
wire has already been fixed in position between the rail-
way-station and the telegraph-office.
COROWA, Tuesday.
Messrs. Piggin and Co. bought 500 bags of wheat to-day
at 4s. Id. The same firm sold to-day 50 draught horses
up to £35 a head.
It might also be noted that “John Smith’s bull-
calf might have broken its leg to-day if it had
fallen into the hole at the corner of Mrs. Smith’s
paddock.”
* * * *
There was a noticeable absence of Sydney
cabbies at the farewells to their generous patron,
Primate Smith. “In journeyings (too) often ”
at a bob a time.
Apropos, a certain very “adjacent ” ecclesiastic,
after handing up a shilling through the roof-hole
at the end of a journey, sang out to cabby (whose
horse suddenly became fractious and kept on the
jump): “My good man, cannot you keep your
horse quiet until I alight?” “No,” said the
cabby, “it ken’t be done. It’s these shillin’ fares
as worries ’im.”
“In journeyings often.” Where the circus is
there will the Premiers and Primates be gathered
together.
* * * *
Australian Primate Saumarez Smith, on
departure from Sydney for England, the other day,
said “ he supposed he might, without affectation,
apply phrases of the great missionary apostle to
his own case, ‘ In labors abundant, in journeyings
often,”’ &c. Quite true, though there are some
little differences between the two cases. For in-
stance, Paul journeyed “in thirst,” whereas the
Right Rev. Saumarez no doubt travels with a
flask. Paul travelled “in weariness ; ” the Pri-
mate goes in carriages and bob cabs; Paul “in
watchings,” and Smith in sleeping-cars. Paul
was flogged by, whereas St. Smith dines with, the
Governor. Where Paul travelled “in naked-
ness,” his right reverence sports apron and
breeches. The strongest parallel between the
pair is to be found in the facts that Paul journeyed
“among heathens” and the Primate among
beastly colonials.

Shriekingly-funny social item from Melb.
Argus :
During the course of a meeting in the Temperance-hall
last night of women workers, who are engaged in the for-
mation of an association on trade-union lines, Miss Good-
win presented Mrs. S. Mauger with a silver teapot and a.
biscuit-barrel as a recompense for the loss of her husband’s
company through his attention to matters connected with
the Factories and Shops Act.
Poor consolation, but most excellent humor.

Mr. and Mrs. de Byke are not mad, neither are they suffering from stringhalt. But they have just
accomplished a record tour on their tandem, and have been unable as yet to discard the postures
acquired in propelling that exhilarating machine. Hence the sensation.

Cabinet in any other joint transaction.
* * • *
The contrast in the mannerin which our great Christian
dailies report the several killings done by the Christians
in Eastern Europe, and the horrible massacres perpe-
trated by the Turk is most gratifying. A spirit of chas-
tened melancholy broods over the former, whilst a lurid
atmosphere surrounds the latter, an atmosphere full of
crimson foam, shrieks, and human fragments :

Apropos of a question put by The Bulle-
tin a week or two ago, if N.S.W. Judge Gibson
did the other day give a man three years for
stealing a tin of baking powder ; Mr. Gibson has
as yet taken no steps to contradict the printed
report that, in awarding this monstrous penalty,
he explained that he was determined to deal
severely with prisoner, on the ground that instead
of pleading guilty in the magistrate’s court, he had
got himself sent before a jury (as was his legal
right!), and put the country to an expense
of something like =£2o. The Bulletin now wants
to know whether the prisoner had a record as an
incorrigible criminal, or whether, as rumored, he
Was a harmless and ordinary sort of person who,
under the circumstances, should (taking typical
Australian police-court sentences as a criterion)
have got a day’s or a week’s gaol—or been simply
fined and cautioned. Three years for a tin of
baking-powder ! There has been nothing like this
since men were hanged for the larceny of forty-one
shillings.
* * * *
A very, very well-known sport is said to
be deeply grieved at his wife’s indisposition to go
for a decree nisi. Since the lady cast him off for
ever, he has made a reasonable show of wanting
to be led astray, without much success. Candi-
dates for platonic friendship are numerous enough.
The sighing grass-widower has found lots of girls
ready to ogle him, drive with him, and even dine
alone in a private room with him, but the maiden
who will go partners in a grande passion and bear
him away to the Blue Mountains, or even to
Healesville, from Saturday to Monday, is not yet
forthcoming. Wanted a co-respondent tall,
handsome, fond of horses, and warm in tempera-
ment. Here is a first-class billet going begging.

* * * *
The tick is rapidly disappearing from the
Far North. In his place, myriads of beetles and
grasshoppers have entered the field, the latter
having developed a most remarkable liking for
human clothing. Also, the multitudinous fly is
slightly more übiquitous and multitudinous than
ever, and the sausage-machine scoops them in in
myriads. “Downy” Northerners never touch a
sausage in fly-season.
The Turkish beast, with bloody hands
And clotted clothes, in slaughter
Wades to his knees, and fills the lands
With tigerish howls whilst he commands
The death of some pale daughter.
He tears the shrieking babe in twain
Before its mangled mother;
Then, throned above a heap of slain,
He gloats upon the hellish pain
Of slack-baked son or brother.
The Christian kills in sober wise—
Disguising well his club, he
Requests the wife who bleeding lies
To please avert her streaming eyes
Before he brains her hubby ;
And so, although he kills his foes,
And still to slay is lusting,
And burns and shoots whole crowds in rows,
As every “ Keristian ” paper shows
He’s never quite disgusting.

fiven their royal patronage to Ed. Pinaud, of
’aris, but never so much as lately ; his perfumes

Europe. The biggest hits of Ed. Pinaud have

and most delicate perfumes ever made; in fact,
they are known everywhere as the quintessences
of the richest flowers grown in France, and are
sure to be rushed by everybody.*

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, March 23,1897.
Mr Dear Mtee—
At the farewell concert given by Herr Kretsch-
inann to his pupil, the clever Yvonne Leverrier,
last week, Sydney Town Hall was packed by a
wildly-applauding audience. Large quantities of
flowers were handed up to the girlish performer,
cheers given for everybody connected with the
show, handkerchiefs waved, and all was enthu-
siasm. It was a free show. And now, because of
this display, and because the four housand or so
invited listeners sat through a long and classical
, programme, including a Rubinstein concerto and
a Schumann ditto without turning restive, every-
body is congratulating everybody else on the love
for the musical art prevailing locally. Miss
Leverrier’s crashing touch often wants tone—she
plays more from her head than from her heart.
When she returns with developed qualities, and
: gives a concert which the cambric-wavers will each
pay the necessary entrance-fee for, then it will be
time to crow over the great local music-loving
public and its appreciation of native talent.
The Saturday night meeting of the League of
Wheelmen’s carnival showed that there is a
demand here for open-air evening amusement. It
was gay and bright to see the trams disgorging
multitudes at the Cricket Ground gates, and to
hear the band playing in front of the lighted-up
stand. All the stands were filled with a crowd
surprisingly dressy, considering it was a night
show. Red gleamed everywhere, the tint was
universal.
Theatrical managers must feel warmly about
Town Hall concerts, bicycle races, and other
attractions. Depressingly thin dress-circles greet
all performers’ eyes just now, and there are
i rumors of some managements dropping much coin
weekly, except at Her Majesty’s, where the long
tin-trumpets quaver Egyptianly to satisfactory
houses, and Bathurst rolls his big blue eyes
upon many juveniles who seem to enjoy the out-
of-season panto. The children devote much
critical attention to the animal procession, and
generally give first place for realism to the camels.
But, then, anything is like a camel—it is such a
miscellaneous animal.
Twenty-six loving couples took advantage of the
day, like Sam Weller’s “gentleman in difficul-
ties,” and were married at St. Mary’s Cathedral
on “the Seventeenth of Ireland.” Lenten re-
strictions are removed on 17th March, and College-
street was a busy matrimonial mart last Wednes-
day in consequence. The bridals were of all
degrees of display—a gorgeous procession with
youthful pages was the most interesting to the
wedding-haunting women who hung around the
portals nearly all day, and didn’t seem to tire of a
spectacle which must have become as monotonous
as a ten-mile bicyele-race. Over two dozen
weddings mean hard work in one day’s business
hours, and several official solemnisers were occupied
in knot-tying with quickness and despatch.
The cinematographe is an optical barrel-organ,
and grinds out pictures instead of tunes. A small
crowd of press-folk was accorded a private view of
yet another cine, on Thursday afternoon at the
Crown Studio, in George-street. A mountain-
gorge unrolled on the screen as it was snapshotted
from a moving train was the newest view shown.
A burning-building scene gave some real effects
in the way of hose played on the smoke ; and the
same red-headed dancer made familiar by other
local exhibitions smiled and shook her flounces
under changing lime-light. Had the boy at the
piano only restrained his ardor in the “ Poet and
Peasant ” overture, the exhibition, which included
the x-rays, kinetoseopic and other scientific toys,
would have been an unalloyed success.
The Lady Hampden, a swagger new ferry-boat
belonging to the Balmain N.F. Co., was lent to
the Engineering Association on Thursday evening
last for a moonlight harbor-trip, It was a most
hilarious outing. So many guests crowded on
board that the white-coated waiters had hardly
room to slide their trays about. There was a con-
cert by male voices and a lively band, and some
lightning-sketches by Mr. Souter, and a loud pop-
ping of soda-water corks all the time. On the re-
turn journey (there was no destination—the ex-
pedition just went there and back again) much
speeohifying took place, and the whole eo., being
mostly engineers and named Mac, sang “ Auld
Lang Syne ” with the fervor of a religious cere-
mony, and shook hands all round, and an impas-
sioned gentleman arose and thanked God for the
existence of the B.N.F. Co.
“ A Night Out,” the piece in which Paulton will
first appear in Australia, is a translation of the
French farce “ L’Hotel du Libre Echange,”
which had a long run at a Parisian Boulevard
theatre (Nouveautes) two years ago. Almost
every manager in London went over to have a
look at it for business purposes, and G. R. Sims
boomed it for tourists by writing it down in the
Referee as too shocking even for Paris ; he con-
sidered it untranslatable, and was, consequently,
aggrieved. It was given up as hopeless, until
Seymour Hicks (husband of Ellaline Terriss) Eng-
lished it presentably by altering the leading
motive completely. In French it was steep, but
funny, and Mme. Pailleron, nice, though naughty,
wore the first chiffon blouse I ever saw. They
were a novelty two years ago.
W. Impett, of Wellington (Maoriland), has
written the “ Chimes Gavotte.” It has gavotted
merrily into a 4th edition on the notes of a bell-
peal. The style suggests that the composer is an
organist. “ Somewhere,” song by Sydney Turn-
bull, only wants a girl with tow hair and a music-
hall manner to sing it into success as a “ serio ”
ballad in a variety first-part, where it should have
a long and useful career. But what is the sense of
Shall I find her ’neath the wave,
Or in some misty cave ?
Is author Bernays going to put on a diving-suit
and search for his love at the bottom of the
’arbor ?
Tasmania has a Governor who knows how to
make the best of both worlds. The Mt. Lyell Co.
invited him to a banquet at Strahan last Friday,
but Gov. Gourmandston, as a devout R.C., had
scruples about feasting on a Friday, and a Lenten
one at that. He begged the spread-givers to
hurry up the banquet and have it the day before —
not the day afterwards, mind you.
The Austral left the Quay last Saturday with
the usual crowd of farewelling friends on the
wharf and a large deck cargo of flowers on board.
This ever-increasing habit of handing nosegays to
departing globe-trotters is solely a first-saloon
attention. The most distinguished passengers on
Saturday’s boat were travelling second —William
Michael Rossetti and his daughter Helen, whose
stay here was curtailed by quarantine. Miss
Rossetti gave her friends some lively grumbles on
the horrors of detention and its dulling effects on
the mind, though it didn’t depress hers very much.
When toney dames and damsels meet to take,
what in Dr. Johnson’s day was called “ a dish of
tea ” together, French is fast becoming the fashion-
able tongue in which to chatter recitations and
sing ballads. Those who pretend they speak the
language watch the performer’s face and smile or
sigh accordingly, while those who don’t pretend
sit in solemn silence in a dull, dark drawing-room
and wish it were bedtime. A Sydneyite was
lately taken to one of these mild foreign dissipa-
tions by his wife, who enacted a bereaved widow’s
part in a playlet. Her accent and manner were
quite perfect, and next Saturday hubby had the
pleasure of reading in social columns that his wife
“ played the widow to perfection.”
“Nemesis’’was played the second night to a
d c. composed almost entirely of show people.
Myra Kemble, J. MacMahon, Meta and Lala
Pelham, the young Bracys, and others were there,
and all seemed to wear the same perplexed ex-
pression, wondering why the piece doesn’t take
better. The crowded audiences that stay away
from the Lyceum would feel glad if once they
were inside, but apparently no attraction is strong
enough to induce them to come in and conceal
those yellow plush chair-backs. The dressing is
all new and bright—there is a tradition that Zidore
de Filoselle and his friends should wear stage boy
clothes. But if the whole piece were put into a
modern dress, and a topical song or two thrown
in, it would go like a Gaiety show. Nina
Osborne k la Florence Lloyd, and the beach-
loungers and belles in fashionable toggery, would
bring the show up to the modernity of Whelan’s
imitations, for which he gets vociferous encores.
At the distribution of medals and certificates to
the Sydney College of Music, on Monday last, so
many girls and women attended that the meeting
overfilled the vestibule and had to be proceeded
with in the big hall. Strings of girls, big, little
and middle-sized, streamed to the platform to get
the awards. In the throng three or four boys
shamefacedly shuffled up. Instructors are some-
times masculine —pupils rarely. Music is looked
at as a girl’s game. Very few of the girls walked
well, they strutted, or waddled, or crept, or jerked
along. And some of them not yet in their teens
had waists !
The ladies’ reading-room at the School of Arts
is at the end of a long passage, in the most
secluded part of the building, and contains half-a-
dozen Queens and other fatuous fashion journals,
and some local weeklies. It is not an enticing
room to read in, so that the lady members will
not be likely to grumble much if, as is proposed,
the committee turn it into a billiard - room.
Some prehistoric prejudice exists about allowing
men and women to take their periodic in-
stalments of literature in the same room,
so the lecture - room will be allotted to
ladies, and the male members will Continue to
have the run of all the new journals and maga-
zines, while in the ladies’ room will be dumped
the same old “ ladies’papers.” In all up-to-date
libraries women sit and read together with men.
The silly distinction of penning off the sexes is as
prudish as the old maid’s arrangement of her
library, which contained separate shelves for male
and female authors. At present, if a woman
wanders into the School of Arts general reading-
room, she attracts as much surprised attention as if
a dodo had fluttered in among the placid pabulum
absorbers.
Nicholson and Co. issue their new music cata-
logue with an up-to-date artistic cover. The list
guides the intending singer through every phase
of vocal sentiment in all the most sought-after
songs, from “The Lost Chotd ” to “The Tin Gee-
Gee,” and a range of piano pieces which takes in
sonatas marked “ very dif.,” as well as the “ Bal-
lyhooley,” is also entirely comprehensive.
The Edison Electric Parlor, in Pitt-street, has
an attractive window illuminated with bulbous
incandescent lights, and inside the hall a bewil-
dering variety of shows. The. cin. is in good
working-order, and, like others of the same,
exhibits chiefly prancing horses and dancing girls ;
applause is always given more to legs—equine and
human than to any stationary objects on the
screen. The X-ray apparatus is weird and creepy,
and relentlessly shows up one’s bony structure. If
it only had been invented in Svengali’s time, he
could have gloated over Trilby’s “ beautiful bones”
by the fluorescent light. All the contents of a
purse can be revealed through the outside leather
and a thick wooden plank. A money-bag, which
I saw myself, laid bare 6d. in silver, two coppers,
and three J nibs, bathed in pale-green luminosity.
Of course, it was a woman’s purse.
An Australian Premier’s wife, preparing to de"
part for the Diamond Jubilee, alleges as the
reason for her anxiety to accept the Royal invite,
that “ the Royal family are expecting us, and will
be so disappointed if we don’t go.” Under the
circumstances, it will not be surprising if a few of
the kawlinies never see their Premiers and
families again, for extra expenses at the Hotel
Cecil will run high, and when the “Premiers of
Old England’s dependencies ” are handed in the
hotel-bill for board and lodging for Mrs. Premier
and suite the total will be alarming. The turbot
and fizz they will so gleefully absorb, under the
impression that the British taxpayer is shouting,
will all be carefully noted in their accounts and
presented to H.M.’s “ paying guests ” as they
leave.
Some local musicians are jibbing at the “com-
plimentary concert” nuisance, which deprives
them of many an evening’s fee. One professional
fiddler counted up his gratis appearances last
year as close on four score, and then, added to this,
he often has a bundle of tickets handed him to sell
for the entertainment. Philosophical professionals
accede to these requests and count the advt. as an
asset, but most of them are agreed that the amount
of gratuitous warbling and strumming required of
them is altogether excessive.
A divorced man comes into town every morning
to business on the cable-oar. One day lie confided
his sorrows to an acquaintance sitting next him
on the dummy. “ It's the way Fate has of rub-
bing it in,” he complained, “ that I should stop
every day exactly opposite the door of the
Divorce-court.” “ What’s wrong with sitting the
other side of the dummy?” asked the friend.
“Never thought of it before,” murmured the dis-
united one—who at once stepped around, and now
rides always with his back to the scene of his dis-
ruption.
A ponderous, but distinctly Nice, person, who
has quite lately taken to learn the bike, astonished
an afternoon tea-party by arriving in its midst
in correctly-cut habit, announcing that she had
travelled the quarter of a mile from her home per
wheel “ after only four lessons, my dear, and I’m
not a bit heated either.” Another arrival, who
had walked unbeknownst behind the portly
cyclist, murmured mysteriously to her next neigh-
bour: “ She is ungratefully suppressing her push-
ing power,” and the two friends, leaving shortly
after, passed on the lawn a muscular boy lying on
the grass panting and prostrate.

“ Melbourne’s pluckiest woman knicker-cham-
pion now admits that cycling in aught but skirts
is a social impossibility. She regrets it. Rode in
‘ rationals ’as long as she could, and still holds
that they are the only sensible clothes for cycling
purposes. But bloomers have been ballooned out
of possibility by ridicule, and by their use by the
socially objectionable. So says this lady, who
ought to be an authority, having ridden in
knickers and skirts of nearly every invented pat-
tern.

parent nowadays in a one-time slim and famous
hunting spinster, who ‘ settled down ’ a year or
two ago. This double-chinned heroine is settling
down so rapidly that her favorite ’orse—if she
means to hunt next season —will probably find its
burden of life greater than it can bear.

also looks very well in cream satm and a tiara
like a gas-lit shop sign. The ill-fated Dacres gave
Miss R. her first theatrical engagement, and
during it she went through some exciting and
turbulent experiences.

to learn that an extremely high-toned and flirt-
some bachelor has been married secretly for several
years past—to a lady of uncertain English and
age. The painful fact was revealed by the excur-
sion into stagedom which several unwitting
damsels lately made ; in that strange realm many
secrets were brought to light, and many little
