“ I hear that a mainland gentleman who lately
took unto his bosom a gilded Tasmanienne had
an experience with Slowbart Customs officials.
Amongst the ’groom’s presents to the bride-elect
was included a valuable diamond necklace. The
Customs people were on the alert, and when the
wedding-presents were announced in the local
press, they arrived on the scene with an enquiry
as to when and how the necklace came. The
finale was that the newly-made hubby had to pay
■£sio duty.
“The composure with which Mrs. Maesmore
Morris, the much-photoed amateur, plays her part
in ‘Two Little Vagabonds,’ is a really remarkable
illustration of Australian ‘cheek,’ or rather self-
esteem. One hears experienced actors speak of
their early struggles to stand motionless, at per-
fect ease, upon the stage. Mrs. Morris, un-
troubled by nervousness, finds no difficulty in
being cool as a cucumber—not a tremor of the
voice, or twitch of the hand, suggests the least
misgiving as to her ability to carry out a few
simple instructions. Up to a certain point, and
under the necessarily calm conditions, there is no
visible difference between cold, natural ‘ cheek ’
and the art which conceals art. People are already
saying that the pretty lady will ‘ do well ’ on the
stage. Well, so she will, as long as there is no-
thing in particular to be done.
“R.C. Archbishop Carr is rousing his Melb.
flock to some more zealous enthusiasm re the com-
pletion of St. Patrick’s Cathedral. On a recent
Sunday, in all the Catholio churches of the arch-
diocese, his priests read a statement of the pro-
gress made, the money expended, the co-operative
efforts and benevolence of the Catholic body, the
chief priest’s own personal solicitations among
the people, and so on. Archbishop Carr’s heart’s
desire is to get the big building completed and
return to headquarters in Dublin. The event
stands a reasonable chance of coming off in No-
vember, when, in the words of H.G., ‘ for
beauty, solidity, and majesty,’ St. Pathrick’s
latest will rank as the finest cathedral in Aus-
tralia. After which a Cardinalship may be the
reward.
“ Local sensation-seekers have just narrowly
escaped a breach-of-promise case. The defendant,
who is wealthy and well known among smart folk’
shrank from the publicity of a trial and settled the
affair out of court by paying five hundred as a
salve to the lady's wounded feelings.”
To hear of John Chinaman’s gifts and graces
(especially his gifts) one has only to visit the
mission-schools and Bible-classes conducted by
young ladies in Lilly Boulke-street and Cabbagee
r Oll. After-hours the zealous teachers can
talk of nothing but ‘my John,’ his fluent reading
his tendency to sing in tune. Then there are
ecstasies over the offerings from grateful converts
—silk handkerchiefs (why not silk stockings’)
lacquered trays, packets of candied ginger One
provident damsel has stored all hers up with a
view to future pecuniary profit.
“Every year the Chings give their teachers a
tea. First time they employed a Chinese caterer
but were quick to notice the poor appetites of
their guests. Now they patronise a European
confectioner, but reserve to themselves the privi
lege of bringing along the ginger, chow-chow and
nuts. Sometimes the fair missionaries quarrel
over the merits of their respective scholars
One boaster cracked up her class unduly at the
teachers’ conference, so a colleague rose and
told the following story (gleaned from her
own John ) at the expense of the braggart’s
pet pupil. The latter bought a dog, she said
from a European market-gardener and gave 255!
for it because the seller declared it was so marvel
lously intelligent. Whenever you said ‘Go inside ’
it did so, an 1 at the order ‘Go outside! ’ its
obedience v, as no less prompt. The purchaser led
the animal home and took it into his hut full 0 f
kerosene-tins and rubbish. Then, not being very
strong in English, he remarked affably ‘Go in si ’
The brute took no notice—perhaps because it was
inside already—so its master tried ‘Go out si'’
with no better result. Then he began to lose faith
and jabbered at lightning speed, though in soft
non-emphatic tones, unheeded by sleepy Toby’
‘Go in si, go out si! go in si ! go out si ! go any
si you li ! You one pound five silling bluggie
letchie ! ’ Naturally the zealous teacher resented
this anecdote, which was further condemned y
the horrified hearers as ‘ most unedifying ! ’ J
“ Apropos of Chinese cult, it’s not only goody-
goody girls that pamper Ah Sam. On various
stations out back the celestial cook is as pleased as
Punch when the mistress of the house goes into
the kitchen with her young lady visitors to bake
cakes. At such times old ‘ slant-eye ’ lays himself
out for fun, dips his fingers into the dabblers’
dough, chases the younger girls round the table,
smears their cheeks with flour, and plays up any-
how.
“At one establishment the pig-tailed gar-
dener rushed into the kitchen one day with bronze
gauntlets on his hands and attacked his compat-
riot, the cook, with a lacquer sword. The latter
a sufferet - from chicken-heart, promptly gave
notice, and his assailant succeeded to his sauce-
pans. Soon, however, the station-proprietor got
a 'letter from a Chinese society in Sydney saying
that the assaulted Ah Curry was related to some
Mongol magnate and blaming the employer
severely for not taking better care of his servants.
At this news the supplanter packed up his swag
and departed, hoping by prompt submission to
avert the threatened vengeance of his countrymen.
-Then the evicted tenant, who had been biding his
time in the nearest township, returned triumph-
antly to his kitchen.

the London boards, where, if beauty and brains
have any value, she should make a name for her
self.

Byrnes goes to England chiefly to mind
Nevermind Nelson, and see that he isn’t bothered
between drinks. In responding to the toast of
his health before leaving Brisbane, Byrnes referred
to the rumor that he did not intend to return to
Queensland. He said the rumor was too absurd
for a serious denial. The Bulletin smiles at the
idea of any man not returning to a country which
pays him as Byrnes is paid and lets him do with
impunity the things that Byrnes is allowed to do
with impunity.
Just when the press organs of right-think-
ing opinion have announced that the “larrikin
pushes ” of Sydney must be “ put down ” anew
and an officer high in the police department ”
has expressed the opinion that nothing but the
lash would do the “ putting down ” effectually, one
police officer is committed for trial on a charge of
brutal assault upon an unfortunate man, just out
of hospital, sleeping in the Domain, while another is
alleged to have stood by and looked on ; and Chief
Justice Darley, after hearing a case in the Supreme
Court in which a third police-officer was con-
victed of assault and battery, said that—
A most gross outrage was perpetrated on these two
young men (the complainants). The police had an oppor-
tunity for apologising for the outrage ; but, instead of
doing that, they sent in reports which the jury by their
verdict had found to be false, and that these young men
about whom these insulting reports were made, who were
of good character, and against whom not one word could
be said, had been grossly outraged. He did not now in-
tend to take the very severe course which at one time
passed through his mind as being his duty to adopt; but
the conduct not only of the police, but the police authori-
ties, was most unwarrantable.
Not only the police, but the police authorities ”
—probably including the officer “ high in the ser-
vice who clings so fondly to the lash—are con-
nected in his Honor’s severe condemnation. And
be it noted that none of the three officers above re-
ferred to is an ordinary constable. One is a
senior-constable, two are senior-sergeants. Ex-
cluding from consideration and comment the case
first mentioned, of which the prima-facie aspect
may or may not be borne out, there is in
several recent conflicts between civilians and
the police more than sufficient warrant for
the assertion that some of the latter, presuming
on the thick-and-thin support of several of the city
magistrates and their own superior officers, are
accustomed to tyrannise over the poorer classes,
who are in no condition to resent ill-usage. It is
°P e ? indeed, whether the “ police
P^ s “’ ” with its outrages unnoticed by a purblind
officialdom, is not a greater menace to law and
order and the public sense of justice than any
larrikin push ” which ever existed.

Can’t Rob this Safe.
Hardly a week passes but what some audaciously
successful safe robbery is announced ; and size,
make, or strength does not seem to baffle safe
theives. The only safe that has resisted all efforts
to lower its precedence, depreciate its value, or ro]b
it of its enviable reputation, is Warner’s “ Safe ”
Cure. It is positively proof against all diseases of
the kidneys and liver. Mr. P. J. Heatherington,
Edwards River Hotel, Deniliquin, N.S.W., writes:
“ It affords me a pleasure to be in a position to
acknowledge the benefit I have derived from the
use of Warner’s ‘ Safe ’ Cure. I had for a long time
suffered from kidney and liver disease, which
caused intense pain in the back and sides, for
which I had tried several of the leading doctors
both in Sydney and Melbourne, but without any
permanent relief ; so I resolved to give Warner a
‘ Safe ’ Cure a trial, and after taking eight bottles
I am thankful to say I have quite recovered my
former good health.’’ This safe is a safe worthy
the name. It protects life, which is more im-
portant than property, and those who rely upon it
are never robbed of good health. The cost of a
trial bottle, 2s. 9d., is within the reach of all.*

“Tom” Bent, ex-Speaker and boss boomer,
was in the recent Warmambool (Vic.) railway
smash. He did not “experience any shock.
Pooh! After all the smashes “Tommy” has
been in he was not likely to be upset by a little
thing like that.
Old Lord Justice Kay, whose death was
reported by cable la t week, had, when at the bar,
one of the richest practices of the century. Judges
used to pay such deference to his learning that it
used to be remarked that, when he was in a case,
equity was spelt with a Kay.
Died, at Ballan, Vic., the other day, Peter
Tilling, aged 76, original discover and first worker
of the famous Egerton reef, which he and a couple
of mates sold for a song. The mine subsequently
made the fortunes of dozens of Ballarat men,
including the late Martin Loughlin and not a few
lawyers.
Of the 50 members at Adelaide Federal
Convention 10 are knights, made up thus: N.S.W.,
1 (Abbctt); Vic., 3 (Turner, Berry, Zeal) ; S.A.,
2 (Baker, Downer); Tas., 2 (Fysh, Braddon) ; and
W.A., 2 (Forrest, Steere). It is estimated that
before the Convention closes .the forty commoners
will “ have got there.”
A certain N.S.W. minor Judge used to
excuse himself for passing drastic sentences, on
the ground that he did so in order to attract the
attention of the Legislature to the absurd seventy
of the existing enactments. That was many years
ago. The Legislature has taken no notice, and the
Judge is still drastic.
Edward Warland died in Scone hospital
last week. He was the last survivor of the volun-
teers who hunted down the “Jew Boy gang in
1840. The gang consisted of seven desperados, ot
whom Davis, the “Jew Boy,” was leader. 1 iey
had been the terror of the Hunter, and working
up towards Murrurrundi they attacked the smal
station home of Edward Warland, who escaped
from their clutches, and from that hour devote
himself to running them down. Ihe whole gang
was captured, and the seven hanged in Sydney,
16th March, 1841.
Dr. Todd, who figured in a medico-legal
case in Sydney last week, and was also conspicu-
ous at the Dean trial, is the only N.S. W. barrister
who is also a legally-qualified medical man.

What a task ! Have
you read his clever
essay on murderer
Wainwright, “Pen,
pencil and poison.” It
is an appreciation of
the poisoner, full of
Wilde’s quaint moral
topsy - turvyism and
shocking epigram ; but
the delight in Wain-
wright’s sin is no mere
pose —the writer makes
it apparent that he
has discovered a con-
genial spirit, and the
two men certainly had
a great deal in com-
mon. Says Wilde :
“ He had that curious
love of green which,
in individuals,is always

artistic temperament.”
Then he raves about
Wainwright's lea n,

a-brac, his adoration

made the poisoning of

sure, her thick ankles
outraging his finesense
of form. Will the re-

W. E. Adcock, recently prominent in a
Perth law-suit, is perhaps the champion Austra-
lian litigant. Adcock, formerly a big N. Terri-
tory merchant, stopped in gaol in Adelaide for
four years in defiance of an Insolvency Court
order to furnish details of certain assets which it
was alleged were concealed in Melb.
FORCE OF EXAMPLE.
S HE: “ What are you looking for?”
H E: “Just looking to see if there’s a woman under the bed, my dear; you're always
looking for a man there! ”
Joe Cook, P.M.G., and James Blanksby,
M.L.C., are portion of the committee which runs
the Primitive Methodist newspaper. Blanksby,
when a full-blown reverend, one time edited that
paper, and F. W. Ward, now of Brisbane
Courier, began his, N.S.W. journalistic career as
editor of the same sheet.
H. H. Lusk, ex-M.H.R. (M.L.)
and ex-barrister of N.S.W., has an
article in the Forum for Dec. on
American women and American litera-
ture. When disbarred in Sydney, he
went to England and entered entirely
upon literary work.
“If you ask me to wliat this
success is due, I will let you into a
secret. Many people think that for
a journal to be popular it must fol-
low public opinion. This is a grand
mistake. A journal that waits for
public opinion to be formed before it
expresses its own, will infallibly in-
cur the contempt of the public. A
newspaper, to be read, must have
opinions of its own.” —David Syme
in 1868, on receiving the public sub-
scription of £750 over the Langton
libel-case.
The much-advertising Inclement
Wragge, of Queensland, modestly de-
mands that he should be appointed
Federal Weather Prophet for all Aus-
tralia, with the head office of the de-
partment at Brisbane, and writes to
the Age a letter mostly “I” and
“ We,” in which he mildly explains
that his “forecasts” are by far the
best in these provinces. Nobody
reading this effusion would imagine
that Wragge has been wandering over
the face of the earth almost continu-
ously for the last two years, while
“ our ” forecasts at Brisbane were
mostly issued by some unnoticed subor-
dinate. The present writer, by the
way, used to study Wragge’s weather
fiction a good deal, in the days when
Wragge was his own “ we,” with a
view to Sunday harbor excursions,
and as the result of much disaster he
regards that long, ill-fed-looking scien-
tist as about the most disappointing
gasmeter, for his size, in Australia.
When the Federal weather prophet
is wanted, how would it be to appoint
a committee to watch and verify
all the weather prophecies for two
years, and let the man with the big-
gest proportion of successes have the
billet?

made for signing documents, as his right arm is
still unusable. The millionaire’s normal signature
is a curious one. He does it in two big splashy
halves. The first half is “ Bra,” and the second
“ssey,” and his kind friends remark that if he
divided it “ Br-assey ” he would attain the obvious
and disarm criticism.
When the Age exploring expedition left
for New Guinea in the latter eighties Dawvid
Syme called Walker, the leader, into his sanctum
for final instructions. “Mr. Walker,” he re-
marked, impressively, “I am led to understand
that most of the troubles wi’ the blacks have
arisen over the native women. So if you find that
any immorality is takkin’ place when you are in
New Guinea, you have the authority of the Age
to suppress it.” This is probably a lie, but it is a
lovely lie.
Artist “ Jack ” Longstaff only a week or
two ago got the medal which goes with the
Travelling Scholarship won by him a decade ago.
The delay is alleged to have been due to difficulty
in securing a suitable design. One was got in Eng-
land, where the token was minted. It is a solid
gold affair worth about £2O.
“BOB” FITZSIMMONS, Champion Pugilist of the
World.
Photo, by Brand.
It is alleged that one fat Australian politi-
cian who has “ gammoned ” unwillingness to ac-
cept Chamberlain's invitation, within a fortnight
of receiving same got his tailor to measure him
for a Court suit, and had the monstrous measure-
ments sent ’Ome so that the suit aforesaid might
be ready agin his arrival. Diplomacy.
Dean Cowper, now in his 87th year, bravely
undertakes the duties of Bishop’s commissary
during the Primate’s absence in England. A year
or so ago the Dean’s health was feeble, and some-
one reported he had resigned on the score of old
age. The shock of the report set him on his feet
at once, and he has kept well to the front since.
T. W. Stanford, of East Melbourne, has
not seen his native ’Murka for 35 years. He has
such a horror of sea-sickness that he won’t even
cross the Pacific to see the University founded by
his brother, to which he intends to leave nearly
all his money and pictures. T. W. was the firSt
man to introduce kerosene lamps and oil info
Victoria, and he made a pot of money out of the
deal.
It is not generally known why the late
Dean Sheridan (R.C.), of Sydney, retired from
active missionary work. Under the old State-aid
regulations Dean Sheridan enjoyed a salary of
.£2OO. The R.C. Church authorities desired him
to give this amount to the “ general fund,” alßo
to surrender a house in Castlereagh-street, Syd-
ney. The dean refused to comply, hence his
retirement.
While Crick, M.P., was addressing an out-
door Convention election meeting in Sydney, the
other night, it started to rain, whereupon the
speaker closed up. “Goon 1 ” yelled the audience
—every audience likes to hear Crick. “‘Go on ’
be hanged,” replied W.P.C. ; “not me. It’s all
right enough for you fellows to stand in the rain.
You badly need a wash. I go for a swim every
morning myself. Ta-ta ! ”
Poor old Baron Von Mueller, ever craving
for public recognition of his life-work, left expresß
instructions that his grave in St. Kilda (Melb.)
cemetery should be merely covered with grass until
such time as the Vic. Government, or the world in
general, might care to acknowledge his services
with a “ brober memorial.” Von Mueller, the
shabby-looking old man, was an ardent admirer
of Von Mueller, the baronial botanist.

6d., the property of Mary Brown* , g ftrgeailfc
Eason stated that in the absence of evidence by

accused’s guilt. But every time accused was

to the market to “try his hand" there.
Accused was discharged with a view to being
taken back to the asylum.

‘ ‘ Victoria is monarch of England, but I
am monarch of Greater Britain.”— Chamberlain
(to his wife).
Napoleon said that in a hundred years
Europe would be Republican or Cossack. It is.
Russia reigns.
S.A. Assembly chamber, where the fifty
Convention delegates will meet, has accommoda-
tion for 54 members.
The four candidates whom the people care-
fully deposited at the very bottom of their respec-
tive polls were Smith (Vic.), Brown (Tas.), Jones
(S.A.), and Robertson (N.S.W.).
One of the older members of S.A. Assem-
bly is in shaky health, and an ambitious resident
in his district has already intimated that he will
be a candidate for election when “ our esteemed
member, Mr. , dies.”
Old-fashioned Tasmania again. At the
Federal elections 22 names had to be crossed out.
Result : eighteen hundred informal votes out of
eight thousand ! The most common mistake was
the leaving of eleven names on the ballot-paper.
Apropos the exodus of Australian
premiers, Colonel John Hay’s couplet :
As the meek beasts in the Garden came flocking for Adam
to name them,
Men for a title to-day crawl to the feet of a Queen.
Apropos opening Federal Convention with
prayer. There was a great fight over it in launch-
ing the Victorian Parliament, 185fi. Assembly
negatived, Council agreed to the Lord's Prayer.
O’Shanassy used habitually to talk loudly just
outside the bar, while the prayer went on.
To the hobbled Queensland politicians
listed last week, add M ‘Master, secured with the
Brisbane mayoralty. Grimes, who has ever shown
himself ready to do Govt.’s dirty work, is so far
left out in the cold, but he is a nonentity who has
probably been passed over as not worth rewarding.
Herbert Parsons, a nephew of millionaire
Angas, who is an articled clerk in Premier King-
ston’s office, wrote to tell him that he could not,
for political reasons, vote for him on Federation
election day. Kingston replied that he did not
ask, nor did he care, how Parsons voted. Par-
sons is standing for the Parliamentary vacancy
caused by Ash's death, and one of his methods of
obtaining votes is the publication of a letter from
Mrs. Ash, saying how good Parsons was to her
husband, and how sure she is that Ash would like
to see him succeed him in the district. The
Parsons politics are of the rigid Individualistic
order, as were those of George Ash, to whom he
was articled.
Premier Reid’s yearning after surreptitious
Protection is evidenced by his desire to keep the
railways under provincial instead
of Federal management. There
will be no Freetrade inside
the Commonwealth
unless the rail-
ways are under
Federal man-
agement. The
only change
will be that in-
stead of keep-
ingout the pro-
duceof thenext
province by
Customs duties,
it will be kept
out by prohibi-
tive ra i 1 way rates,
and instead of
helping local indus-
tries by differential
tariffs they will be
helped by carrying
their products at one-
quarter the rate
charged on the products
of the people over the bor-
der. For instance, Victoria
threatens to put a duty of 2s. fid. per ton on
N.S.W. coal ; that’s Protection. N.S.W. charges,
or recently charged, a special railway-rate of
2s. 6d. per ton on Victorian coal at Albury, in
addition to the ordinary mileage charge; that’s
Freetrade, but it gets there just the same. And
if the railways remain under provincial manage-
ment, it will keep on getting there just the same.
The artificial ports of Queensland are
wondrous contrivances. Towage, lighterage and
pilotage of the Ettrickdale, steamer with 4780 tons
of wheat, up Brisbane River, amounted to one
thousand pounds! This was discussed in Bris-
bane Chamber of Commerce, and it was admitted
that the river was no better than it was 30 years
ago. And something like a million has been spent
on it in various ways !
The troubles of Tozer,
the dummy-Premier-elect of
Leperland, will begin shortly.
A nice little plant has been
worked on him. The Labor-
party, whose leaders he tried
to nobble last session, may be
trusted to bark at him pretty
vigorously, the discontented
Ministerialists will show their
independence by assailing
him on the flank, and the
Byrnes faction will be stok-
ing up in the background.
When things get to about
their worst Nelson will bob
up serenely with Tommy,
and “go for ’’ Tozer for
having made a holy mess of
things.
The Kanakaland Impe-
rialists are whipping the cat
over the Convention elections
in the democratic provinces.
They think now they could
have squeezed in on a popu-
lar vote. The Labor-party
would have adopted the same
stupid tactics as their breth-
ren in N.S. Wales —run a
full list, talked a hurricane
about what they were going
to do, and got snuffed out;
the Catholic and Orange fac-
tions could have been sooled
on to one another ; and the
Government could have plan-
ted their ten black-labor ad-
vocates solid. All but. It’s
doubtful whether they could
have worked their big gun
(Griffith) even then. He is
a completely burst piece of
ordnance. There are not
many fear him, few trust
him, he is loved by none.
Windsor and Richmond Gazette (N.S.W.):
Bulletin calls the Protectionist party in Victoria the
Liberal party. Why ? In Canada, the Liberal party is
that which espouses Freetrade ; in N.S.W. ditto; in Great
Britain ditto; and ditto ditto everywhere else where
there are two parties.
Then The Bulletin is to assume that the Argus
is a. Radical paper, and that it was the Murray
Smith crowd who brought the Tories to their
knees in “ the Berry Revolution.”
Adelaide people are wroth because the
Federal Enabling Act, as passed there, gives each
of the ten S.A. representatives £2 2s. daily from
the date of election to the time the Convention is
dissolved, or about =£3so per man. They think
Ministers and legislators, who are paid already,
should forego their claim to payment, and that
the others should be content with fees while the
Convention is actually sitting. But, per contra,
it cost Solomon, M.P., quite .£lOOO to secure his
seat, while the legal members of Convention argue
that they have to sacrifice briefs in order to serve
the electors.

S.A. is the only province which has not
returned the Speaker of the Assembly among its
representatives. Jenkin Coles did not stand
because he thought his office should keep him out
of controversial politics.
A SMALL RESERVATION.
REV. BROWN : "Smith, I hear bad accounts of your youngest son;
wants checking ; spare the rod and spoil the child, you know."
SMITH: “No, sir! I don't believe in severity. Moral suasion's my
motto. I've reared seven sons now, and I’m proud to say I
never struck one of them yet—except in self-defence !”
The latest alarmist writes to the papers to
poin > out that no provision is made for a possible
deadlock in the Convention through the votes on
some important question being equal, and dread-
ful consequences may ensue. For that matter, no
provision was made for the equally horrible pos-
sibility that all the candidates who put up might
have got exactly the same number of votes, and so
no one been elected at all. It didn’t happen, of
course, but if it had happened the situation would
have been simply awful.
In the Tasmanian Federal Con-
vention election, although only 88.' 5
electors recorded their votes, there were
1194 invalid papers !
The Prudent Federation crowd,
with its doleful tale about the loss of
N.S.W. trade through Federal railway
management, and the necessity of stick-
ing to provincial control to retain this
trade, pas got its facts upside down.
Under the provincial system, one-
quarter of the N.S.W. exports go out
t ’id Adelaide and Melbourne. There-
fore, provincial railway management
doesn’t keep the trade of the province
for its own ports, and the claim that
N.S.W. should stick to provincial
management in order to keep the
trade falls through with a crash. There
is nothing to be gained by pointing out
that Federal control will allow part of
the N.S.W. trade to go to outside
ports when it is going there in any
case, and has been doing so all along.
“I have heard of a rather ex-
traordinary proposal made to the British
Postal Department by the German
authorities,” writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent. “It is that
each European country should carry
and deliver the others’ mails free on
a reciprocity basis. At present the
English mails landed at Naples or
Brindisi pay a toll-tax by weight to
both Italy and France. Altogether
England would not have much the
worst, probably it would have the best,
of such an arrangement, yet the autho-
rities at St. Martin’s-Le-Grand profess
to be much shocked at the scandalous
socialism they discern in the proposal.”
By some inexplicable muddle some of the
N.S.W. Federal election votes were counted twice
over, and the figures were considerably inflated in
consequence. Barton’s sensational i 04,000 votes
are thus diminished to 98,000, and there is a corres-
ponding reduction all round till Robertson, of the
Mangy Dog party, brings up the harmless neces-
sary tail with 1983.
The supremest absurdity in connection
withN.S.W. Premier Reid’s trip to Great Grovel-
land was “Johnny” Hawthorne’s endeavor as
Govt, whip, to nobble support for his boss. The
enthusiastic unanimity which he reported to exist
m favor of the project is ominous of a big splutter
when Parliament meets, and an attempt to jump
Jolly-an’-Fat’s reserved seat. If Lyne and Co
could only induce Carruthers to go a-grovel-
The other day Melb. Argus recovered
sufficiently to start demonstrating by an awful
confusion of figures that the Federal election
after all shows Victoria to be thoroughly Tory a*-
heart. Considering that the returns were made
up of ten Liberals and on'lv two Tories and
these two got in mostly through the support of
the Liberal paper, this is rather steep, but the
Argus says it is quite satisfied with the result
bo now everybody is quite satisfied, barring the
Labor-party, and everybody has won a moral
victory.
Can ballot-boxes in sparse country elec-
torates be shuffled or otherwise manipulated?
The delays in the Vic. Federal returns suggest
the question. Apropos of this, a neat definition of
“ poll ” is given, with the help of a comp., in a
standard law dictionary. It is “ the art of regis-
tering votes at an election.”
Premier Turner is the only one of the five
who takes all his official work with him to
Adelaide and proposes to run his department by
night and attend the convention by day. Practio-
ally the whole machinery of the Premier’s and
Treasurer’s departments goes along with Turner,
and he also proposes to draft one or two Acts and
get his financial statement started, and shove
things ahead generally in view of his visit to Eng-
land. As the results of all this will probably be
fatal, the other Premiers will be quite handy and
convenient for attendance at Turner’s funeral.
Truthful Tozer, at present gallivanting in
Tasmania, says Queensland will come into the
second Federal Convention. Tozer isn’t such a
fool as not to understand that anti-Federation, on
top of leg-irons, land-grabs, black-labor, and
Q.N. Bank horrors makes the load of infamy too
heavy for the present Q. rulers to hump out of the
way of the steam-roller of Ba.nanaland Democracy.
So he would jettison anti-federalism, and, if neces-
sary, black-labor, in order to save the other goods
and chattels. But what a splendid asset the
Q.N.B. will be to the Federal Government!
More than one Convention member con-
siders that the Federal Constitution makers will
be invited to determine whether or not members
of the Federal Senate shall be distinguished by a
life or hereditary title. W. C. Wentworth put a
plank of this sort in his first Australian Constitu-
tion Bill, but it is pointed out that circumstanoes
are now different, and that “the practical
unanimity lately shown to federate under the
Crown ” proves the popular tern pier favorable to
innovation. The popular temper will, in the
event of such a resolution being carried, prove it-
self unexpectedly lively.
The Queensland Labor-party is at its very
worst in Parliament, and at its very best on the
stump during the recess. Throughout the land in
factory, workshop, and on the hillside, the Labor
politician exalts himself and propounds the gospel
of discontent, and the hearts of the people are
touched, the realities of life and their own political
responsibilities are brought home to them. How
different from the average Government supporter I
After lazily dozing out the session within call of
the division-bell, he thinks he has done well if he
meets his committee in a back parlour where, safe
from the mob, he can read them a list of the jobs
he has been able to put through for them.

ot the U.B. Congress. Each House in the U.S.,
ley thoughtfully point out, keeps a chaplain of
its own, who has nothing to do except open it

annum, the pastors are so deeply interested in

PROPOSAL POP AMENDMENT OP EXISTING
SCHEME OP ARRANGEMENT.
1. All the extended Deposit Receipts, which will amount to about .£3,350,000, shall be cancelled
and replaced by Transferable Deposit Receipts as from Ist July, 1897.
2. The Transferable Deposit Receipts shall bear interest at the rate of 3 per cent, per annum,
payable half-yearly on Ist January and Ist July, with an additional 1 per cent, per annum out of
profits after payment of Preference Dividend, but the right to such additional 1 per cent, shall not
come into force until after Ist July, 1900, the amount during the interval being carried to a Special
Account as provided in Article 5.
3. The Transferable Deposit Receipts shall be payable as under, namely:—
As to 5 per cent, of the original amount on Ist July, 1899

or at the option of the Bank at any time before those dates on giving three months’ notice, in sums of
not less than 5 per cent, of the original amount.
4. The holders of Transferable Deposit Receipts shall rank, as regards principal and interest, pari
passu with each other, and after the other Creditors of the Bank.
5. After payment of 3 per cent, on the Transferable Deposit Receipts and the Preference
Dividend, the following provisions shall be made out of surplus profit, namely :
(a) Until Ist of July, 1900, a sum equivalent to 1 per cent, per annum on the Transferable
Deposit Receipts outstanding shall be carried to a Special Account to secure primarily the
interest at 3 per cent, on such deposits, and ultimately to be repaid, so far as it has not been
used for that purpose, to holders of the Transferable Deposit Receipts pro rata on the dates
when the last 50 per cent, of those receipts are repaid.
(b) After Ist July, 1900, to payment of additional interest up to 1 per cent, per annum on the
Transferable Deposit Receipts outstanding, making the interest up to 4 per cent, per annum.
6. No dividend in excess of 2 per cent, per annum shall be paid to Ordinary Shareholders until all
the Transferable Deposit Receipts have been repaid.
London, sth February, 1897.

Patents Office,
Sydney, 12th March, 1897.
APPLICATION FOR AMENDMENT OF
LETT FRS PATENT UNDER THE
PATENTS LAW AMENDMENT ACT,
1895.
NOTICE is hereby given that any person or
persons intending to Oppose this application must
leave at the Patents Office, within one month
from the 12th instant, notice in the prescribed
, form of his or their objections thereto.
Letters Patent No. 453, 21st January, 1888,
granted to T ’.e Cassel Gold Extracting Com-
pany (Limited), for “ Improvements in obtain-
ing gold and silver from ores and other
compounds.”
The Australian Gold Recovery Company
(Limited), as assignees of the Cassel Gold Extract-
ing Company (Limited) [through their Patent
Agent, Fred. Walsh, manager for Edward Waters],
have applied for leave to amend the Specification
of the Letters Patent above referred to.
The proposed amendments are as follow :
1. To insert after the paragraph terminating
with the words “or closed vessels” the follow-
ing :
“ Whereas a claim simply and broadly for the
use of cyanogen or a cyanide or cyanogen-
yielding substance might be held to in-
clude solutions of any strength, and
whereas solutions containing cyanogen in
a proportion not exceeding eight parts of
cyanogen to one thousand parts of water
are most advantageous, having a selec-
tive action such as to dissolve gold and
silver in preference to the baser metals,
whilst stronger solutions, besides being
more costly, are of less practical value, we
hereby disclaim the use of such stronger
solutions.”
2. In the claim to strike out the words
“cyanogen or cyanide or other substance or com-
pound containing or yielding cyanogen substan-
tially as specified,” and to substitute therefor the
following words :
“ A solution containing cyanogen in a propor-
tion not exceeding eight parts of cyanogen
to one thousand parts of water. ”
A. PARRY LONG,
Examiner of Patents.

SPOUTING NOTIONS.
Good as is Newhaven, it is perhaps too much to
expect him to win Sydney Cup with the thumping impost
of 9st. 51b. Resolute is the blot of the handicap, and he
only has to be delivered at the post fit and well to win.
The Meddler, Hopsootch, Tom, and Reka appear his
most dangerous opponents. Kelso seems to hold the key
of the Doncaster, and his selected should about win,
although Pharamond, Dalmeny, Valiant, and Phillip may
be expected to run well. The crack two-year-old Aurum
is much fancied by a lot of good judges, but Bst. 61b. is a
crusher for a youngster in such a race.
• * • *
The weights for S.A.J.C. Autumn handicaps
give promise of good acceptances. In Goodwood Handicap
the Newmarket failure, Staffa, has been given a good
chance, whilst the well-bred Thunder Queen looks, on
paper, very like the winner of Adelaide Cup.
* * * •
The Caulfield Cup winner, Cremorne, is gaining
many friends for Doncaster by his good work at Rand-
wick, but he will probably find 9st. 91b. a steadier for a
fast mile. The Newmarket disappointment, Valiant, is
going well at exercise, and he may show something like
true form when the punting public is not “ on.”
* * * *
Sweep-money considerations brought out a good
field of handicap horses at Moorefield (N.S.W.) last Satur-
day, when Kelso simply farmed the big event by running
first, third, fourth and sixth with horses under his charge.
The winner turned up in the hitherto unlucky Reka, and
this raking brother to Portsea will have a big say in the
Doncaster and Sydney Cup, in which races he is nicely
handicapped with 7st. 91b. and 7st. 51b.
• » * •
Cabled that the Queen’s Bench Division has just
upheld the claim of the Anti-Gambling League that ready-
money betting at Tattersall’s, in the ring and at race-
courses, is illegal. A Sydney case will be affected by this
decision. And generally the gentle gambler will have his
occupation gone, in theory anyway, until the Court of
Appeal upsets the Q.B.D. or the House of Lords upsets
both. But it is significant that nothing is said about
betting on credit.
• • • *
The fashionably-bred youngster Carbinier (Car-
bine—Barley) is to be put through his pas by Walter
Hickenbotham, the trainer of Newhaven. Barley is an
imported mare by the mighty Barcaldine out of the very
handsome Lonely, an Oaks winner, so, if fashionable nicks
go for aught, this colt should turn out a hummer.
• » * *
M.L. Amateur Athletic Association, just dis-
covering that bookies did howling biz at its last cham-
pionship meeting, has turned wrathfully censorious and
denunciatory. Yet everyone could see it on the day.
Not only the public, but competitors, backed themselves
or their fancies. The “ lily-white” amateur, who doesn’t
race for money, but who puts his year’s savings on his
chance and then sweeps down the ’straight for a pawn-
shop medal, is a truly wonderful product of modern
athleticism. Writer knows of one alleged amateur who
won £2OO at a minor meeting a while back. And it isn’t
easy to scoop in that sum in M.L. just now.
• • • •
Gov. Jerry Smith has given up horse-racing
in Westralia. He had a string of horses, but they seemed
to have Gov. Jerry on a string too.
* • •
In North M.L. a most astute turfite got taken
down in a painful way. A Maori priced an indifferent
galloping crock in training, which the owner was glad to
sell, giving three weeks’ credit. The neddy skinned the
pool at an outback meeting the week afterwards under a
false name, and next day the heathen returned the prad
saying he couldn’t raise the selling price. Result: a dis-
qualified horse in a respectable stable, and a vanished
Maori with pocketed stakes.
* * * *
W. R. Wilson takes with him to England, this
trip, a St. Alban’s stud-book and history of the stable
during his six years’ reign. Which suggests that “ the
master of St. Alban’s” will be open to receive- offers for
all or any of his blood stock.
* * * *
Paragraphs in the Melbourne, “respectable”
press, re the send-off dinner to bookmakers Arnfield and
Sam Allen, gave an air of decent convivality to what was
really a vulgar oiyie. It is time that editors ceased to
provide free advertisement for Ikey Mo’s little bean-
feasts, unless they (the editors) like to show a little
public spirit by publishing the truth about the behaviour
of the guests.
* * * *
R. Hesketh, the blind mat-maker of Tamworth
who won about £4OOO in a sweep lately, was waited upon,
when the telegram announcing the fact became known, by
representatives of the local Ladies’ Benevolent Society.
These produced an account showing that in the twelve
past years the old man had been relieved by them to the
extent of £lB3 6s. 7d., and they signified a desire for a
liberal cheque. Hesketh gave them £lOO. “ I’d a-given
ye £200,” he said, “ only for yer greedy ’urry.”
* « * »
Donald Wallace pursues an unwise policy in
naming his yearlings; purchasers invariably prefer to
label their own purchases. “ He’s not a bad nag, and I’d
have given the price for him, only for the blanky name,”
has been heard more than once in the saleyards.
* * * •
The question is often asked by the ordinary
Sydney citizen—“ Why are the totes permitted to do
business so openly,, seeing it is illegal ? ” Perhaps it would
be worth Inspector-General Fosbery’s while taking a walk
along Pitt or King streets, for he would daily see police-
men in uniform and plainclothes detectives choosing
their doubles and backing their fancies.
* * • *
Proprietor Webster, of the Ascot pony race-
course, Melb. (a nephew of George Webster, the ware-
houseman), graduated in the thimble-and-thread depart-
ment, and when he first took to the turf showed a fond
remembrance of old friends by naming some of the events
after various “ heads of departments ” in Nunky’s big
shop. There were the James Blankets Handicap, for
instance, and the Charlie Muslins Maiden Plate, and so on
ad. lib. This kind of celebrity didn’t seem to suit the
favored parties, however. They served the racing Web-
ster with an important-looking document, desiring him to
discontinue the practice of wafting horsey odors towards
Flinders Lane.
* * » •
A third half-yearly volume of J. J. Miller’s
Australian Racing Chronicle (from Aug. 1, 1896, to
January 31, 1897) has just been issued at the moderate
price of ss.,'together with some figures showing the dif-
ference in sporting value between two half-yearly volumes
of the Chronicle (cost 10s.) and the annual Turf Register
(published at 30s. from the Argus office), which used to
be the recognised record of racing in this country.
Whereas Miller’s laboriously-compiled history contains
particulars of 1194 meetings in the 12 months the Turf
Register deals with 358 only, less than a third of
the full total. The index to this latest volume
of the Racing Chronicle comprises names and pedi-
grees of over 5000 horses, begotten by 600 odd sires.
Newminster ranks as champion sire in the matter
of stakes won by Newhaven and other offspring, but
as a wholesale producer of small winners the famous
stallion comes “ nowhere ” behind Sunrise, whose stock
captured 73 races, of one sort or other, during the six
months. J. J. Miller notifies breeders and horse-students
in general that he is prepared to supply on application
detailed performances of the progeny of any sire in Aus-
tralia, from Ist Aug. last.
» * » *
Horrors upon horrors’ head accumulate. Oh !
blessed St. Patrick, that we should live to see it 1
Here upon the very Saint’s day itself comes the yellow
agony out on the top-side. At Elwick (Tas.), on the 17th
March, no less, a £45 div. was paid on the tote, and five
accursed Chows scooped it in. For an outlay of two
shillings each flat-nosed heathen raked in nine lovely
sovereigns, and the whole bilin’ wouldn’t give the little
boy that rode the winner half-a-sov. amongst the lot of
them. The harp that once :
Ah, holy St. Pat, is it sleepin’ ye are ?
Or what are ye doin’ at all ?
To let your own childer fall into the soup,
While the Chow comes on top of it all.
The heathen that cannot, unless in a cart,
Tell the head of a horse from his tail
Crawls up to the window and rakes in the pot,
While the boys that have stood by ye fail.
The bit of a stick, the pride of our hearts,
It’s a Joss-stick we’ll make of it now ;
And greet ye wid crackers, baptize ye again,
Joss Patrick, the Saint of a Chow !
• * * *
South Australian: “ The Bulletin of March 13
rather unjustly suggested that Providence in the shape of
rain came to the assistance of the South Australians in
their recent match with the Victorians. Facts are that
the Victorians batted all one day on a perfect wicket, and
half-an-hour on the second day. S.A. had the rest of the
second day on the same wicket, and when stumps were
drawn were 66 runs on with 7 wickets, including those of
Hill and Giffen, to fall—so that it may fairly be said they
had the match well in hand. On the Sunday rain fell,
and on the Monday the wicket was so sticky that the
batsmen were at the mercy of the bowlers, yet S.A. with
its last 7 wickets made more runs than its 10 opponents,
all batting under the same disadvantages.”
* » • *
Most Australian followers of the game thought
ex-bank-clerk Corbett, after years of bluffing and play-
acting, would, at the last moment, have wriggled out of
meeting ex-blacksmith Fitzsimmons. But, after all, the
fight came off—with the result that Fitz. knocked the
American out in 14 rounds. Whatever Corbett may be
now, Australia was satisfied that he was up to champion-
ship form in 1890, when he fought a 64-round “ draw ”
with Peter Jackson, probably the greatest “ Queensberry”
fighter the world has yet produced. Jackson, at the
time, was said to be suffering from a weak ankle, but
given that in, the man who could stand before the black
until his injured limb went against him, showed himself
to be a formidable opponent. From the meagre account
to hand, it appears that Corbett had somewhat the best
of the early part of the contest, but it is a peculiarity of
most of Fitz.'s fights that he apparently plays second
fiddle until the opening appears for those sledge-hammer
blows of his. Fitz. is described as “covered in blood”
during the first few rounds, but it is not the blood-letting
hits that do the damage. Fitzsimmons—a leaf from the
book of sculler Beach —now announces his retirement.
Sensible man —to retire unbeaten and in the zenith of his
fame. No gamer or better man of his weight ever stood
in a prize-ring.
* * * *
The column account bestowed by the D.T. on
the Corbett-Fitzsimmons tussle gives the knock-out to a
tacit understanding between Sydney’s morning dailies to
take no stock in punching-bouts. The papers dropped
pugilism from their columns when the noble art was at
its height in Sydney—a piping-hot sport enlivened by
frequent manslaughter and the wrecking of public halls
by disappointed barrackers.
* * * *
Last Saturday afternoon a bleak wind and
threatening sky thinned the crowd at N.S.W. Wheelmen's
League meeting, but the sky clearing, some 12,000 people
rolled up for the night racing. These and other indica-
tions show that the cycle craze is progressing with big
strides. One Sydney cycling firm is said to have £BOOO
worth of cycles out on time-payment.
A hot field came out for the first heat of the
First Class Handicap—Martin, Walne, Green, Ken. Lewis,
Porta, Kellow, Megson, Payne, and others. It was Mar-
tin who got the reception as the competitors pedalled
round to their marks. But it was Megson who got in the
desperate sprint home. At the bell’s ringing Plugger
Bill jumped to the front, then Ken. Lewis cut him down,
then Green rushed to the lead, then Walne, then Megson
came with a splendid dash and left the lot hopelessly be-
hind. Megson, the other day, put up a new Aus. mile-
record of lmin. 48 3-ssec.
Edwards, the English flyer, won’t face Megson
now in a sprint. Edwards scratched in his-and-Megson’s
heat of the Metropolitan Handicap (one mile, *2O). Meg-
son’s dash home in this heat was a splendid effort.
“ Jim ”M Times won the First Class Handicap,
£l5 first prize. His return to form caused much cheering.
M'lnnes does not get the chance of regular training that
most racers get. He has to do 18 or 20 hours a day some-
times, stoking on an engine.
Lesna did win the Grand Paced Tournament
(10 miles, £3O), and there would have been no excuse for
his losing it. Both Green and Megson (especially Megson)
had used up their steam in previous races, but Lesna
hadn’t been on his bicycle. Lesna and Green were equally
well paced—indeed, it was the fairest exhibition of pacing
yet seen in Sydney. Green held Lesna for five miles,
then he squeaked and only just saved himself from being
lapped in the remaining five. Megson, whose real dis-
tance is not over two miles, quit early. “ Joe ” had had
a terrible day’s work, without going in for another ten
miles at night.
The handicapper, of course, is made to be
growled at, but no finish could be more close and exciting
than that of the Sydney Wheel Race, two miles. Fitz-
gerald (Vic.), Bailey, and Matthews, in that order, were
separated only by inches; prizes, £lOO, £25, £5.
The Martin-Megson fiasco was an unfortunate
affair for the League. The match (ten miles), however,
will educate the public to the fact that a rider perfectly
paced can easily beat his superior indifferently paced.
Megson rides Dunlop tyres, and Martin rides Palmer.-
Hence the strong array of Dunlop pacing was at Megson’s
disposal, while Martin had but three triplets to pace him.
The Dunlop teams, managed by James, drew Megson
away and away from Martin, till the latter, recognising
the hopelessness of continuing, threw up his hands and
wheeled off the track. “I can’t race him with that
pacing,” said Martin, “I’m not an iron man.”
They would disqualify a man in America for
looking round as Hawley did in the finish of his heat of
the Second-class Handicap. Sec. Hunter should see to
this. There have been several broken bones already. An
ungainly rider looking round at a race’s finish might pro-
vide the grand-stand with a ghastly spectacle.
* • * *
How Bilmartin does battle for the cash 1 Re-
turning to Vic. on Wednesday he won the five-mile scratch
at Geelong on the afternoon of St. Patrick’s Day, then
rushed for the train and reachedHelb. by 8 p.m., arriving
at the Exhibition building in time to start for the mile.
Bill was beaten in this, but he wasted no time and hustled
off to bed to catch the return train the next day to
Sydney.
* » * •
Troublous days have fallen upon Plugger Bill;
the time when he used to win six out of seven races in an
afternoon seems to have departed for ever. Every time
he races now his head sinks lower upon his handle-bars,
and the solid end of his back rises higher. In his heat of
the Sydney Plate he just managed, by kicks of despera-
tion, to make a dead-heat of it with Harris, and in the
Final (£2O, £7 and £3) he couldn’t get upon the track of
Lester in the run home. Taylor (Vic.), too, lost Martin
in their heat of the Metropolitan Handicap. Taylor, how-
ever, was paced by a good man all through.
* » ♦ •
Bill Martin pocketed .£145 as his earnings for
the week ending March 6 :
’Tis said that money’s meant to roll,
So nice and round they make it,
And so it swiftly rolls; but, though
Too quick for others it may go,
Plain Bill can overtake it.
* * * •
Too much Second and Third-Class Handicap
will kill the game. The public won’t pay 2s. to see a lot
of unknowns and novices compete. Last Saturday after-
noon there was not one spark of enthusiasm until the
gods came out for the First-Class Handicap.
* * * •
The Vic. rifle-team, which is to shoot at the
Bisley competitions, will consist of the best marksmen
the province can produce in a series of diverse tests. As
the majority of top-notch shooters have hitherto been
civilians, it is highly probable that the same proportion
will prevail among the leading men in the tests. If so,
the team, which is despatched more for the purpose of
showing, the noble Australian soldier ready to fight for
England’s Queen than for competitive purposes, will be
composed chiefly of common, ordinary, everyday artisans
and -bushmen, who are absolutely incapable of keeping
their little fingers to the seams of their trousers. From
an Himperial standpoint, the throwing open of the test
was a huge tactical blunder ; the team should have been
restricted to the members of the military and naval
forces.
« * * *
Mr. Matthew Seal, chairman of Tas. Fisheries
Board, an enthusiastic angler, well-known as a fish-
acclimatisation enthusiast, was found dead alongside his
boat at the Great Lake, where he had gone fishing with
Lord Gormanston. Deceased, who was 63 years of age,
was a son of the late Chas. Seal, well-known in connection
with whaling enterprise in early years. For some years
he was in business at Ballarat, but had lived in retirement
at Hobart since ’74.

“ Federal train ” provided to carry
the N.S.W. and Yic. delegates and their wives
and belongings—about 80 people in all—from
Melbourne to Adelaide was intended to be a
specially luxurious affair. Among other things it
was promised by the railway department that
at Murray Bridge breakfast would be served to
the travellers in bed, but this part of the scheme
fell through. The refreshment-room keeper, hear-
ing of it, telegraphed that he was a respectable
male person, and that he flatly declined to carry
in breakfast to other men’s wives and daughters in
bed -in fact, he wouldn’t intrude on any lady’s
sleeping apartment, not even if the lady guaran-
teed to completely disappear under the bed-
clothes till he was gone. So that luxury was
declared off. ;
The wing of the Melb. St. Patrick’s pro-
cession which marched into the city from Bruns-
wick was an extraordinary spectacle. Hitherto
there were no minor processions at all, but, after
“ the battle of Brunswick ” last year, the bhoys
couldn’t resist asking the Orangemen to thread on
the tails av their coats by arranging a little celebra-
tion on the same spot. As a sort of final gage of
defiance, a small, unobtrusive advt. was put in the
Herald on the night before, calling upon citizens
to assemble at Brunswick to “ discuss equality ”
and when the hour arrived 40 massive Hibernians
with lengthy upper lips, belltoppers, frock-coats,
and big blackthorn sticks, turned up. On the
spot they found 27 troopers and 40 foot constables
who had apparently been there all night, and all
hopes of a little harmless fun were knocked on
the head. Then a start was made for Melb., and
down the Sydney road they marched, the 40 green-
sashed Irishmen in the middle and the 67 coppers
walking on either side. No wonder a marvelling
nevv-chum diagnosed the procession as a police-
man s funeral.


Mr. Jas. Peddle, architect for Atcherley
and Dawson, is erecting at N.S.W. Agricultural
Society’s grounds a huge globe, 45 feet high, for
the use of those celebrated tea men during the
coming show. Here cups of tea will be dispensed
gratis to thousands of thirsty visitors, and the
merits of the famous Globe blend can be practically
tested by any to whom it is still a boon untasted.*

Frossard’s Cavour Cigars are unequalled any-

from nicotine. They are absolutely uninjurious,

ployed.
T. B. Browne’s “Advertiser’s A.B.C. and
Advertisement Press Directory ” —now in its 11th
year —is a handsome volume of 1100 pp., chockfull
of information concerning newspapers of the
present age and all countries. The various sec-
tions are embedded in a mass of information con-
cerning population and trade and resources,
which adds considerably to the value of this well-
known handbook as a work of reference.*

Wbat is Wolfe’s Scboapps?
IT IS said there are still some few unsophisticated persons who
ask the above question. Well, Wolfe’s Schnapps
is a medicinal diet drink of eminently salutary quali-
ties, and is manufactured by the proprietor at his
factory at Schiedam, Holland. It is made from the
best barley that can be selected in Europe, with the
essence of an aromatic Italian berry of acknowledged
and extraordinary medicinal properties. The distress-
ing effect upon the stomach, bowels, and bladder
produced by impure water is well-known; Wolfe’s
Schnapps is an absolute corrective of the injurious
properties of bad Water, and consequently prevents
the diseases which they occasion. Wolfe’s Schnapps
is sold in large and small bottles by all respectable
Publicans and Spirit Dealers.

Bee-poison, according to the Australian
Christian World, is a sovereign remedy for
cancer, rheumatism, snakebite, and lots of other
things. But, in this paper’s opinion, pigeon’s
milk is even better, for it will make brains grow
where there were none before. Emu’s milk, also,
will, on application to the feet of the unemployed,
cause to grow thereon immediately a pair of 225.
6d. kangaroo-leather spring sides, and, applied to
a bald head, it will promptly adorn it with a full
thatch and a silken tile to protect it from the
weather.
There are now in N.S.W. only 4397 acres
under wine. Bijiji vineyard, Corowa (200 acres,
and now seven years old), is the largest in the pro-
vince, and the yield this year will be 25,000 gallons.
In the English market, Mr. Bray, the owner, has
cleared over 2s. per gallon for 18 months’-old wine,
after deducting all expenses.
Letter received by relief-officer in Bris-
bane from a hard-up : —“ I thank you for setting
the police on my track. I came to Queensland
some years ago with =£so in pocket.” (Here fol-
lows a really pitiable tale of destitution). . . .
“But keep your pauper relief; keep your brown
sugar, and help to pay immigration-lecturers to
bring out more unfortunate dupes to this sunburnt
Gomorrah, where an honest man has as much
chance of prospering as a vine in the Arctic
regions. ”
New Australia, as an example of true
mateship, has gone into liquidation. All its life
it kept coming wallop against the disastrous
question, “ Who is to wash up?” Apparently it
struck against this problem once too often and
foundered. There is to be a general division of
the assets. Ihe original concession is annulled,
and instead the Government grants a separate
farm to each settler, where he can put a fence
around his house and warn off other mates with a
hoe if he wants to. Also, the grant of 800 dollars
per month from the Paraguayan Government is to
be continued, only presumably each mate will get
his share of the grant separately. To go all the
way from Australia in order to set up a model
settlement and be a bright and shining light to all
the world, and then drop to living, in part at
least, on the charity of a kind-hearted Greaser
Ministry, is a great fall, but it is at least a blessing
that the charitable Greaser was there to fall upon.
As regards Bane’s Cosine settlement, nothing has
been heard for quite a long time ; now and then a
faint echo alleges that Bane is in England looking
for recruits, but the Cosmo colonists themselves
have almost dropped out of knowledge.
Q. Electric Telegraph Gazette quotes
Q. electrical engineer Hesketh on the reasons for
the Postal Conference’s decision to change from
the Morse code to the Universal. Abridged—
The present lack of uniformity in the codes used by the
different Australasian provinces is objectionable in the
extreme. Westralia uses the Universal alphabet but
special figures. S.A. has altered the letters L X and the
figure 9 to render its code less liable to error. Between
the codes in use in Q., N.S.W., and Vic., there are a few
minor differences. M.L. already uses the Universal code.
Any code which uses three signs to make its letters is
more liable to error than a code which uses only two.
The combination of two I’s for Y is distinctly erroneous.
As regards speed of working, counting the number of dots
and dashes required to signal 1000 words in either code, there
is a slight difference in favor of the Universal code. The
same cannot be said of figures, but Q. has always to check
its Morse figures by words. With the Universal code
there is not the least doubt about a figure. But the main
reason for change is the consideration of uniformity with
the rest of the telegraphic world. In Australasia to-day
there are in use three codes dissimilar to any in use in
any other part of the world. Including the code used by
military and naval authorities, there are four. By the
introduction of the Universal code, we shall come into
uniformity, not only among ourselves, but with the rest
of the world, the U.S. alone excepted. The inconvenience
the operators will feel is temporary and slight —unless
they are obstinate or lazy.

On the Murchison lately, a blackfellow,
while winding a sgal. drum of water up from a
deep shaft, took a spell half-way and let go the
windlass-handle to spit on his hands. The handle
went round and knocked his brains out. Now it
is a hard job to get a Murchison blackfellow to
touch a windlass at all.
In one of the trashy feminine penny
weekly imported papers the domestic servant’s
hours are laid out in a programme of hard-graft
extending from 6 a.m. to 10 p.m. No mention is
made of nights or Sundays off, and “ younv
wives” are specially warned against “possible
petty pilferers.”

A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfahne Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The grea,t virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice ; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite- sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should, be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W.A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common ? ” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

Mr. Button writes later:—“The Pills I had from you
were of great benefit to me and others. In one case,
when three hunters and trappers were travelling about
the ranges near my place on the Rose River, one was taken
very ill; his mates intended taking him in a w’aggon to
Wangaratta (about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor.
I gave him a few of your Pills. I am pleased to inform
you that, having to pass their camp next day, I found
him up and pegging-out his skins and looking well. This
speaks volumes for your valuable Remedies.”

The above selected Press opinions are quite sufficient
to show the esteem Herr Rassmussen is held in by those
who have the best opportunity to watch his career.

£>UTHERGLEN and CARLYLE DEEP
JlV> LEADS COMPANY
(To be Registered as a No-Liability Company).
CAPITAL—4O,OOO, in 40,000 Shares of £1 each.
14,000 shares fully paid-up, and 7000 contributing
shares, paid up to 2s. 6d., are to be retained
by the Rutherglen and Carlyle Proprietary
Gold-mining Company No Liability, which
is the vendor company.
19,000 contributing shares are now offered to the
public at Is. per share on application and
Is. fid. on allotment. The whole of the pro-
ceeds, less flotation expenses, to be placed
to the credit of the company.
PROVISIONAL DIRECTORS:
Samuel R. Walter, Esq. (director Great Nor-
thern Extended, Great Southern, Great
Southern No. 1, Rutherglen; Chiltem Valley,
Chiltern, &c.).

Long Tunnel, Walhalla; Great Southern
No. 1, Rutherglen, &c.).

All of whom are directors of the vendor company.

This company is being formed for the purpose
of acquiring from the Rutherglen and Carlyle
Proprietary Gold-mining Company No Liability
all their right, title, and interest in an immense
area of alluvial ground, approximating 3600 acres,
situated at Rutherglen. The property has been
proved by bores passing through favorable strata
and striking wash from 3ft. to lift, in depth, and
in several of which gold was obtained. The
ground was bored so as to prove that the lead,
which is a junction of the Wahgunyah, Lanark-
shire, and Robert Burns leads, traverses, as far as
it is possible to judge, through the entire length of
the property from south to north. Consequently
it is a most valuable one, and the preliminary
prospecting work having been completed, the pre-
sent proposed company is thus placed in a most
favorable position to sink its shaft and drive on
the wash.
East St. Kilda, 17th July, 1896.
Messrs. Meudell and Cameron, 31 Queen-street,
Melbourne,
Dear Sirs, —
In response to your request for a report upon
the property of the Rutherglen and Carlyle Pro-
prietary Gold-mining Company, I beg to submit
the facts and general conclusions already set forth
in my official report of May, 1894, with such fur-
ther remarks as are justified by records of borings
since executed on the property.
As shown on the plans, the area occupied by the
company contains about 4000 acres, bounded on
the west and north by the Murray River, the
greatest length (in the western portion) from north
to south being nearly 3| miles, and the width from
east to west, in the northern portion, about 2|
miles. The southerly extremity of the western
portion is about one mile wide, and lies between
two well-defined outcrops of silurian bed-rock,
that on the west being at Wahgunyah, and trace-
able thence along the river to near the north-
western corner of the ground, while that on the
east is on a contiguous area, shown on the plans as
once held by the Golden Valley Company. There
is no other possible passage than between these
outcrops, and through the entire length of the
western portion of the property, for the trunk
lead formed by the combination of the Bobbie
Burns, Lanarkshire, and Wahgunyah leads, once
very profitably worked on shallower ground to the
south-east, and trending towards the Rutherglen
and Carlyle Company’s area.
There is also strong evidence in support of the
belief that another trunk lead, formed by the com-
bination of the Great Southern, Garibaldi, Rose of
Victoria, Rose of Sharon, and other leads, will be
found to pass into the norther portion of the
ground on the eastern side, and trend westerly
across it to join the first-mentioned lead.
The evidence is distinct that the property cer-
tainly contains a length of nearly 3£ miles on the
course of one lead, and very probably to 2 miles
on that of the other.
Since the date of my official report, a series of
bores have been put down in search of the exact
position of the first-mentioned or western trunk
lead, and the records show that the deepest ground
with coarse wash sft. thick was proved at a depth
of 370 ft. from the surface, in the central part of
the western section of the area.
With respect to the probable auriferous quality
of the ground, satisfactory evidence is afforded by
its position directly over the course of prolonga-
tion of the auriferous belt of rocks which supplied
the gold at the sources of the leads. The strike
Prospectus.
of this belt carries it directly under the property,
and it may therefore be confidently assumed that
the gravels in the deep ground contain gold, de-
rived from the abrasion of rich local auriferous
matrices, equal to those which supplied the leads
near the source of the latter.
I feel justified on the above-stated grounds in ex-
pressing the confident opinion that the property of
the Rutherglen and Carlyle Proprietary Company
presents a most legitimate and promising field for
mining enterprise, fully warranting the expendi-
ture of the capital necessary to develope the ground
and provide adequate machinery.
The close proximity of the Wahgunyah railway
station is most favorable for the easy delivery on
the ground of all machinery, timber, and other
mining requisites.
I am, See.,
REGINALD A. E. MURRAY, F.G.S., London.
No. 1 LINE of BORES, 25 Chains from South-
ern Boundary:—
No. 1 bore bottomed at 258 ft. on bed-rock
No. 2 bore bottomed at 311 ft. 6in. passed
through lift, washdirt, carrying gold
No. 3 bore bottomed at 279 ft., soft reef, gravel
on top
No. 4 bore bottomed at 284 ft. on bed-rock
No. 5 bore bottomed at 294 ft. on bed-rock
No. 2 LINE, on Gooramadda-road, If miles
north from No. 1 line:—
No. 6 bore, lost
No. 7 bore, lost at 278 ft.
No. 8 bore bottomed at 328 ft., 2ft. wash
No. 9 bore bottomed at 238 ft. on bed-rock
No. 10 bore bottomed at 350 ft. on bed-rock
No. 11 bore bottomed at 374 ft., sft. 6in. very
coarse wash, carrying gold
No. 12 bore, half-a-mile east of No. 11 bore,
bottomed at 310 ft. on bed-rock. This bore
indicates another system of leads coming in
from south-east, which will be proved by
further boring.
The terms upon which this property is offered
are most reasonable. The vendor company asks
for no cash, and in part payment for its vast area
is accepting 7000 shares, in lieu of cash, paid up to
2s. 6d., for distribution amongst its shareholders,
who are a representative body of mining and in-
fluential men in Melbourne.
The directors of the vendor company offer this
property to their shareholders and the public with
every confidence, and from the recent develop-
ments of the leads at Rutherglen it is anticipated
that there is a great future before the proposed
company.
Agreements have been completed with the
various owners of the ground, and in no case has
royalty to be paid, which is a most favorable
feature in regard to this company.
The large area now offered by the present com-
pany is sufficient to allow of several extensive
claims being formed therefrom, from which holders
in the proposed company would derive substantial
benefit.
Any further information desired can be obtained
at the offices of the agents for the company.
MEUDELL & CAMERON,
Agents for the Company, 31 Queen-street, Mel-
bourne.

And yet another Cure of Locomotor
Ataxia,
SYDNEY INHABITANTS ASTOUNDED.
PARTIAL PARALYSIS, SUPPOSED BY
MOST PEOPLE TO BE INCURABLE,
HAS AT LAST BEEN CONQUERED
BY DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS.
MIRACLES ARE BEING WROUGHT IN
OUR MIDST.
INVESTIGATION BY A NEWSPAPER
REPORTER.
A wonderful instance of the recuperative power
of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills is just to hand, says
the Press Association, and an account is given
below by our representative, who interviewed the
lady in question (Mrs. Nellie Denver, of Countess
Cottage, William-street, Double Bay), who, for
over the last six years, has been a martyr to that
dreaded affliction, locomotor ataxia, and it is the
almost instantaneous benefit derived from the use
of these Pills that makes the following little
history an item of such trilling interest. In Mrs.
Denver’s own words she stated :
“About six years ago, when residing in
Hobart, Tasmania, I had the misfortune to meet
with a serious accident, which happened in the
simplest manner possible, as I will endeavour to
relate. On leaving my house by the back gate the
bolt slipped, whilst I had hold of it, which caused
me to fall very heavily, which I subsequently dis-
covered had seriously injured my spine, although,
strange to say, I felt no ill-effects for about two-
years after. I then commenced to suffer from
great weakness and dizziness, and an intolerable
feeling of exhaustion; in fact, I felt so nervous
and uneasy that I feared to cross the street,
alarmed that I might meet with some accident
from a passing vehicle. Not at first recognising
the really serious condition to which I was reduced,
I did not seek medical attendance, but when I at
last became aware of my serious condition, I was
persuaded by my friends to go to the Sydney Hos-
pital, where I remained two months without re-
ceiving any permanent benefit. On leaving the
hospital I again rather neglected my state of
health, and on applying for re-admission was in-
formed that my case was perfectly hopeless. Being
unfortunately not over-blessed with this world’s
goods, I obtained admission to the Newington
Asylum, where I remained seven months ; yet
still I got no better, being unable to swallow any
nourishing food. I then took two courses of
electric baths, for which I paid six guineas, and
after them, with the exception of some slight al-
leviation of my suffering, I cannot say that they
did me much good ; in fact, I could not even walk,
so I returned home feeling very despondent, and
imagining that there was no cure in store for me.
One day, on reading the daily paper, I noticed an
account of a marvellous recovery effected by Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, and consider-
ing that this case (the one of Boilermaker Jarvis,
suffering from locomotor ataxia) closely resembled
mine, I determined to make a trial of them, and
sent a boy from the house to buy some for me. I
experienced relief after the first box, and have
steadily improved ever since-; my appetite re-
turned and I ceased to suffer from that terrible
feeling of nervousness. lam now in my seventh
box, and can can confidently assert that I am
most decidedly improved in health. My friends
also notice a marked improvement in my condition,
and this I entirely attribute to the health-giving
properties of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills ; in fact, it
must be solely due to them, for I have tried no
other remedy. The proprietors of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills are at perfect liberty to make whatever
use they may think fit of this statement, for I feel
deeply grateful for the benefit I have derived from
them.
(Signed) “NELLIE DENVER,
“ Countess Cottage,
“ William-street,
“ Double Bay,
“ Sydney, N.S.W.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have cured numerous
cases of paralysis, locomotor ataxy, spinal disease,
rheumatism and sciatica ; also of diseases arising
from impoverishment, and vitiated humors of the
blood, which cause scrofula, rickets, chronic ery-
sipelas, consumption of the bowels and lungs,
anaemia, pale and sallow complexion, general
muscular weakness, lass of appetite, palpitations,
pains in the back, nervous headache, early decay,
all forms of female Wteakness and hysteria. These
Pills are not a purgative. They are genuine only
with the full name, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People, and are sold by chemists and by Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Company, Sydney, who will
forward, post paid, on receipt of stamps or post
order, one box for 2s. 9d., or half dozen for 14s 9d.
They are unrivalled as a tonic for both sexes.*

“This is a case of connubial bankruptcy,

A recent hunting up of old records shows
that the first galloWs in N.S.W. was erected not
on Gallows Hill (Essex-st.), as is generally sup-
posed, but on the spot in King-street where now
stands the Jubilee statue of Her Gracious. j
Dear Bulletin, — Re old and present
Scotch oath. From Supreme to police-court this
impressive oath is administered by the Judge, not
gabbled by a mere slovenly usher or policeman as
in Australia. Hence, in part, its impressiveness.
Lord Craighill, a recently-deceased and conscien-
tious, but very verbose, Supreme Court Judge,
when swearing a juvenile witness used to frighten
the sold out of him thus : “ My boy, do you know
the sanctity of an oath?” “No, sir.” “My
boy, do you know what it is to speak the truth ? ’
“Ye yes, sir.” “Well, my boy, if you speak the
truth all will go well with you ; but if you swerve
from the paths of verity you will find yourself
landed in a concatenation of circumstances from
which you will find it well-nigh, so to speak, im-
possible to extricate yourself.” Collapse of boy !
—Writer.
Religion does not pay a squatter. A fit
seized Old Hundred, and he asked all the travel-
lers to attend prayers at the homestead. The
text unfortunately was, “Do untoothers as ye
would be done by.” The travelling parson did
not give it out as a verse of the Bible, but only as
a valuable proverb. But Scotty the Bummer was
on hand, and bailed up Mr. Century (in presence
of the preacher) for rations, tobacco and the loan
of a Bible. He parted, but the service is now
more select.
