Dear Bulletin, — I write to propose that a
company of three or four swagmen be appointed
to rejoice on the 20th of June and have their ex.’s
defrayed from the track to London by the various
Governments of Australia. The candidates would
number at least 10,000, but I imagine that the
selections would lie among :
1. Myself, Scotty the Wrinkler.
2. Henry Lawson, if he ranks still as a swaggy and has
not swelled head, “ By merit raised to his proud emi-
nence.” (Milton.)
3. 6xB.
4. Boiling Billy. (Where has he got to, by the way ?)
You will acknowledge that the nomads of the
bush are of far more importance than the toy-
soldiers or premiers of the various States (that’s
the new word, isn’t it ?) The conditions of em-
bassy ought to be thoroughly practical, explana-
tory and illustrative ; the passages, necessarily,
being either stowaway, cadged, worked, or
steerage if provided by Bill Cow, which is the
younger John Bull. Their (the vopageurs’) uni-
form ought to, and must be, that of everyday
wear, no togging out in store clothes, laundried
shirts, shiny boots and hard-hitters. I know
places of entertainment where the presence of
such exponents of the grand bush-life would be
welcomed. I even believe it could be made a
means of profit, and if the selected were
true men, the funds might be employed
in taking up a swagman’s home or rest on
one of the fishing-grounds, where there were
also rabbits and ducks. Nicest and respectable
and stake-in-the-country folks will jeer at this
proposal; the soldier-delegates and the Agents-
General will probably militate against outspoken
information re travellers’ huts, shearers’ pig-
geries, coolie-barracklike bunks, darn and creek
water with green scum, autocratic Phoenician
move-on traps, honorary dishonorable J.P.’s; but
the swag movement will catch on, and it will
attract vauriens who can be assured of tucker,
leisure, sport, literature, argument, tobacco, and
periodical eruptions under the fostering influence
of dear old Matilda. So, round ’em up again,
“Tramp.” Good-bye, Mother Publichouse.
Here’s another off the track, and that’s—Yours,
Scotty the Wrinkler.
The Only Certain Moustache Producer.

The Home-coming .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He staggered back against the wall ; the
hand that held the heavy stick sank slowly to his
side. An awful look of horror was in his eyes.
Then his muscles seemed suddenly to collapse,
and he fell loosely into the chair beside him ; a
clammy perspiration came over his white face,
and the stick clattered on the ground.
What was he doing ? he wondered ; where was
he ? He gazed vacantly in front of him—at the
window half open where a ray of early morning
sun was peeping, at the cobwebs hanging from
the sash, and the vine tendrils clinging to the
verandah - post. There his eyes stayed, never
travelling to the huddled figure crouching on the
floor. Then a smile came to his face, making
more ghastly the horror it could not hide. Oh,
yes, he knew where he was—he was home at last;
m his own home, the home that held every pure
thought and every affection he had ever possessed.
Yes, that was the window where his mother had
lifted him, a small, toddling child, to watch the
cattle being rounded up—the window through
which he had crept many a time on his return
from some boyish escapade. How stealthily he
used to creep through, holding his breath the
while, but what tell-tale marks of muddy boots
were left on the chintz of the window seat. Never
mind, mother would beg him off, or if father’s
stick were too near his hand she would steal at
night to his tiny bedroom with plum-cake and
apples and soft words to soothe his hot-headed
fury of rebellion. His resentment was always so
uncontrollable and passionate. He could remem-
ber how, as a mere baby, he would answer back
his father’s blows with all the force of his puny
fists, giving blow for blow, and unable to check his
passion though he knew it must bring greater
punishment. Once he had caught up a knife, and
with his back to a corner of the room defied any-
one to touch him, and even his mother’s pleading
was slow to bring contrition.
Ah, God ! how had he repaid her for her love
and pity ? He had gone from home and left her
to listen for the truant feet which never returned.
He had deserted her all these years—a bad son
and a profligate. He had broken his mother’s
heart; and her dying moan, they said, had been
for her boy, her boy, to see him before she died.
Who had said that ? It was a lie, for he had come
back, and here he was in his happy home.
It had been hard work, though, that home-
coming. He could never have done it if he had
not known that those dear lips would plead for
his forgiveness, and welcome him with the won-
derful, unvarying mother-love which he so little
deserved. He felt an unresting anxiety for a
glimpse of the sad, love-filled face. And for her
sake he would bear patiently with his father’s
reproaches. Heaven knew there had been good
cause for them. He had always been a hard
father, upright and inflexible—too hard, in those
long-ago school-boy days—but it was rough luck
that he should have a blackguard son.
He had no money to take him home. Well, he
must tramp it, and get an odd job or two to help
him along. He hated work, but his mother’s face
was before him in all his dreams, and he must go
to her. And so he had tramped it, with his swag
at his back, and had rolled himself in his blanket
at nights, and tramped again at daybreak. His
clothes were ragged and his boots torn, but what
matter ? She would not think of his clothes.
Nearer and nearer—but his courage began to
fail him. He unrolled his swag and took a good
strong nip from a bottle, and then went on feeling
more cheerful. It was night, but there was a
good moon. He could see the homestead with
the dark cedar-trees and the straight prickly
bunya, and the spreading fig. One more nip, a
stronger one, to make a man of him. He put his
swag down and knocked with his heavy stick
upon the door.
* * ♦ *
It had been far easier than he had hoped.
His father had said no word, and his mother had
put out her arms to him with the same tender,
forgiving look he had so often pictured. She was
not changed, she was just the same. Dead? Who
had said so ? Someone had said she was dead and
had cursed him for having killed her, and for that
lie he had struck the speaker to the ground. She
was here but a moment since, smiling through her
dimmed eyes. Where was she now? She must
have gone back to bed, and why should he sit there
so still ? He mhst follow her. He had his story
to tell and her forgiveness to ask. His limbs had
grown so heavy and strange, and what was this
damp like the dew of death upon his face ? Why,
there .was his father beneath the window; but
what was he doing on the ground ? Fallen asleep?
* * « *
The ray of sunlight slanted downward into
the room as the sun rose. It fell on a grey head
that hung backwards, and then on a great, black,
swollen bruise on the temple, on a few drops of
clotted blood, and then on the riding-whip which
was still fiercely clenched in the stiffening fingers.
The watcher staggered to his feet. His strain-
ing eyes stared forward, then fell on the stick at
his feet with a dark stain on one of its knobs.
rhere were sounds of coming feet, an opening
door, and cries of horror and dismay. He did not
heed them nor the rough grasp upon his arm.
His eyes had travelled back to the grey head on
the floor, where the sunlight streamed ever hotter
and brighter.
MAMOOSE.
Old Johnny.
[FOR THE BULLEITN.]
The old man looked about him with satis-
faction ; the rocky ridge of apple scrub and hill man-
grove, the distant flat of white mahogany, were all
in view.
“ Ain’t it good, Jack ? ” he said to the boy, “ a
hundred splendid acres, and all our own ! We
came from the land, we go to the land —it is a
man’s place. I should have been here years ago.”
“But what’s the good of all this scrub to us,
dad ? ” said Jack, tentatively ; “ there’s no soil to
plough, no grass, no nothing.”
“ Never you worry about that, lad ; we have got
all the soil we want down on the flat, and we can
shift it here with a wire tramline ; or, I’ve got a
better scheme—this is a good site for a township.
We can peg out allotments, and they should fetch
—yes—at the very least, a pound a foot, with
extra for corner blocks, particularly if we left a
reserve for recreation and others for a church and
school of arts. Pay ! It would pay like a gold-
mine.”
It was the day following the confirmation of
their conditional purchase, and old Johnny and
Jack were out on the place to build a house, so that
the house-mother could follow. This construction
of the dwelling was a lasting pleasure to the old
man; fixing on the site, splitting the wall slabs,
and stripping bark for the roof.
“ I read somewhere, Jack, that every man ought
to build his own house, as a snail builds its shell.
Build it to suit and fit him ; to be part of himself
and his character. A man should be known by his
home. Fancy living in a Sydney terrace, or a
room in a coffee-palace, known by your number —
as well be a rabbit in a warren. While here we
carve destiny with our own hands, and stand for
what we are. Yes, the bush is the place, my lad ;
never you go back to be a nobody in a city crowd.”
“It’s jolly hard work, dad; my hands are all
over blisters,” said Jack, picking up the axe
daintily.
“ Hard work, lad ? Yes ; but healthy ! Finest
thing in the world—make you grow six-foot-six
before you know where you are ; and think of the
appetite you get ! Why, there’s many a man
would give a thousand pounds for your appetite
alone ! ”
J ack had never regarded his digestive longings
from their economic aspect, and remained silent,
wondering.
* * * *
“Isn’t it just beautiful, Maria ?” the old
man said, as he led his wife proudly into the
newly - finished humpy. “Two rooms—a king
on board ship does not have a state cabin half the
size—chimney, and loft; and all ours. No rent
to pay, no taxes, no firing, gas, baker, or milk-
man. Fish in the creek, honey in the trees, and
should we want a bit of meat, hares and wallabies
come hopping to us.”
“ All very fine,” said the wife, “ but you don’t
mean to say we are going to live in this ! ” and she
looked at the open slabs, burnt clay floor, and
rude bark roof with a sniff of disdain. “ And
what’s an old man like you going to do with all
this ? ” —and she gave a sweep of her finger that
embraced all the land prospect. “Fish in the
creek, indeed ! Fish in the sea ! You ought to
have more sense at your age than to drag us out
into this wilderness —ten miles from anywhere —
to camp in a stable. And I’m sure there’s snakes,
it looks just like the place for them ; but I warn
you, the first one I see off I go. And so this is
our wonderful country home ? ”
“Now, Maria, you are quite wrong about the
snakes. I won’t say but down on the flat you
might see an odd one”—the old man had killed
three that morning—“ but up here on the ridge
we never saw such a thing all the time we were
building the house.” Johnny here winked very
hard at his son, who was on the point of speaking,
for he recollected how a maternal diamond and
her brood that had been sacrificed on the founda-
tion of the chimney.
“And you’re not going to smoke your nasty
tobacco in here to make us all sick.”
“ All right, my dear. I’ll smoke outside.” And
from that day forward, as Johnny and his pipe
were inseparable, wet or dry, hot or cold, his place
was a tree-stump in the foreground.
* * * *
Next day the man and boy began the task
of clearing the flat, whereon grew giant maho-
ganies, apple trees, and white gums which needed
tank-like excavations to reach their roots, and
fell finally with a crash that resounded and
echoed for miles.
At every fall old Johnny beamed with delight,
walked round the tree, counting that space as
already cleared, and reckoning in anticipation the
corn that was to come off it.
“ Why, you’ll never get it done,” said his wife
critically, regarding the progress made, “ and how
are we going to live ? ”
“ There’s an acre down in a month, Maria, and
think of the value of the timber ! In Sydney we
should have to pay a pound a load for it.”
“We are not in Sydney, worse luck ; and you’ve
got to burn all that off just like other people.”
“ Remember the pipeclay Jack found this morn-
ing under the hut—beautiful white clay in a big
seam, at a penny a block—supposing we only sell
three hundred a day, that is twenty-five shillings,
and with machinery—which I shall order at once
—we shall turn out ten times as much. Think of
twelve-pounds-ten a day ! ”
“ Fiddlesticks ! Do you think people are going
to eat your clay, build houses with it, or wear it as
clothing? You will be lucky if you sell enough
to buy your nasty tobacco.”
“ I never saw such a woman as you, Maria.
Here we have a house and an extensive estate of our
own, all our simple wants supplied ”
“And why don’t you say, ‘And work like
niggers from week’s end to week’s end, and never
a penny better off, or likely to be.’ ”
“Think of the free and independent life, my
dear! ”
“ Free and independent grandmother ! ”
* * * «
The flat, with its rugged surface of pits and
mounds like a vast pock-marked face ; the old
man’s head just discernible as he delves at the
top of a red-gum. Jack wearily cross-cutting a
slain monster of barn-like proportions.
“ So you have taken up this selection, have
you ? ” The speaker was a man on horseback, who
had ridden up unobserved.
“ Yes, a hundred acres of it; and I was just in
time,” answered Johnny, as he climbed out of his
hole.
“ And what are you going to do with it ? ”
“ Why, live on it. It will feed us, I suppose. ’’
“ Feed a bandicoot! Why, man alive, this fiat

Interview with ex-Champion James Corbett. . . . What he says of his coming fight with Fitzsimmons.
From the St. Louis, Mo., Chronicle.
James J. Corbett’s history as a ring hero will reach the end
bf the chapter when his battle with Bob Fitzsimmons is won and
lost, for, as the world knows, Corbett is matched to fight Fitz-
simmons. Corbett, attired in a frock-coat of the period, plug hat,
and shod in patent leathers, sat in his apartments in the Southern
Hotel at St. Louis the other day, and delivered of a few opinions
regarding his coming fight.
“I am fully aware that Fitz will give me the most scientific
battle of my career,” said the champion to a Chronicle man.

“He is a hard
hitter and cool-
headed. I saw him
fight Dempsey and
posted myself on
his style. He is
my equal almost
in height and

after carefully

with mine, I can’t
see where he has
any ad vantage over

younger, stronger
and shiftier, hit
oftener and mix
my blows more.
Fitzsimmons is
foxy—he was cute
enough to feign
grogginess in seve-
ral of his battles,
thus throwing his opponents off their guard. He can’t fool me by
working the groggy dodge. I will take no chances with him, I
think I can whip him within fifteen rounds. After my fight with
Fitzsimmons I will devote my entire attention to my theatrical
enterprises and retire permanently from the prize ring. ”
Corbett is now the picture of health, weighing 214 pounds.
“ I am bigger, better and stronger than ever before,” replied the
champion when asked about his health. The rheumatic com-
plaint that held me captive off and on some time ago has left me
completely, and I know I am cured of it. I contracted rheu-
matism before training for my fight with Peter Jackson,” con-
tinued Corbett. “ How I got it or where I got it is a mystery to
me, but that I had it is a fact that I am not likely to forget very
soon. I suffered a good deal with rheumatism after that fight.
Some days my arms, wrists and fingers would be so stiff and
swollen that 1 could not use them at all. My legs also pained
me, but to a less degree. Then, again, all this would leave with-
out any apparent reason, and I would not be troubled again for
weeks." Of course I doctored for my complaint continuously, but
the attacks seemed to come every few weeks just the same. A
short time after my fight with Jackson I went east and met and
defeated Dominick M‘Caffrey, though I was handicapped by
rheumatism in my right leg in my encounter with M‘Caffrey.
These periodical attacks of rheumatism affected me un-
til a few weeks before I began training for my fight
with Mitchell. One day I sat in the Coleman House, New York.
My eye chanced to cross an advertisement of Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People. I had never heard of them before, and as
an experiment purchased a box. I consumed two boxes and was
pleased with the results, for the pains began to leave my arms and
legs. After taking four boxes according to directions, I found
myself greatly improved. The improvement is permanent, lam
sure, for I haven’t been troubled with rheumatism since. Before
using the pills the rheumatic attacks returned every month or so,

or excesses, will find them an undoubted specific.
you are on is swept by a flood ten-foot deep at
least once a year, and it’s the only land you have
got that is any good. That’s how it is in these
parts —where there is soil there is flood. No
flood, no soil. No one but a newchum ever tries
cultivation. I give you three years at the outside
to be sick of it; and, mind you, don’t turn any
cattle into my run, or I’ll have to impound them
—so I give you fair warning. Get up ! ” and,
turning his horse about, the stranger cantered off.
“ That’s Einlayson, dad ; the man who owns all
the land next ours.”
“ How these people do talk. ‘ Land no good t
Why has he got so much, then ? And floods ! Of
course, there are floods —that is what makes the
land so rich, and manures it without expense for
the new crop. That is the beauty of this place. J
saw the point at once when I was looking at it.
See how envious and greedy men are, Jack. That
Einlayson wants our piece of ground plain enough.
‘ Eeed a bandicoot,’ indeed ! It has been proved
that one acre of land properly cultivated will sup-
port three families. When we get the crop har-
vested I will take on fresh hands, build more
rooms to the house, and get a cow, and a few pigs
for winter bacon.”
But despite the old man’s energy it was three
long years before the first crop of corn cobbed on
the flat. It was a fine crop—green stems standing
in regimental rows, vivid green, gracious, mur-
muring and rustling to every breeze. An army
might have lain securely hidden in the mass of
foliage. It was the joy of Johnny’s heart to point
his crop out to his wife. “ Won’t feed a bandi-
coot ! ” Look at that.
Before the harvest the rains came—days, weeks
of rain ; and one night they were wakened in their
sleep by a strange noise sounding above the beat-
ing of the storm —a groaning, rushing sound with
a mighty roar. The old man got up, and even in
the blackness of the night he could see from the
door, down below him, the wide valley a shining,
foaming, glistening plain.
“ What’s the matter, Johnny?” his wife called
from the bed.
“ Only a bit of a fresh in the creek, my dear ” ;
and the old man turned in again contentedly.
In the morning, a surprise awaited them. The
flat had disappeared, and nothing but a semblance
of green grass on the surface of the yellow current
showed where the corn had been.
Johnny stood and looked at it, and for a moment
the habitual smile left his face.
“ Now, what do you say, you old fool ? Where
is your fine crop of corn?” exclaimed the wife,
speaking as though the flood had been the personal
act of her husband.
“ Well, Maria, I won’t say it isn’t vexing ; but,
when you come to think of it, the flood will do the
ground a world of good—bring down fresh soil,
make it more fertile. That is the best of our flat:
it is always being made richer. Think of Egypt— ”
“ Think of Jericho ! How are we going to live,
now our money is nearly gone? One thing’s
settled, I’m off to town. No more bush for me ;
I’ll take a situation. One idiot in a family is
enough. ”
The resolve of the wife was not weakened when,
a week later, the water having gone down, its
effects were made plain. The beautiful flat, with
its dwarf forest of shining green cornstalks, was
now a shiny morass of sand-heaps, stranded logs,
and slush. Not only was the crop ruined, but
months of labor were required to clear the ground.
So the wife departed, the old man seeing her to
the station. If he was sad at the parting, his
spirits revived before he reached home.
“ I tell you what it is, Jack. The bush, at the
start, is not the right place for a woman. While
it fits a nian like a good suit of clothes, it’s hardly
in her line, and your mother was always a little
bit particular. Now we can smoke in the house,
lad, puff away all over the shop.” And Johnny
laughed his jolly old laugh, as though it was the
best joke in the world.
* * » *
Two years more the father and son strove
and toiled. The fourth year was rainless for
months, the corn a mere shadow of the year be-
fore, and when marketed the cheque was so small
that none but Johnny could have smiled at it.
The fifth year promised better, the weather was
favorable, the corn magnificent. The old man
walked from stalk to stalk with unfeigned delight,
measuring each cob with a foot-rule.
It was a sultry afternoon. They were chipping
in the rows when the day darkened strangely ;
big inky clouds rose in the east and in the west,
and started racing up the sky, moving with the
swiftness of scenes at a theatre, arid spreading
over each half of the blue in frothing masses.
There was a flash, a clap of thunder that made the
ground tremble, as a big tree, struck from top to
bottom, fell into the creek. Throwing down their
hoes, they raced to the house. It was not far
distant, but ere they staggered to the door they
were bruised and beaten by the hailstones that
rained down on them large as hen-eggs, jagged in
shape and sharp as flints. And the storm drove
with crushing violence, smashed their windows,
perforating sheet-iron and killing their few fowls
as they ran. As they crouched in the house, the
roof went off bodily and was carried far away.
They could not hear their own voices, though they
shouted, for the bellowing of the storm. In less
than half-an-hour it was over, the sky cleared, the
sun shone again, and Johnny and his son, treading
the ice-covered ground, went down to the flat.
Not a single stalk was standing. Elattened to
the ground in mud and hail, the field looked as
though a party of giants had been dancing there.
As the old man looked on the ruin, Jack saw in
his face the first look of despair he had ever seen
there. There were tears on the wrinkled cheek.
“It's no use, dad,” said the boy, “ we must give
it best.”
With a kind of half-choke in the throat, Johnny
straightened himself.
“ It’s rough, lad, it’s rough, I don’t say it isn’t.
But man is made for trouble, and the sooner it
comes the sooner it’s over. Care killed the cat.
Let’s laugh to-day and cry to-morrow. Pigs would
do well on those cobs, and, now I come to think of
it, I’ve heard say corn pays best turned into pork.
One thing, they’d walk to market and save cartage,
besides all the shelling and husking. Yes, pigs
are the game—real Berkshires, none of your bush
hogs, all heads and tails.”
Unfortunately there was no money left to buy
the proposed live stock, so that project hung tire ;
and, crowning misery of all, the storekeeper
declined to give them further credit.
“ I tell you what, lad, perhaps it’s just as well.
We’ll go and see Einlayson.
* * * •
“ So you want to sell out, do you ? I knew
you would ; they all do. Didn’t I tell you years
ago it would not feed a bandicoot? This is my
offer, my last word, take it or leave it. I’ll give
you ten pounds for your interest.
“Ten pounds ! Why we have paid -625 on it,
besides all the improvements.”
“Improvements, you call them. Destroying
all the best of the timber. Which is it to be—yes,
or no ? ”
* •* *
They walked by the road-side, swags at
their bricks.
“ You shouldn’t have taken the money, Dad.
“That’s all right, Jack. Ten pounds is ten
pounds. Now we are free to go where we
as we like. All the world is ours to explore”
and Jolnmie stretched his hand in advance enthu-
siastically. “No more worry about crops or
weather, no more working for nix, and ten golden
sovereigns to spend, lad ! Why, we are princes,
real princes.
HENRY FLETCILLER.
Charles Booth has published, this year,
Yol. VIII. of his extremely painstaking statistical
record of London employments. The most curious
thing is the success with which women-nurses,
abetted by the doctors, absolutely block the hos-
pital training of man-nurses.
A Maryborough (Q.) paper records a
“ highly romantic wedding.” A blind lady-resident
of some 40 summers recently married a gentleman
from the South on three weeks’ acquaintance.
Verily, Love is blind ; as a compensation, how-
ever, Hymen soon learns to see double.
The state of Alabama (U.S.A.) lias enacted
that in future no woman shall wear shirt-waists,
divided - skirts, bloomers, tight - fitting jerseys,
evening-dress showing more than three inches of
undressed chest, or “ stockings that fasten visibly
above the knee.” Visibly above the knee ! How
visibly ?
McG. : “I ran across a noble example of
Thrift in Collins-st., the other day, when an old
fellow tapped me on the arm and, producing a
huge gold chronometer, asked me to read the time
for him. The aged one had evidently enough of
the world’s goods to buy a 40-guinea timepiece,
but his education had not reached the high stage
of being able to tell the time. Murray Smith and
co. ought to get hold of this old gent at next elec-
tion-time and exhibit him, like a Salvation Army
‘ trophy,’ as a clinching argument for the dual
vote. ”
Ed. Bulletin, — Be telepathy. When a
telegraph-operator, I had to exchange business
with a repeating station hundreds of miles away,
and, as was customary, the receiving operator
would take the business in batches of six, then
either giving the “0.K.” receipt or else getting
corrected repetitions of any words mutilated
in transmission. Did such “ smashing ” of
the signals occur whilst I was receiving business
my mind apparently became intently fixed
on the missing portion of the message, and
at the completion of the batch, two out
of the staff of five operators at the trans-
mitting station invariably made the correc-
tions unasked by me, and when questioned
they could not explain why they did so, except
that they were impelled to repeat the missing
word, although the whole message had, so far as
they were aware, gone through correctly. When
working with the other three operators I had to
seek corrections in the usual way. As I never met
any of that staff I had no opportunity of gauging
their temperaments, but until I left the service
my own was so excessively nervous as to make
life a burden. The subject has for many years
been almost forgotten by me, but whilst travelling
in Victoria lately I heard an operator relating a
similar experience. —Ex-Operator.

The Lay of the Temperance Crank.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was the ancient temp’rance crank —he spake
with many a blink
(Was something weird and wond’rous in his fre-
quent wat’ry wink).
“Young man,” he cried in quav’ring tones, “avoid
the cussed drink ! ”
“Sir Crank, ’ I said, “ye sober saints, ye know
not what ye say ;
This earth, O crank, is often dry, and dull and
drear and gray,
And what were man but desert dust, did he not
wet his clay?
“ The world is mostly sham, O crank. Its hair is
mainly dyed;
Its jokes are mostly ancient gibes by ancient
heathen shied
What time the Plesiosaurus plesiosaurted in his
pride.
“ The girl we wed is not the girl we sallied forth
to woo;
Our friends depart and leave us naught beyond an
IOU;
They kick our dog—then shake our hands and
swear their hearts are true.
“ Sweet faith —alack !is leaving us. Her com-
forts go as well,
Our Heav’n’s a mystic Aryan myth (so modern
sages tell) —
The Devil’s dead—his crew are sped—and ice is
cheap in Hell.
“ The fallacies that fooled our sires we’ve bared
them to the bone ;
The lies that ruled the world of old we’ve dragged
them from their throne,
And crowned instead some dodd’ring, weak-kneed
fictions of our own.
Wherefore, O crank, I say to thee the world is
waxing queer,
She needs Illusion’s gentle oil to lubricate her
gear,
That gentle oil, O waterlogged, she findeth yet in
beer.
“A land there is, 0 gentle crank, of spreading
field and fen,
Where rots a slavish Mongol race in squalid
Mongol den—
(O milk and wat’ry virtues ! have you milk and
wat’ry men?)
“ Depart, I say, and view the sons of sandy Pee-
chee-lee ;
Go, pitch thy tent amid that band beatified by
tea
Where never mortal heard of Bass or dreamt of
J.R.D.
“ Depart, I say, and gaze thy fill, then cry from
bended knee,
‘ I thank the gods my countrymen are other men
than ye ’;
Then hie thee home and drink thee drunk, and
bless the barley bree ! ”
N.Q.
P. LUFTIG.
